II

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis introduces a comprehensive support framework for Danish MedTech compani­
es as they transition toward the circular economy. While existing literature offers various 
tools and methods on this topic, there are notable gaps in addressing the specific needs 
of the MedTech industry, such as 1) lack of consideration of multiple factors and their in­
terdependencies, 2) focusing on either generic guidelines for multiple industries or for one 
specific company and 3) providing guidance and actionable steps for the circular initiatives. 
To bridge these gaps, this thesis proposes a support framework tailored to the Danish Med­
Tech industry. It includes a roadmap with actionable activities to guide companies in achie­
ving circularity, especially in the area of circular product innovation, of which the industry 
is facing the greatest challenges. The framework aims to assist companies in redesigning 
single-use devices into reusable alternatives, thus reducing waste production. Moreover, it 
takes into consideration the necessary supporting systems and structures required for these 
circular initiatives to be both feasible and viable. This contribution to academic literature will 
provide valuable insights to support the circular economy transition within MedTech and aid 
Danish MedTech companies in their circular transition.
Keywords: Circular economy, Transition, Innovation, Circular product design, Circular 
product innovation, Roadmap, Danish MedTech, SUDs, Medical devices

V
Learning Objectives
Learning Objectives
1.	 Plan, organize, and carry out a thesis project involving diverse stakeholders from the 
academic institution and the case company while meeting their expectations tow­
ards the deliverable(s).
2.	 Review and analyze relevant literature and data related to the MedTech industry 
and the circular economy concept to extract and synthesize the findings and under­
stand the potential challenges of implementing the Circular Economy in this industry.
3.	 Identify the key stakeholders across the value chain and the surrounding ecosystem 
for the MedTech industry and discuss their relations and impact on transitioning tow­
ard a Circular Economy.
4.	 Devise a recommendation for stakeholder engagement within the MedTech industry 
for the transition toward a circular economy.
5.	 Measure the circularity readiness of the case company and discover the improve­
ment opportunities with the greatest potential for the circular economy.
6.	 Investigate the relevant factors and circular strategies that can assist MedTech com­
panies in transitioning to a circular economy prioritized through benchmarking and 
supported through academic literature.
7.	 Employ the findings and factors from the literature to develop the support framework.
8.	 Develop a support framework to facilitate the successful development of a roadmap 
to assist MedTech companies in transitioning toward the circular economy.
9.	 Evaluate the usability and usefulness of the support framework by testing it with the 
case company.
10.	Conclude, discuss, and reflect on the support framework, its potential drawbacks, 
barriers, and limitations, and its application.

Introduction
"There is only one planet Earth, yet by 2050, the world will be consuming as if there were three"
- (European Commission, 2020)
With a growing global population and pro­
duct demand advancing in conjunction, the 
pressure on natural resources is becoming in­
creasingly prominent. However, despite the in­
creasing demand, the world’s resource supply 
remains finite, which necessitates the transiti­
on from the traditional linear economy, which 
is characterized by its take-make-use-dispose 
model, toward more sustainable and circular 
practices (Bocken et al., 2017) to alleviate the 
current resource consumption. This is especial­
ly pressing within healthcare and the Medical 
Technology (MedTech) industry, which is now 
becoming aware of and recognizing their posi­
tion as among the most polluting sectors in the 
world (Schneider, 2023). 
A substantial contributor to this is the unsus­
tainable sourcing and disposal of the industry’s 
single-use medical devices (SUDs), which are 
discarded after just one use cycle, generating 
a significant amount of waste (Warden, 2022; 
MedTech Europe, 2018). The large amount of 
waste produced by the healthcare industry 
sums up to 38.911 tons, corresponding to 4,4 
tons of waste produced per hour (Jensen, 2023), 
thereby highlighting the critical necessity for 
more sustainable and circular practices within 
the industry overall (Ramos et al., 2023) to re­
duce the amount of waste produced. Embra­
cing a circular model and approach presents a 
promising solution to solve this problem. 
The circular economy emphasizes extending 
the life of the devices by looping their resour­
ces as much as possible (EMF, 2013a), there­
by reducing the dependence on solely virgin 
materials and ultimately minimizing waste 
(Bocken et al., 2016). For the healthcare sector, 
this would mean transitioning away from SUDs 
and instead moving toward more sustainable, 
circular, and reusable alternatives. However, 
this transition toward the circular economy is 
filled with challenges. 
The healthcare industry is one of the most he­
avily regulated industries globally due to the 
principal importance of patient safety (Læge­
middelstyrelsen, 2023; Karlsson and Öhman, 
2005). The stringent regulations, originating 
from the Medical Device Regulation (MDR) 
(European Parliament, 2017), make the adop­
tion of circular economy strategies and pra­
ctices, such as the reuse of medical devices, 
less enticing for companies to consider as 
they pose a significant barrier (Kane, Bakker, 
and Balkenende, 2018). Additionally, the sig­
nificant changes to the current system, value 
chain, and organization that are required for 
the transition, further discourage the MedTech 
industry from adopting the circular practices 
and subsequently embarking on the circular 
economy transition journey (Eisenreich et al., 
2022; Marquet and Vetters, 2023). 
Considering these barriers and the necessity 
for the transition, this thesis aims to examine 
how to facilitate the transition process toward 
the circular economy for the Danish MedTech 
industry, using Coloplast as a case study. The 
study will focus on identifying and addressing 
the factors where the industry is facing the gre­
atest challenges to most effectively reduce the 
waste production, especially concerning SUDs. 
This thesis aims to provide support for the in­
dustry in transitioning toward more circular 
practices by addressing these obstacles. The 
investigation was initiated with the following 
research questions:
a) Why are MedTech companies having difficul­
ties transitioning to a circular economy?
b) What are the existing tools, frameworks, 
guidelines, and methods that can facilitate the 
MedTech industry’s transition toward circulari­
ty?
c) How can a roadmap enable companies in 
their transition toward the circular economy?
IX
Introduction
==PAGE:12==
Methodology
Throughout the process, this thesis emplo­
yed the Design Research Methodology (DRM) 
(Blessing and Chakrabarti, 2009), and this 
report was structured accordingly. This met­
hodology was chosen for its unique combina­
tion of academic research and design, shifting 
the focus from product design to improving 
the design process. The DRM's objective is to 
develop a support system based on models 
and theories that facilitate more effective and 
efficient design practices (Blessing and Cha­
krabarti, 2009), which aligns with the intended 
outcome of this thesis.
The DRM consists of four phases (Figure 0.1). 
Normally, it is an iterative process, allowing 
for revisiting prior phases when a subsequent 
one is completed. However, due to the time 
restraints of this thesis project, the DRM was 
utilized linearly. 
Research Clarification (RC). The first stage of 
the DRM focused on investigating literature to 
gain a preliminary understanding of the per­
ceived problem. For this thesis, this was done 
through literature reviews to investigate why 
MedTech companies struggle to transition tow­
ards a circular economy and identify gaps in the 
literature regarding what was being done to fa­
cilitate this transition. Based on these investi­
gations, a thesis goal and research plan for the 
following phases were developed.
Descriptive Study I (DS-I). The second stage 
dived deeper into the problem by utilizing em­
pirical data to identify the most significant fac­
tors MedTech companies must consider when 
transitioning to the circular economy. Through 
literature research, a deeper understanding of 
these factors was gained. These factors must be 
addressed in the Prescriptive Study phase to im­
Figure 0.1: Design Research Methodology framework (adapted from Blessing and Chakrabarti, 2009)

Methodology

prove the current situation. Moreover, DS-I also 
looked into how to evaluate the support system’s 
performance for Descriptive Study-II through the 
development of support requirements.
Prescriptive Study (PS). The third phase con­
cerned developing the support framework to 
facilitate the circular transition, integrating all 
the relevant findings from DS-I. This phase was 
iterative, with the support iterated upon th­
rough testing to ensure its usability and useful­
ness. With the support finalized, an evaluation 
plan for the support was developed to be utili­
zed in Descriptive Study-II.
Descriptive Study II (DS-II). The final phase in­
cluded evaluating the developed support fra­
mework through testing with the case company 
Coloplast to assess its applicability and usability 
for the Danish MedTech industry. Lastly, based 
on the feedback and evaluation of the support 
framework's success, a discussion on potential 
improvement possibilities and limitations of the 
support framework was offered.
Methods
In addition to the DRM methodology that guided 
the project, multiple other methods, tools, and 
approaches were utilized, and these will be pre­
sented throughout the report when applied. 
However, two main ones will be presented in 
the following.
System Thinking. The idea behind system 
thinking is that everything is interconnected 
and interdependent rather than individual 
disconnected parts. System thinking is essen­
tial when dealing with complex issues like the 
circular economy and the transition. De Weck, 
Roos, and Magee (2011) have developed a mo­
del for engineering systems as an approach to 
work holistically with complex problems. This 
approach focuses on creating, integrating, and 
handling complex systems, their lifecycles, and 
stakeholders. The model has three levels: the ar­
tifact level, the complex systems level, and the 
socio-technical engineering system (Figure 0.2). 
These three levels are interconnected, meaning 
that if something changes in one of the levels, it 
affects the remaining two. Therefore, it is impor­
tant to approach all three levels simultaneously 
when considering the circular transition. As the 
aim of this thesis was also to ensure concurrent 
consideration of the entire system’s elements, 
lifecycles, and stakeholders, the approach was 
a great fit to be used for this thesis, as each de­
cision relating to the circular transition impacts 
the remaining system and its levels.
ready2LOOP. ready2LOOP is a platform de­
veloped by Pigosso and McAloone (2021). The 
platform offers circular economy readiness as­
sessments for several value chain layers across 
eight dimensions (Figure 0.3). The assessment 
requires inputs from representatives across 
various departments within a company, each 
providing their insights by responding to 30 
qualitative questions through a readiness scale 
(Pigosso and McAloone, 2021). The collaborati­
ve effort provides insights into the current sta­
te of the company's practices across different 
fields, which delivers a holistic overview of the 
company's circular efforts. Based on the inputs, 
the ready2LOOP platform generates a total rea­
diness score in percentages and an overview 
of the company's strengths and weaknesses. 
It also allows for benchmarking possibilities to 
see how the company performed compared to 
others across industries and geographical bor­
ders. This tool was utilized in the DS-I phase by 
assessing the case company’s readiness, bench­
marking it against its industry, and identifying 
the most impactful areas to focus on in DS-I and 
implemented in PS for the support.

XII

==PAGE:15==
Case Company
The chosen case company, Coloplast, is a Danish 
MedTech company founded in 1957 that devel­
ops, produces, and distributes medical devices - 
mainly made from plastic, as the name suggests 
- for people with personal and private medical 
conditions (Coloplast, 2023a). 
“This company exists because we want to help 
people with intimate healthcare needs. Under­
standing the people you serve is critical, and 
understanding is much more than just asking 
questions. it’s the ability to get yourself in the 
shoes of the people that you work for, not just 
in a rational way but also in an emotional way.” 
- Kristian Villumsen, President and CEO, Coloplast
As stated by Kristian Villumsen and further sup­
ported by Coloplast’s mission and vision, Colop­
last is a company that prioritizes people. They 
pride themselves on the fact that “Coloplast is 
built on the ability to listen to users' needs - and 
respond with solutions that make their lives easier” 
(Coloplast, 2023b).
Coloplast employs around 14.500 people glo­
bally (Coloplast, 2023a), with headquarters, 
innovation, and pilot site in Denmark, as well 
as nine manufacturing sites across the conti­
nents (Figure 0.4, Appendix A). At the different 
production sites, distinct products or compo­
nents are manufactured for Coloplast across 
“Making life easier” 
- Mission
”Setting the global standard for 
listening and responding” 
- Vision
Figure 0.4: Map showing where Coloplast has production sites
each of its six business areas, which have vast­
ly different value chains with different exter­
nal providers and internal stakeholders within 
Coloplast, emphasizing the complexity of the 
company’s infrastructure.
Business areas. Coloplast’s business areas inclu­
de Ostomy Care, Continence Care, Wound & Skin 
Care, Interventional Urology, and Voice & Respi­
ratory Care (Coloplast, 2009), classified as clas­
ses I or II, determined by the EU’s Medical Device 
Classification (European Commission, 2023a). 
The classification is based on the product’s pur­
pose and risk level during use; the lower the 
class, the lower the risk. Most of the devices in 
Coloplast’s portfolio are moreover categorized 
as single-use-devices (SUDs), and based on this, 
Coloplast functions as the ideal case for this the­
sis due to its products’ short lifespan and linear 
lifecycles, where there is great potential for the 
circular transition to make a difference.
XIII
Case Company
==PAGE:16==
Case - Sensura Mio. Ostomy Care is Coloplast’s 
oldest, most established, and most prominent 
business area. Therefore, for this thesis, ostomy 
care was selected as the focus point due to its 
established presence in the market and the 
pressing need for circular practices (Appendix 
A; Appendix B). Within the area, the most popu­
lar product family in both Denmark and globally 
is Sensura Mio, and this will, therefore, serve as 
the reference case for this thesis. This decision 
was made to facilitate data flow from the case 
company and to get access to the insights of 
the company's employees who specialize in this 
business area. This would provide relevant in­
dustry-specific empirical data and knowledge 
regarding SUDs in the MedTech context to the 
thesis. In Figure 0.5, the three ostomy bag types, 
as well as their three variations, can be seen. In 
Denmark, there is a preference for the One-pie­
ce option (60%) (Appendix B), further empha­
sizing the need for more circular practices due 
to the daily consumption ranging from one to 
three devices a day, depending on the type of 
ostomy (Appendix B).
Ambitions. Coloplast has set ambitious goals 
to promote more sustainable patterns and aims 
to fulfill them by 2025 (Coloplast, 2021). They 
focus on multiple Sustainable Development 
Goals (SDGs), including responsible production 
and consumption patterns (SDG 12) and acti­
ons towards climate change (SDG 13) (Figure 
0.6). What remains unclear, however, is how 
the company intends to achieve these goals, as 
they have not stated concrete actions on how 
to reach them due to confidentiality and se­
crecy within the industry (Stepniewska, 2024). 
It also remains unfeasible that they will be able 
to reach these goals before 2025, with their cur­
rent fulfillment of them based on numbers from 
2022/2023 (Figure 0.6). 
Coloplast has been selected as the case company 
to facilitate their transition towards the circular 
economy. This transition will potentially help the 
company to reduce its environmental impact, 
improve their products’ circularity, and possibly 
lower the emissions associated with scope 3, 
thereby assisting Coloplast in reaching some of 
their long-term goals (Figure 0.6). And moreover, 
show the benefits and potentials of the circular 
transition such a transition can bring to the Med­
Tech industry as a whole.
 
Case Company Interactions
Throughout the thesis, the project has been in 
contact with the case company to obtain re­
levant information related to the thesis and its 
scope. The most significant communication ac­
tivities can be seen in Table 0.1. The project was 
put in contact with the company representatives 
through the case company supervisor, who sele­
cted the relevant departments based on the in­
formation that was required during the different 
phases throughout the project.

==PAGE:19==
1
Research Clarification
The first phase of the Design Research Methodology was Research 
Clarification. The goal of the phase was to gain a preliminary under­
standing of the current situation through literature research into the 
MedTech industry, the circular economy, and why MedTech companies 
are having difficulties transitioning toward circularity. Based on the­
se findings, a systematic literature review was conducted looking into 
how to facilitate this transition for the industry, which would assist 
in establishing the overall goal for the thesis, developing a research 
plan to guide the subsequent phases, and defining the focus for the 
Descriptive Study I phase.
Ultimately, the Research Clarification stage aimed to address the fol­
lowing introductory research questions:
a) Why are MedTech companies having difficulties transitioning to a 
circular economy?
b) What are the existing tools, frameworks, guidelines, and methods 
that can facilitate the MedTech industry’s transition toward circularity?
c) How can a roadmap enable companies in their transition toward the 
circular economy?
Research Clarification
==PAGE:20==
Exploratory Literature Review
Box 1.1
Search string for the exploratory litera­
ture review
(“circular economy” OR “environmen­
tal sustainability” OR sustainability” OR 
“circular transition” OR “circular roadmap” 
OR “circular business models” OR resource 
efficiency” OR “closed-loop” OR “waste re­

An exploratory literature review was conduc­
ted to identify evidence in literature and the 
MedTech industry to address research question 
a). The data, to address this research question, 
was collected using search words configured 
into a search string (Box 1.1), that was utilized 
in the DTU FindIt research database, which led 
to an extensive literature research. Developing 
the search strings was an iterative process, as 
more keywords were identified using the ori­
ginal search string and then building upon it 
using keywords from relevant articles. The lite­
rature originated from books, research articles, 
and reports with a high level of credibility, and 
when possible, recent literature was preferred 
to ensure data validity. Some of the relevant 
literature was also retrieved through snowbal­
ling. Snowballing facilitated the collection of 
related studies that were not encompassed by 
database searches using the search string but 
were found through the source's bibliography. 
The Unsustainable Ways of 
the Healthcare Sector
Ensuring patient safety is the core of the Hip­
pocratic oath and remains one the central prin­
ciples amongst healthcare professionals and in 
the healthcare sector with the saying “... do not 
harm”. However, when looking at the impact 
of the healthcare sector as a whole, it ranks as 
one of the most polluting sectors in the world 
and accounts for 5,2% of all global carbon di­
oxide emissions (Schneider, 2023). In Denmark, 
this number is even higher at 6% (CFBH, 2019). 
“The health care sector is very energy, resource, 
and waste intensive.” 
- Amy Collins, Medical Director of Physician En­
gagement and Education, Practice Greenhealth
As stated by MD Amy Collins (Schneider, 2023), 
among others, the healthcare sector signifi­
cantly impacts the earth's natural resources, 
which translates to a large amount of waste 
products at the other end, which data provi­
ded by Danske Regioner corroborates (Jensen, 
2023). There are no calculations of the collec­
ted waste produced by Denmark's entire he­
althcare sector. However, it is known that the 
accumulated waste produced by the Danish 
hospitals in 2021 added up to 38.911 tons, cor­
2
Research Clarification
==PAGE:21==
responding to 4,4 tons of waste produced per 
hour (Jensen, 2023), providing an overview and 
understanding of the size of the problem.
In recent years, the healthcare sector's focus 
has been primarily directed toward upholding 
the values of patient and employee safety, as 
well as service quality (Karlsson and Öhman, 
2005). The significance of these particular valu­
es has been observed to take precedence over 
considerations for circularity and minimization 
of ecological footprints in the healthcare se­
ctor. However, despite the recognition of the 
issue and the factual data supporting the need 
for environmental action, efforts to mitigate 
the environmental impacts have been deprio­
ritized or postponed compared to other indu­
stry practices (Karlsson and Öhman, 2005). 
The exact reason why the healthcare sector has 
been lagging behind other industries in this re­
gard for many years is not definitively known, 
as literature investigating this particular aspect 
is scarce (Karlsson and Öhman, 2005). How­
ever, one could argue that this is due to the 
prioritization of patient safety. Due to that, 
healthcare, particularly MedTech, is one of the 
most heavily regulated areas globally, with 
the primary purpose of the regulations being 
to improve patient safety by ensuring corre­
ct documentation, monitoring, and beneficial 
new devices to patients (Lægemiddelstyrelsen, 
2023). While patient safety remains paramount 
in MedTech, the industry actively attempts to 
address circularity challenges while attemp­
ting to strike a balance between the two aspe­
cts (Warden, 2022; MedTech Europe, 2018). 
And with new Danish and European environ­
mentally sustainable regulations regarding the 
green transition coming into effect in the fol­
lowing years, greater demands are being put 
on the industry to fulfill them. This has alrea­
dy come to light for the Danish Regions and 
their tender processes, as they have demanded 
that by 2025 (Larsen, 2022), green purchases 
should be the norm, which puts pressure on 
the development of new medical equipment 
and their suppliers if they want to stay compe­
titive. And even more so in Region Midtjylland, 
where they aim to be circular by 2030 (Larsen, 
2022). Moreover, regulations regarding waste 
handling have been implemented, as part of 
the Danish climate plan for the circular eco­
nomy, by the beginning of January 2023, requi­
ring all companies and private consumers to 
sort waste into 10 fractions to enable effecti­
ve recycling and further value recovery (Vinge, 
2023). However, looking into the best-perfor­
ming hospital in Denmark in the South of Jut­
land, 69% of waste is still being sent directly to 
incineration (Danske Regioner, 2021), making 
the need for more circular practices necessary. 
This is corroborated by Rasmus Nielsen, head 
of procurement at Region H, who speaks about 
the need for circular strategies of narrowing or 
even closing the resource loop: 
“Waste handling and recycling is not a sustai­
nable solution [...]. Instead should we work 
towards limiting or entirely avoid resource con­
sumption where we can.” 
(translated from Danske Regioner, 2021)
Looking beyond the highly regulated sector 
aspect, what other reasons exist for the chal­
lenging transition toward environmental sus­
tainability and circular economy? It involves 
complexity (Moultrie, Sutcliffe, and Maier, 2015). 
Medical devices have a high level of complexi­
ty in terms of composition. The primary reason 
for the complexity is the labeling as hazardous 
waste after use (Moultrie, Sutcliffe, and Maier, 
2015) due to its direct contact with the bodily 
fluids of an individual, rendering some or the 
entire product unfit for further use, as stated by 
the Medical Device Regulation (MDR) (Europe­
an Parliament, 2017). This aspect complicates 
further sustainable management of the device 
and leaves the product to proceed to incine­
ration instead of more circular value recovery 
paths. 
The Underlying Problems of Existing 
Medical Devices
The main source of waste in healthcare is due to 
the high amount of disposed medical devices. 
Those medical devices vary based on their type 
and purpose but also their classification regar­
ding patient safety. As a “medical device” is an 
extensive term due to varying factors, a definiti­
on by the European Parliament, Regulation (EU) 
2017/745 classifies them based on their functi­
onality:
3
Research Clarification
==PAGE:22==
“medical device” means any instrument, appara­
tus, appliance, software, implant, reagent, mate­
rial or other article intended by the manufacturer 
to be used, alone or in combination, for human 
beings for one or more of the following specific 
medical purposes: 
1) diagnosis, prevention, monitoring, prediction, 
prognosis, treatment or alleviation of disease, 
2) diagnosis, monitoring, treatment, alleviation 
of, or compensation for, an injury or disability, 
3) investigation, replacement or modification of 
the anatomy or of a physiological or pathological 
process or state 4) providing information by me­
ans of in vitro examination of specimens derived 
from the human body, including organ, blood 
and tissue donations” 
(European Parliament, 2017)
Due to their sterility, safety, and convenience, 
single-use medical devices (SUDs) are the pre­
ferred method and standard in healthcare (Choi 
and Chen, 2022; Ramos et al., 2023). However, 
they are also the cause of the generation of lar­
ge amounts of carbon emissions and waste and 
are becoming a burden on the environment and 
society (Ramos et al., 2023). The shift towards 
SUDs has occurred in recent decades. Before the 
1970s, medical devices were considered reusable 
and composed of rubber, glass, or metal - hig­
her integrity and durable materials that could 
easily be cleaned or wiped down. However, with 
growing demand and higher levels of complexity 
required for the products, manufacturers shifted 
toward single-use products primarily composed 
of virgin plastic (Gautam and Sahney, 2020).
SUDs. SUDs are considered the most reliable 
option regarding patient safety - compared to 
their reusable counterparts (Moszczynki, 2009). 
On the other hand, the primary problem with 
SUDs is their unsustainable linear lifecycle model 
in which products are produced, used once, and 
disposed of. Due to the complexities of what be­
comes medical waste and its composition, many 
waste managers and plastic recyclers hesitate to 
accept plastic waste from healthcare establish­
ments and industries (Gamba, Napierska, and Zo­
tinca, 2021). This leads to a lower percentage of 
plastic being recycled, so although it is thought 
that the main solution to handling plastic waste 
is to recycle it, only 10% of all plastic ever produ­
ced has been recycled (Gamba, Napierska, and 
Zotinca, 2021). 
For healthcare and the MedTech industry, recy­
cling has been considered an unappealing and 
uneconomical course of action compared to 
the relatively low cost of virgin plastics. In ad­
dition, plastics cannot be recycled indefinitely, 
and as the plastic quality degrades, the pla­
stic needs to be downcycled into lower-gra­
de products (Gamba, Napierska, and Zotinca, 
2021). Moreover, by 2060, plastic production is 
expected to triple, making recycling no longer 
a viable option for dealing with plastic waste 
(Gamba, Napierska, and Zotinca, 2021). Hence, 
although it may not be possible to completely 
eliminate the use of plastics, focusing on mi­
nimizing the use of plastics and redesigning 
the devices to reusable alternatives can lead 
to a substantial decrease in plastic consumpti­
on (Gamba, Napierska, and Zotinca, 2021). This 
underscores the need to reevaluate product 
design and consumption practices in order to 
minimize the generation of waste. 
Reprocessed SUDs. One of the ways to reduce 
waste is by reusing SUDs through reprocessing. 
Reprocessing includes a process on a used de­
vice to ensure its safe reuse through cleaning, 
disinfection, sterilization, and restoring the fun­
ctional safety of the device to its original state 
(European Commission, 2021a). Reusing SUDs 
is a well-known practice, though rarely utilized 
in developed countries - including Denmark 
(Appendix B; Gautam and Sahney, 2020). Here, 
the practice is most commonly found when 
dealing with expensive devices (Gautam and 
Sahney, 2020). The rarity is due to the risk of 
infection, the spread of disease, and the pos­
sibility of malfunction (Gautam and Sahney, 
2020). As stated earlier, the most essential 
aspect of healthcare is patient safety, so little 
compromise is considered in this regard. WHO 
also has its skepticism regarding the reproces­
sing of SUDs, as the products - whether or not 
they have been designed with reusability in 
mind - might lose or have their characteristics 
altered when undergoing reprocessing (Hern­
andez, 2020), resulting in chemical corrosion, 
stretching and/or breaking of the material, etc. 
Reusable devices. A third alternative exists, 
which is medical devices designed for reuse. 
The reusable medical devices have a similar 
starting cost to that of the SUDs, but their de­
4
Research Clarification
==PAGE:23==
sign and more durable composition allow for 
reprocessing to ensure patient safety without 
lessening the product’s structural integrity. 
Subsequently, they have a higher life expec­
tancy, thereby reducing waste (Descôteaux, 
Blackmore, and Parsons, 1998).
However, the efforts put into recycling or re­
using these devices are low. One of the rea­
sons is due to toxic by-products (Jain, 2022). 
Moreover, implementing reuse or recycling 
would entail high investment costs from the 
company's side due to the required changes 
to their current business model and product’s 
design targeting single-use-cycle devices (Jain, 
2022). Ensuring that recycled and reused de­
vices meet the same sterility standards as new 
devices presents another challenge for the 
company's processes. Another important issue 
arises from the ethical side regarding the ab­
sence of patients' consent and possible legal 
problems that could appear if the patients suf­
fered due to improper reuse and sterility of a 
medical device (I’Ons, 2021; Ngo, 2020).
Due to the large amount of literature and the 
fact that several countries, such as Sweden, 
Belgium, and Germany (European Commission, 
2021b), already employ and authorize the use 
of reprocessed SUDs to save costs (Gautam and 
Sahney, 2020), there was a need to delve into 
the reasons why opting for this approach may 
not always present as the optimal solution in 
the broader context. When examining available 
medical device alternatives, one must compare 
before making any conclusions on the “better” 
solution. Based on literature investigating the 
three types of medical devices, three parame­
ters for comparison were established: Patient 
Safety, Overall cost, and Environmental Impact, 
as the three were recurring across the identi­
fied literature (Sherman, Raibley and Eckelman, 
2018; Keil et al., 2023; Unger and Landis, 2016; 
Gautam and Sahney, 2020; SCENIHR, 2010). 
These three will be discussed in the following.
The SUDs ranked as the best out of the 
three in patient safety. This is due to the 
level of its sterility, meaning minimal risk 
of cross-contamination and infection 
(Ramos et al., 2023). Comparing the re­
processed SUDs to the virgin SUDs, the 
same level of sterility cannot be upheld, 
as the device needs to undergo repro­
cessing to be reused. However, as it is 
not designed for reuse, its composition 
is not made to undergo reprocessing, 
resulting in damage to the device, de­
grading its quality, and thus endan­
gering patient safety (SCENIHR, 2010). 
Moreover, because it is not designed for 
reuse, the reprocessing process might 
not be effective, making the sterilizati­
on and disinfection process ineffective 
(Malchevsky et al., 1995). Looking at the 
reusable medical device, it is designed 
for reuse and can uphold its integrity 
when undergoing reprocessing (SCE­
NIHR, 2010), but it cannot uphold the 
same level of sterility as virgin SUDs.
$
Patient Safety
Overall Cost
The overall cost includes all costs rela­
ted to the product, such as purchasing 
costs, costs related to its use cycle(s), 
and disposal. The virgin SUD is made to 
be mass-produced at a small cost (SCE­
NIHR, 2010). This means that a repro­
cessed SUD reduces the cost twofold in 
terms of purchasing costs and the cost 
of handling medical waste (Unger and 
Lanid, 2016). Even with the reprocessing 
fees, the total cost across its lifecycle is 
reduced significantly, which also was 
the main reason for the healthcare in­
dustry starting to adopt this alternative 
despite the reduced safety (DesCôteaux, 
Blackmore, and Parsons, 1998). Compa­
ring these two types with the reusable 
5
Research Clarification
==PAGE:24==
Environmental Impact
medical device, a significant economic 
saving can be found (Sherman, Raib­
ley, and Eckelman, 2018). Because of its 
longer lifecycle with multiple uses, the 
overall economic costs are lower (Gam­
ba, Napierska, and Zotinga, 2021) than 
those of its single-use counterparts, 
putting it at an economic advantage in 
the comparison. 
With the SUDs having only one use 
cycle, it renders a high negative en­
vironmental impact due to their direct 
resource consumption from virgin ma­
terials (Sørensen, Larsen, and Andersen, 
2022). Comparing it to the reprocessed 
alternative, an improved environmen­
tal profile can be found as it utilizes the 
circular economy reprocessing strategy 
(SCENIHR, 2010), allowing for reuse. 
Looking at reusable medical devices, an 
even better profile can be found as their 
lifetime emissions are smaller than tho­
se of SUDs due to the reuse aspect and 
prolonged lifetimes (Unger and Landis, 
2016). Through research investigating 
the comparison between the reusable 
medical device and its single-use alter­
natives, the reusable device is still found 
to be the best environmental alternative 
due to its low material use per use cycle 
(Keil et al., 2023).
Based on this general comparison of the three 
groups, the reusable alternative was deemed 
the best option compared to the other two op­
tions, as seen in Table 1.1. Moszczynski (2009) 
corroborates this. 
“How can we design these devices so they can 
be reused and still meet the needs of patients?” 
- Moszczynski (2009)
The author sees the potential of redesigning 
medical devices for reusability to reduce the 
amount of waste but emphasizes the need to 
continuously fulfill the needs of the patients in 
terms of safety, as it remains the industry's core 
value (Moszczynski, 2009). Even though there is 
a need for more sustainable practices, transitio­
ning toward more circular alternatives remains 
a complex process. However, the possibilities 
must be explored due to the potential environ­
mental and economic gains for the companies.
The Circular Economy as a Me­
ans to Tackle Waste
As mentioned previously, most businesses fol­
low the traditional linear economy model - also 
known as the take-make-waste approach (El­
len MacArthur Foundation (EMF), 2013a). A li­
near economy is a system where the resources 
extracted for manufacturing a product end up 
as waste at the end of their lifetime. From raw 
material extraction to disposal, the linear eco­
nomy moves in one direction, creating a pol­
luting system where natural resources decrease 
and pollutants increase. The circular economy 
is the alternative approach seeking to resolve 
these problems, and transitioning to a circular 
economy can create value without needing 
Table 1.1: Comparison between SUDs, reprocessed SUDs, and reusable medical devices on three selected 
parameters: Patient safety, Overall cost of the product, and Environmental impact across its lifecycle, where 
the device types are ranked from the best to worst in terms of their fulfillment of the three parameters 
SUDs
Reprocessed SUDs
Reusable devices
Patient safety
Overall cost
Environmental impact
6
Research Clarification
==PAGE:25==
extra resources (EMF, 2013a). The circular eco­
nomy is an economic model focused on mini­
mizing waste and maximizing resource efficien­
cy (EMF, 2013a), by shifting focus on products 
kept in a circular loop and used again for as 
long as possible, keeping resources at their hig­
hest value, thus avoiding waste generation and 
resource consumption (EMF, 2013a). A circular 
economy aims to eliminate waste, regenerate 
nature, and circulate materials as much as pos­
sible (EMF, 2013a). This approach must replace 
the current linear economy, which consumes 
the world’s finite resources (EMF, 2013b), as the 
linear model leads to resource depletion. Thus, 
implementation of circular practices can allevi­
ate environmental issues by promoting resour­
ce efficiency, reducing waste generation, and 
fostering circular production and consumption 
patterns (Kane, Bakker and Balkenende, 2018).
To advance toward more circular practices, it 
becomes essential to implement strategies like 
the ones described in the Butterfly diagram 
(EMF, 2013b). The Butterfly diagram provides 
a concise visual representation of the circular 
economy system, displaying the circular flows 
of materials and proposing strategies to shift 
towards circularity (Figure 1.1). Overall, the But­
terfly diagram promotes a clear understanding 
of circular economy principles to drive busines­
ses towards sustainable outcomes (EMF, 2013b). 
The diagram encompasses the technological 
and biological cycle. Where the technical cyc­
le includes processes like reuse, remanufactu­
ring, recycling, and the biological cycle includes 
processes like composting and soil restoration 
(EMF, 2013b). Medical devices are part of the 
technological cycle, as they are categorized as 
"products that deliver a service" (Bocken et al., 
2016). However, SUDs would be categorized as 
"products of consumption" (Bocken et al., 2016), 
meaning they would fit into the biological cyc­
le. As this thesis focuses on advancing circulari­
ty within MedTech companies through reusable 
medical devices, the focus will remain on the 
technological cycle
In the context of the circular economy, the te­
chnological cycle differs from the traditional 
linear economy by incorporating both End-
of-Use (EoU) and End-of-Life (EoL) strategies, 
whereas the linear economy only considers 
EoL.  In the circular economy, there is a dif­
ference between EoU and EoL strategies (EMF, 
2013b). EoU refers to a product that no longer 
fully serves its purpose but still holds value, 
allowing for potential value recovery (New­
Tech Recycling, 2022). On the other hand, EoL 
occurs when a product is completely worn out 
or damaged, with its value exhausted entirely 
(NewTech Recycling, 2022). The Butterfly dia­
gram emphasizes the EoU strategies within the 
technical cycle to preserve the utility and value 
of products for as long as possible, with design 
strategies such as reuse, repair, and refurbis­
hment to maintain product and component 
circulation at a high value while recycling and 
disposal are reserved for products at the end of 
their usable life (EMF, 2013b).
Moreover, the diagram underlines that mate­
rial and energy flow can be diverted from the 
traditional linear economy and redirected tow­
ard various circular feedback loops (Milonton, 
2020). Each loop represents a circular strategy 
to maximize economic value while minimizing 
environmental impact (Milonton, 2020). The 
cycles are organized according to a circular 
hierarchy, with smaller loops being more profi­
table and having a lower environmental impact 
and with the most circular approach being the 
one that preserves materials at their highest 
possible value (Thorin, 2020). 
Circular economy frameworks. Businesses 
must adopt circular strategies to transition 
towards circular ways. Existing frameworks are 
designed to guide the adoption of circular stra­
Box 1.2
Goals of Circular Economy (EMF, 2013a)
•	
Eliminate waste generation throug­
hout the products’ lifecycle
•	
Re/circulate materials for as long as 
possible through various strategies
•	
Minimize environmental impact on 
the ecosystem and human health
•	
Maximize economic value by opti­
mizing resource usage, minimizing 
waste, and adapting innovative bu­
siness models
7
Research Clarification
==PAGE:26==
Figure 1.1: Butterfly diagram showing the technical and biological cycles and their loops (EMF, 2013a)
tegies, prioritizing environmental sustainability, 
efficiency, and resilience. Utilizing frameworks 
such as the “Zero Waste Hierarchy” (ZWIA, 
2022), “Categorization of approaches for redu­
cing resource use” (Bocken et al., 2016), and 
the “Circular Strategies Scanner” (Blomsma 
et al., 2019) are crucial for guiding businesses 
toward the adoption of circular strategies. The­
se frameworks prioritize principles like rede­
sign, reduction, reuse, and recycling, aligning 
with the regenerative and industrial systems 
depicted in the Butterfly diagram. By incorpo­
rating these frameworks, companies can stra­
tegically pinpoint areas for waste minimization 
and resource optimization to facilitate the shift 
towards circularity, ultimately enhancing their 
environmental impact (Blomsma et al., 2019; 
Bocken et al., 2016). Figure 1.2 shows the men­
tioned frameworks, underlining their purpose 
and their focus on the circular transition. 
Transitioning the MedTech 
Industry toward the Circular 
Economy
The healthcare industry, as of now, relies he­
avily on a linear supply chain, which usually en­
courages the usage of SUDs and their following 
disposal (MacNeill et al., 2020). The healthcare 
industry's transition towards a circular eco­
nomy is crucial due to the problem of material 
waste generated by medical devices. Circular 
practices can help alleviate this issue faced by 
the MedTech industry by promoting resour­
ce efficiency, reducing waste generation, and 
fostering circular production and consumpti­
on patterns within healthcare facilities (Kane, 
Bakker, and Balkenende, 2018). Nevertheless, 
a transition towards a more circular approach 
has been slowly implemented in the healthca­
re system. The main reasons for implementing 
circular strategies in the MedTech industry are 
due to regulatory incentives that pressure bu­
8
Research Clarification
==PAGE:27==
Figure 1.2: Circular Economy Frameworks
Zero Waste Hierarchy (ZWIA, 2022)
The hierarchy plays a crucial role in reducing 
waste generation and prioritizes actions such 
as refusing, reducing, and reusing over recy­
cling and disposal. It provides a clear guide­
line for sustainable resource management, 
showcasing the prioritization of various stra­
tegies, with the highest priority at the top of 
the pyramid and the least preferable at the 
bottom. By adopting this hierarchy, compa­
nies have the ability to greatly reduce their 
environmental impact and make progress 
towards reaching their zero waste goals.
Categorization of approaches for reducing 
resource use (Bocken et al., 2016)
The framework focuses on “slowing, closing, 
and narrowing” resource loops to promote 
circularity in business models and product 
design. This method reduces waste and re­
source extraction while promoting innova­
tive business strategies to improve product 
durability, efficiency, and recyclability. It fo­
sters a shift towards service models such as 
product-as-a-service and reverse logistics sy­
stems. By incorporating principles of slowing, 
closing, and narrowing resource loops, bu­
sinesses can not only reduce resource con­
sumption but also promote environmental 
sustainability.
Circular Strategies Scanner (Blomsma et 
al., 2019)
The scanner is a model that provides a ho­
listic view of the impacts and prioritization 
of circular strategies. The scanner explores 
six improvement areas by delving into va­
rious aspects of circularity across different 
stages of a product's lifecycle. The scanner 
was designed for businesses to get a com­
prehensive overview of their current per­
formance and help them identify specific 
areas where their efforts could improve to 
become more circular. As well as, to create 
specific strategies to improve resource ef­
ficiency and waste reduction.
9
Research Clarification
==PAGE:28==
sinesses to move towards a circular economy 
(MacNeill et al., 2020). 
However, there is a lack of research on how to 
transition towards a circular economy within 
MedTech. MedTech companies do not know 
what to consider when planning their circular 
transition. They do not know how to active­
ly apply circular business models and design 
principles within the healthcare industry, 
which creates an obstacle for them (Kane, Bak­
ker, and Balkenende, 2018). 
The absence of data regar­
ding circular design imple­
mentation and its effects on 
the industry, makes it hard for 
MedTech businesses to take 
their first steps toward it. Whi­
le data on its implementation 
is lacking, Ramos et al. (2023) stress that ma­
nufacturers must lead this shift by integrating 
circular principles into the medical devices’ 
design, as they need to take responsibility for 
their products’ environmental impacts by de­
signing devices with circularity in mind. Thus, 
purposeful design, stakeholder demand, and 
innovative business models are the key drivers 
in this shift and could enable a push from the 
private sector to transition toward a circular 
economy (MacNeill et al., 2020; Guzzo et al., 
2020; Ramos et al., 2023; Marquet and Vetters, 
2023).
Collaborative Stakeholder Network
Establishing partnerships across the value chain 
is essential for companies to adopt circular 
practices successfully. Collaboration between 
suppliers, providers, and EoL management faci­
litates the sharing of knowledge and expertise, 
which contributes to a more holistic approach 
to circularity (Marquet and Vetters, 2023). The 
success of these collaborative networks requi­
res the process illustrated in Figure 1.3. Another 
important collaboration that should be establis­
hed is with policymakers (Marquet and Vetters, 
2023). MedTech companies need to comply with 
overarching industry laws and healthcare-spe­
cific regulations. External factors introduce ad­
ded complexity, requiring MedTech companies 
to navigate and adapt to new and unforeseen 
challenges while transitioning toward a circular 
economy (Marquet and Vetters, 2023).  Know­
ing that regulations change regularly, ongoing 
collaboration could ensure that companies stay 
informed and ready for future objectives driven 
by policymakers and mitigate external factors as 
much as possible (Marquet and Vetters, 2023).
User demand is one of the drivers toward a 
circular transition in the MedTech industry. 
Educating primary users within the healthca­
re industry can create awareness of circula­
rity in the MedTech industry 
(Marquet and Vetters, 2023). 
By instilling knowledge about 
the benefits of circularity in 
medical devices, stakeholders 
can make informed decisions 
and actively push demand for 
circularity within the health­
care system. Engaging stakeholders can shape 
the market, which presents an opportunity to 
transform the market and industry (MacNeill et 
al., 2020).
Design Strategies 
Another factor that could potentially help push 
towards circularity involves a shift in the de­
sign of products. MedTech companies aspire

disposable society, let’s step 
back and see how we can do 
things differently.”

==PAGE:29==
to improve and explore the possibilities for 
re-designing their devices and packaging to 
enhance recyclability (Kane, Bakker and Bal­
kenende, 2018). By actively thinking about 
product design, these companies aim to give 
their devices a second life by repurposing or 
reusing products or their materials. However, 
MedTech companies struggle to identify what 
design strategies they should implement for 
their medical devices (Kane, Bakker, and Bal­
kenende, 2018).
Kane, Bakker, and Balkenende's (2018) re­
search examines circular design strategies 
that rely on the individual medical device’s va­
lue and criticality. In this context, value goes 
beyond the traditional understanding of eco­
nomic value as it also considers the technical, 
functional, and regulatory value, as well as the 
aesthetic appeal of the design of the medical 
devices (Kane, Bakker, and Balkenende, 2018). 
Criticality concerns the medical device's infec­
tion risk based on the Spaulding classification 
(Spaulding, 1971), which categorizes it based 
on the device’s contact with the patient during 
use, which assists in determining the level of 
disinfection or sterilization that should be per­
formed before the next use cycle or recycling 
(Figure 1.4). This is crucial from a circular per­
spective, as to recover the value of the medical 
devices, they need to go through the decon­
tamination processes. The non-critical devices 
do not enter a patient's body and, therefore, 
require a low level of disinfection, like cleaning 
(Spaulding, 1971). The semi-critical devices 
come in contact with mucous membranes and 
require high-level chemical disinfection, mea­
ning that all microorganisms, except for bac­
terial spores, are destroyed (Spaulding, 1971). 
The critical devices enter the human tissue and 
the vascular system, resulting in them having 
to be sterilized, which involves either heat or 
chemical sanitation that kills all microorganis­
ms (Spaulding, 1971). 
By mapping medical devices by their value and 
criticality on a matrix shown in Figure 1.5, an 
understanding of what circular strategies can 
be utilized for them emerges. This matrix is es­
sential in guiding MedTech companies toward 
implementing suitable circular strategies for 
their specific devices. By categorizing devices 
and aligning them with appropriate circular 
strategies, companies can optimize resour­
ce utilization, minimize waste, and enhance 
overall sustainability in the MedTech industry 
(Kane, Bakker, and Balkenende, 2018).
Business Models and Infrastructure
When designing a product to be more circular, 
a supporting business model is crucial for the 
system’s success (Marquet and Vetters, 2023). 
Guzzo et al. (2020) investigate these suppor­
ting circular business models within the Med­

==PAGE:30==
Tech industry. Their research identified nine 
industry-specific circular business models that 
combine products and services by identifying 
the number of potential use cycles to deliver 
core value propositions. These can be achieved 
through processes revolving around 1) repair 
and maintenance, 2) reuse and redistributi­
on, 3) refurbishment and remanufacturing, or 
4) recycling (Guzzo et al., 2020). Guzzo et al. 
(2020) expanded upon Kane, Bakker, and Bal­
kenende’s (2018) model and proposed busi­
ness models for the case studies in the field 
of MedTech, detailing the positioning of their 
devices in the matrix. However, Guzzo et al. 
(2020) emphasized that the unique nature 
of circular business models may vary among 
companies due to different factors, thus pre­
senting a challenge for implementation by 
other MedTech companies.  
Thus, implementing these strategies requires 
careful consideration, not only due to the ap­
plicability of the supporting business model but 
also due to significant infrastructural changes 
that would need to be followed to sustain the 
alterations to the business model and across 
the value chain (Marquet and Vetters, 2023). 
An opportunity to address these issues emer­
ges by adapting Product-Service Systems (PSS) 
and reverse logistics (RL) (Guzzo et al., 2020). 
PSS provides a strategic path for companies to 
navigate and overcome infrastructural challen­
ges, creating a more seamless transition tow­
ards circularity (Guzzo et al., 2020). Whereas, 
RL provides a way for companies to take action 
to recover value and take responsibility for the 
device at EoL (Guzzo et al., 2020). Nevertheless, 
not all obstacles can be addressed by imple­
menting PSS and RL, and MedTech companies 
face numerous barriers and obstacles on their 
way to a circular economy (Tura et al., 2019). 
Transition Barriers
To gain a better understanding of the factors 
that might influence MedTech companies in 
their transition toward the circular economy, 
Figure 1.5: Matrix (Kane, Bakker, and Balkenende, 2018) representing circular 
design strategies based on medical devices’ value and criticality
12
Research Clarification
==PAGE:31==
Policies such as the EU's Circular Eco­
nomy Action Plan (CEAP) and Danish 
Regions' directives pressure companies 
to adopt greener practices (European 
Commission, 2015; Danske Regioner, 
2020). Failure to comply may result in 
losing tenders as hospitals prioritize en­
vironmentally friendly products by 2025 
(RFI, 2018). However, the prospects for 
sustainable medical devices, like rema­
nufactured devices, are what hospitals 
usually avoid due to the complexity and 
costs of value recovery processes (Ma­
hjoob, Alfadhli, and Omachonu, 2023). 
Even if they choose options for material 
recovery, there is difficulty in tracking 
the products and knowing where they 
end up at the end of their use (Mahjoob, 
Alfadhli, and Omachonu, 2023). 
Political
a PESTEL analysis (Aguilar, 1967) was conduc­
ted. The PESTEL analysis allows for obtaining 
contextual information about impacts on the 
performance of a business or sector. Compa­
nies use it to understand market conditions 
before shifting towards a new market or de­
veloping new strategies (Aguilar, 1967). For this 
thesis, these barriers were identified through 
an exploratory literature review, and the com­
prehensive diagram with results can be seen 
in Appendix C. The main barriers the MedTech 
sector faces are categorized into the following 
categories: political, economic, social, techno­
logical, environmental, and legal. In addition, 
since the thesis focuses on MedTech in Den­
mark, the regulatory and legislative framework 
focuses on the ones pertaining and affecting 
Danish companies.
Social
People need to want to transition tow­
ard more sustainable ways, and they 
are open to choosing more circular me­
dical products (Chambon et al., 2023). 
Thus, there is an incentive for MedTech 
companies to transition (Kirchherr et al., 
2018; Alfina et al., 2022; Tura et al., 2019). 
Despite the proven safety of devices that 
undergo value recovery processes, users 
often harbor misconceptions about re­
cycled, reused, and refurbished devices. 
They may perceive them as inferior or 
believe that they are they are not as safe 
as they are the device’s secondary user 
(MacNeill et al., 2020). However, trans­
parent communication from manufac­
turers about the devices, including their 
reprocessing, composition, and environ­
mental advantages, typically leads users 
to prefer these over conventional pro­
ducts if given the choice (Chambon et 
al., 2003).
Economic
Companies willing to make their first 
step toward circular transition often 
face no financial support or funding 
to become more circular (Kirchherr et 
al., 2018; Alfina et al., 2022; Tura et al., 
2019). If MedTech needs to shift to a 
circular economy and make changes th­
roughout their supply chain, high costs 
would follow due to restructuring acti­
ons of facilities, staff, processes, etc. This 
means that companies would face high 
investment costs in the shift at their own 
risk (Kirchherr et al., 2018; Alfina et al., 
2022; Tura et al., 2019). Even if MedTech 
companies could surpass this barrier, 
another barrier would appear: the lack 
of tools to ensure the long-term benefits 
for a circular economy (Tura et al., 2019).
13
Research Clarification
==PAGE:32==
Legal
14
Research Clarification
Existing regulations also pose a signifi­
cant barrier to transitioning to circular 
practices (Kirchherr et al., 2018; Alfina 
et al., 2022; Tura et al., 2019). Particular­
ly in MedTech, the development is he­
avily influenced by legislation, such as 
MDR that creates rules for the device, its 
waste handling, design, etc. (European 
Parliament, 2017). Moreover, the medi­
cal devices cannot contain rare or toxic 
materials with a harmful environmental 
impact, which is regulated through the 
RoHS (Reduction of Hazardous Substan­
ces) directive (European Commission, 
2023b) limiting the amount and types 
of substances in the devices themselves, 
which is further regulated by the REACH 
(Registration, Evaluation, Authorization, 
and restrictions of CHemicals) regula­
tion (European Commission, 2023c) to 
protect the environment and human 
health from chemical substances. Addi­
tionally, there is limited support in regu­
lations surrounding medical waste recy­
cling options due to their classification 
as toxic waste (KPMG, 2023; Ngo, 2020). 
Conclusion: Focus on factors while naviga­
ting through barriers
Transitioning towards circularity in the MedTech 
industry is challenging due to various barriers 
that are hard to navigate (Tura et al., 2019). The 
biggest challenges can be seen in the legal area 
of Danish MedTech due to the many regulations 
companies need to comply with, as well as the 
lack of guidance regarding the handling of ha­
zardous waste. Moreover, MedTech lacks support 
not only financially but also from policymakers 
and users of the devices. These barriers should 
be kept in mind and not overlooked in order to 
identify opportunities to develop strategic re­
sponses. MedTech companies can effectively 
manage their current obstacles while enhancing 
value creation by prioritizing design strategies, 
implementing innovative business models, and 
fostering a collaborative stakeholder network.
Technological & Environmental
​As mentioned previously, the 
waste generated by healthcare 
makes it one of the most pol­
luting industries in the world 
(Schneider, 2023). Most of the 
waste is generated by SUDs, which is 
then in Denmark, mostly incinerated 
and its heat is used as district heating, 
which is one of the cheapest heating 
sources (Thorin, 2020). The plants have 
become so efficient that with the cur­
rent waste collection system, almost 
no recycling processes can compete in 
terms of value per tonne of waste (Tho­
rin, 2020). This shows how the techno­
logical choices in waste management 
affect the environmental factors. More­
over, companies still opt for virgin ma­
terials, as currently, the prices of virgin 
materials are lower than those of more 
sustainable options. Thus, businesses 
do not see the economic benefits of 
choosing more circular and sustainable 
options (Kirchherr et al., 2018).
==PAGE:33==
15
Research Clarification
Systematic Literature Review
Due to these barriers, MedTech companies are 
facing challenges in employing circular initia­
tives, therefore, a systematic literature review 
(Biolchini et al., 2005) was conducted to exa­
mine the literature regarding existing sup­
ports assisting companies' transition toward 
the circular economy. Specifically, the focus 
was on how these supports assist companies 
across various industries, including MedTech, 
in reaching the circular economy. Based on the 
objective of this review, the primary research 
question b) was formulated:
b) What are the existing tools, frameworks, 
guidelines, and methods that can facilitate the 
MedTech industry’s transition toward circularity?
To address this, a systematic literature review 
was conducted with a developed protocol (Ap­
pendix D) outlining the various aspects of the 
literature review, including formulation, source 
and study collection, and information extracti­
on (Figure 1.6). It should be noted that publica­
tions regarding IT and IoT as aids for the circular 
transition were excluded in order to narrow 
the scope of the review. This resulted in a total 
of 18 publications about existing guidance for 
companies to transition to a circular economy. 
The discovered sources were categorized ba­
sed on a number of defined types of enablers 
(Figure 1.6), which were established based on 
identified patterns among the sources. All of 
the found enablers shared a common purpo­
se: to help companies transition to a circular 
economy - however, with varying perspectives 
and approaches.
A framework offers a structured conceptual 
model that serves as a foundation for solving 
a particular problem. When examining the fra­
mework type, it becomes evident that they 
tend to focus on business model innovation to 
aid the transition to circular ways. These fra­
meworks offer comprehensive definitions of 
circular strategies with emphasis on: 
•	
design for resource recovery (Silk et al., 
2019)
•	
circular economy ecosystems (Marantes, 
Soares, and Silva, 2023)
•	
considerations for the transition toward 
circular business models (Halonen, Majuri, 
and Lanz, 2019; Geissdoerfer et al., 2017)
•	
taxonomy of circular strategies for ma­
nufacturing companies (Blomsma et al., 
2019)
•	
a holistic approach for embedding circular 
concepts into corporate decision-making 
(Mendoza et al., 2017)
Frameworks assist companies in the transition 
by providing general guidance of transitional 
considerations, which helps businesses identi­
fy relevant approaches based on their industry 
context. This means that businesses can adapt 
those frameworks to specific contexts, allow­
ing flexibility in implementation. However, 
one of the limitations companies face is the 
complexity of these frameworks. The majority 
of them rely on expert knowledge to commu­
nicate aspects to the companies to effectively 
implement them. Frameworks are usually also 
standardized, meaning that some of the pro­
posed practices might not apply to every in­
dustry.
The second most prevalent category identified 
within the literature was a support tool. The 
support tools focus on transforming business 
models and product design innovation tow­
ards circularity, with an emphasis on:
•	
industry specifications (Pruhs et al., 2024; 
Moultrie et al., 2016)
•	
circular practice focus (Mallick et al., 2024; 
Galati and Adamashvili, 2023)
•	
a more holistic approach and tools on how 
to tackle the transition (Pigosso and McA­
loone, 2021)
As mentioned above, support tools aim to of­
fer assistance in transforming business models 
and product design practices towards circula­
rity, addressing specific challenges or oppor­
tunities within processes. They aid in creating 
a straightforward implementation process to 
make it more manageable for businesses to 
understand and plan their first steps toward 
the circular transition. However, the tools were 
found to have a narrow scope, meaning they 
focus on a specific aspect of circularity, negle­
cting the broader challenges. Similarly to fra­
meworks, support tools usually tend to over­

Research Clarification
look specific industry or region needs, which 
results in a lack of customization for certain 
sectors. 
Guidelines tend to provide structured re­
commendations and steps for implementing 
circular economy principles based on diffe­
rent aspects of the business, such as business 
models, product development, and transition 
strategies. Guidelines offer high-level consi­
derations and adoption guides for incorpora­
ting circular economy principles (Perotti et al., 
2023; Aguiar and Jugend, 2022; McAloone et 
al., 2017) by:
•	
providing process steps for integrating 
circular business models (Perotti et al., 
2023)
•	
proposing a matrix guide for new product 
development (Aguiar and Jugend, 2022)
•	
providing toolkits with overall guidelines 
related to six areas in the circular transition 
(McAloone et al., 2017)
Similarly to other tools, the considerations are 
usually high-level, which helps companies un­
derstand the key concepts, barriers, or oppor­
tunities regarding the transition to the circular 
economy. They also tend to provide resources 
to support businesses implementing circular 
strategies while navigating various challenges. 
However, as seen with the two previous types, 
guidelines tend to overlook unique challenges 
faced by specific industries or regions due to 
high-level considerations. Additionally, busi­
nesses have difficulty understanding and ef­
fectively implementing the guidelines without 
sufficient expertise or resources due to their 
abstract nature.
The last type identified through the literature 
review was a roadmap. A roadmap is a step-
by-step path map that outlines the process of 
achieving certain goals, often including a time­
line. In terms of the roadmap type, it was ob­
served that there is a significant emphasis put 
on circular business model development (Diaz 
and Baumgartner, 2024; Gomes, Moreira, and 
Ometto, 2023; Frishammar and Parida, 2019; 
MacNiell et al., 2020). These roadmaps were 
designed to be used during workshops with a 
focus on the following:
•	
generalized 
industry 
application 
(Go­
mes, Moreira, and Ometto, 2023; Diaz and 
Baumgartner, 2024)
•	
MedTech industry application (MacNiell et 
al., 2020)
•	
the business context of the company 
(Frishammar and Parida, 2019)
Generally, roadmaps facilitate strategic plan­
ning by outlining steps, actions, and initiati­
ves necessary to transition towards a circular 
economy. Roadmaps provide a structured plan 
that considers milestones for implementing 
circular strategies and transforming business 
models. Moreover, they help to identify barri­
ers and opportunities regarding the transition 
to a circular economy, along with factors like 
value chain improvements or stakeholder en­
gagement. Most importantly, a roadmap is a 
tool that is specifically targeted towards the 
businesses themselves, meaning that the out­
put of the roadmap is supposed to be easily 
understood by a company. However, creating 
a roadmap is still too complex for businesses, 
as developing a circular economy roadmap is 
dependent on experts in roadmaps and the 
circular economy. And even if created by an 
expert, the adaptation of the holistic roadmap 
by the company themselves would require 
external assistance to understand what each 
step entails and what exact actions a company 
should take.
Gaps
Examining existing tools for companies to tran­
sition to a circular economy reveals various 
gaps that companies face. 
One issue lies in the absence of structured 
guidance for assisting in companies' imple­
mentation opportunities (Mendoza et al., 2017; 
Moultrie et al., 2016). While enablers exist, they 
often fail to provide comprehensive imple­
mentation plans, and companies often strugg­
le with the lack of information (Mauss, Thiemt, 
and Fottner., 2022). They do not provide com­
panies with actionable steps on how to imple­
ment more circular practices but rather focus 
on the future state or considerations during 
the transition. This is not only because enablers 
do not provide how to integrate the strategies 
==PAGE:36==
into their current ways but it is also because 
the transition path is established by an outside 
expert (Mendoza et al., 2017; Diaz and Baum­
gartner, 2024). 
This ties in with another issue of tools being 
too complex and knowledge-dependent for 
companies to apply themselves (Mendoza et 
al., 2017; Diaz and Baumgartner, 2024). Thus, 
they can only be utilized with the help of an 
expert in the tool and/or circular economy, 
which results in companies' lack of owners­
hip and control over their transition choices. 
This results in confusion about future steps for 
the companies and overwhelms them with the 
amount of information provided. 
Furthermore, existing tools frequently focus 
only on one aspect, being either product de­
sign, business innovation, or collaborative sta­
keholder network. This prevents companies 
from developing a holistic solution that ad­
dresses the entirety of the circular transition 
process. This lack of a holistic overview leaves 
them overwhelmed with how to tackle the en­
tirety of the circular transition (Mendoza et al., 
2017). Nevertheless, there are tools that have 
a more holistic view, but they tend to be too 
high level, making them hard to carry out (Ma­
cNiell et al., 2020),  as all the publications ap­
plied a theoretical approach.
The publications either targeted all industries or 
were very company-specific, but as industries 
have different priorities, regulations, processes, 
etc. these generic tools are not useful for their 
transition to the circular economy (Mendoza 
et al., 2017), meaning that the tools lacked a 
contextual background, making it hard for Da­
nish MedTech to apply. On the other hand, the 
company-specific tools (Frishammar and Pari­
da, 2019) provided in a few publications were 
similarly hard to implement, as they were in­
tended only for the company it was developed 
for, making it hard for other companies to utili­
ze because of differences between businesses. 
Based on these identified gaps, step-by-step 
guidance was deemed necessary to ensure a 
successful path for companies to overcome 
barriers to transition toward the circular eco­
nomy (Mauss, Thiemt, and Fottner, 2022). 
Moreover, the developed support should be 
industry-specific, as different sectors face dif­
ferent challenges (Mendoza et al., 2017). Thus, 
an easy-to-use tool tailored to companies and 
their needs should be created to gain accep­
tance and move forward toward circularity 
(Pruhs et al., 2024). 
Transition Considerations
The literature review identified three key fac­
tors in pursuing the transition toward the 
circular economy: product design innovation, 
supporting circular business models, and en­
suring stakeholder engagement and collabora­
tion. These considerations underline their im­
portance in the circular transition regardless of 
the type of enabler. 
As mentioned, both circular product design 
As mentioned, both circular product design 
and circular business models are important 
for a successful transition (Mendoza et al., 
2017; Pruhs et al., 2024; Bocken et al., 2014). 
The parallel adaptation of both increases the 
likelihood of a successful implementation and 
synergy between the circular economy design 
strategies and business models (Bocken et al., 
2014). However, major changes would need 
to be adopted, such as new ways of thinking, 
innovative combinations of design strategies, 
and business model tools to advance toward a 
circular economy (Mendoza et al., 2017; Pruhs 
et al., 2024). The interplay of circular business 
models, circular design approaches, and pro­
duct lifecycle incentives is the foundation for

circular design principles (Pruhs et al., 2024). 
Moreover, the inclusion of lifecycle thinking 
is crucial during the decision-making process 
toward more environmentally sustainable out­
comes (Mendoza et al., 2017; Tecchio et al., 
2017; Aguiar and Jugend, 2022; Pruhs et al., 
2024), as it ensures a holistic approach and 
considerations of the entire lifespan of a pro­
duct, from raw material extraction to EoL.
Additionally, stakeholder engagement and col­
laboration emerge as essential for value crea­
tion when transitioning towards circular ways 
(Mendoza et al., 2017; Aguiar and Jugend, 2022; 
Mauss, Thiemt, and Fottner, 2022; Galati and 
Adamashvili, 2023; Gomes, Moreira, and Om­
etto, 2023). Incentivizing engagement and col­
laboration with stakeholders across the value 
chain and ecosystem can assist in the creation 
of a collaborative network, allowing for colla­
boration, partnerships, innovation, and value 
creation. Thus, when considering the transfor­
mation from a linear model to a circular one, 
the value chain should be considered part of 
the implementation to succeed (Kalmykova, 
Sadagopan, and Rosado, 2018). 
Reflection
There is a need to bridge the gaps for the 
circular transition that could be facilitated by 
a tool that would consider all the previously 
mentioned considerations and be easily un­
derstandable as well as usable by the compa­
nies independently. This is where a need for 
roadmaps emerges. As seen from the systema­
tic literature review, roadmaps offer a detailed 
overview of how to achieve a certain goal. This 
capability holds significant potential in facili­
tating the transition for MedTech companies. 
However, the currently existing roadmaps are 
either too high-level or company-specific, ma­
king it challenging for MedTech companies to 
adopt them and ensure ownership (Diaz and 
Baumgartner, 2024; Mendoza et al., 2017). Ad­
ditionally, existing roadmaps often neglect the 
interdependence of various factors, which are 
crucial for successfully implementing circular 
initiatives. Instead, they tend to focus solely on 
one or two aspects at a time, overlooking the 
broader interconnectedness necessary for ef­
fective implementation.
Hence, a necessity for a roadmap tailored to 
Danish MedTech companies arose, incorpora­
ting the aforementioned considerations and 
providing a structured way of navigating th­
rough a transition to more circular ways while 
ensuring companies' control over the process. 
Knowing that, the following research question 
was formulated, which was addressed through 
a literature review.
c) How can a roadmap enable companies in 
their transition toward the circular economy?
==PAGE:38==
Roadmaps as an Enabler
A roadmap is a flexible tool used to plan inno­
vation processes for short and long-term plans 
used by organizations to meet their targets 
(Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2003). Roadmap­
ping can support various aims and has many dif­
ferent types, including a technology roadmap. 
The technological roadmapping technique links 
together technology resources, organizational 
goals, and changing surroundings (Phaal, Far­
rukh, and Probert, 2003). The technology aspect 
can be thought of as a tool, method, system, 
or innovative idea that is essential for achieving 
strategic goals or objectives within the road­
map (Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2003). Those 
objectives are put on a time-based structure 
that enables the development of strategic plans 
within a company. A technology roadmap does 
not have a pre-established format, meaning 
that it is up to an organization how they choose 
to visually display it (Phaal, Farrukh, and Pro­
bert, 2003; Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2004). 
Due to the different aims of the roadmap based 
on organizations’ needs and business objec­
tives, the roadmaps need to be customized to 
suit the particular application. However, there 
are eight main types of roadmaps with different 
purposes, as seen in Box 1.4. 
Designing a Roadmap
No matter the type of roadmap, there are a few 
aspects a technological roadmaps should con­
sist of. The first aspect is the roadmap’s layers, 
which are designed to fit the particular orga­
nization. A roadmap typically consists of three 
main layers (Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2004), 
as seen in Figure 1.7. These layers, while not ne­
cessarily visual, play a critical role in guiding the 
roadmap's development:
•	
The “know-why” layer focuses on the driver 
of the roadmap and is usually influenced by 
external factors. 
•	
The “know-how” layer focuses on the tech­
nical expertise, such as competencies, skills, 
and infrastructure, required to address the 
challenges outlined in the roadmap.
•	
The “know-what” layer forms a bridge bet­
ween the roadmap's purpose and the avai­
lable resources, highlighting the strategic 
allocation of resources to achieve the out­
lined goals. 
Box 1.4
Different types of roadmaps and 
their main purpose/goal (adapted 
from Phaal et al., 2003)
Product Planning. The most com­
mon type of technology roadmaps 
used for product development and 
often include technology linked to 
manufacturing processes and pro­
duct generation.
Service/capability Planning. Mostly 
applicable to service-based compa­
nies investigating technology as a 
support for their organization.
Strategic Planning. Suggested a ge­
neral strategic approach for evalua­
ting various opportunities or challen­
ges within an organization.
Long-range 
Planning. 
Entails 
long-time plans that are often imple­
mented at the sector level.
Knowledge Asset Planning. Know­
ledge assets and management initi­
atives are the focus when creating a 
roadmap.
Process Planning. Designed to sup­
port knowledge management with a 
high focus on process areas like de­
veloping new products.
Integration Planning. The focus is 
on integrating various technologies 
and how the combinations affect the 
surrounding products and systems.
Program Planning. It focuses on 
strategies directly related to project 
planning and implementation.
