Research Clarification
==PAGE:39==
21
Research Clarification
Another aspect of a roadmap is its timefra­
me, which should be adapted based on the 
particular situation, also known as the “know-
when” (Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2004). Ti­
meframes can take different forms and shapes, 
varying from precise dates to just an overall 
idea of short and long-term goals.
Annotations are also needed for the roadmap 
structure to present extra information within 
each layer (Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2004). 
That information varies from linkages between 
layers and sublayers to key elements like ar­
rows and lines to more visual aids like color 
coding, labeling, notes, etc. Similarly to other 
aspects of roadmaps, those change based on 
organizations' preferences (Phaal, Farrukh, and 
Probert, 2004).
The various layers of a roadmap should consist 
of activities that show how the desired goal will 
be reached (Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2003). 
The activities in the roadmap follow a chronolo­
gical order, and they are interconnected (Phaal, 
Farrukh, and Probert, 2004). The level of detail in 
these activities varies based on the organizati­
on's preference, from a comprehensive plan to 
a high-level overview.
When developing the activities, it is crucial to 
consider both the opportunities and challenges 
specific to the organization (Phaal, Farrukh, and 
Probert, 2003). This process involves identifying 
drivers and technologies that align with the over­
arching goal. Thus, a comprehensive roadmap 
requires a broader contextual understanding of 
Box 1.5
A roadmap (Phaal, Farrukh, and Pro­
bert, 2003):
•	
Helps organizations plan short and 
long-term objectives to meet com­
pany targets
•	
Is a very flexible tool allowing for cust­
omizations based on the company’s 
needs 
•	
Consists of a set timeline, layers, activi­
ties, and annotations
Figure 1.7: Generalized structure with various layers of a roadmap and its elements
the problem while also considering specific com­
ponents or products targeted for improvement.
Customizing the roadmap and its processes be­
comes crucial during the planning stage for the 
activities. This helps companies underline the 
objectives of their business and process and re­
flect upon the roadmapping process and how 
each activity can help achieve the greater goal. 
The company's active involvement in the road­
mapping process ensures ownership of the ro­
admap, fostering a sense of accountability and 
commitment to its implementation (Phaal, Far­
rukh, and Probert, 2003). 
Technology Circular Roadmap
Roadmapping offers clear benefits for compa­
nies in establishing an overview of a plan to 
tackle a particular objective. Due to its flexi­
bility, it is feasible to create roadmaps with 
a circular transition perspective in mind, as 
==PAGE:40==
also identified in existing literature (Diaz and 
Baumgartner, 2024; Gomes, Moreira, and Om­
etto, 2023; Frishammar and Parida, 2019; Ma­
cNiell et al., 2020). Therefore, roadmaps can 
tackle the objective of MedTech companies to 
become circular by adapting the various fac­
tors to follow circular considerations such as 
circular product design, business model devel­
opment, and stakeholder collaboration.
However, adapting technical roadmaps by com­
panies would heavily rely on experts in the field, 
particularly in the context of conducting works­
hops to create a roadmap (Phaal, Farrukh, and 
Probert, 2003), similar to the previous existing 
circular roadmaps (Diaz and Baumgartner, 2024; 
Mendoza et al., 2017). This presents an obstac­
le for companies to produce such roadmaps as 
they must seek external assistance in order to 
develop a technology roadmap.

Research Clarification
==PAGE:41==
23
Research Clarification
Conclusion
This marked the conclusion of the Research 
Clarification phase of this thesis. In this phase, 
it was found that Danish MedTech companies 
are becoming increasingly pressured by Euro­
pean and national directives to transition tow­
ard more circular and sustainable ways. This 
shift requires organizational changes and key 
factors to push the demand, such as establis­
hing stakeholder networks, innovative design 
strategies, and business models. However, an 
identified barrier when transitioning towards 
circular practices is striking a balance between 
functionality and patient safety. Since patient 
safety is paramount for MedTech companies, 
it presents a challenge for companies to shift 
towards circular design while maintaining me­
dical devices’ functionality and sterility. Imple­
menting circular strategies requires innovative 
strategies and financial investments to satisfy 
regulatory standards and secure a position in 
the market. Moreover, Danish MedTech com­
panies face other various barriers in their tran­
sition, such as regulatory obstacles regarding 
waste handling processes and negative user 
perception on circular products. The biggest 
issue, however, was the lack of clarity on how 
their transition should look and where to begin 
their journey. Thus, a need was found for a tool 
tackling a successful transition to the circular 
economy for MedTech companies. 
Thus, a systematic literature review was con­
ducted to research existing tools, frameworks, 
and guidelines, focusing on assisting compa­
nies transitioning toward circularity. Multiple 
gaps were identified, as currently, no actio­
nable tools exist that can assist the MedTech 
industry in the transition process. This is due to 
the lack of actionable activities and focus on 
one or two aspects of the circular transition, 
overlooking the holistic view and interconne­
ctedness of the necessary aspects for a suc­
cessful circular transition. MedTech companies 
need step-by-step industry-specific guidance 
to ensure a successful transition through the 
barriers. Thus, a need was found for a transi­
tional roadmap to ensure the success of the 
transition process for MedTech companies.
Roadmaps can potentially serve as an enab­
ler for circular transition for MedTech compa­
nies as they are tools tailored for companies 
to create and utilize. Roadmaps can be used 
as an actionable tool to tackle a circular eco­
nomy transition. However, the creation of ro­
admaps relies heavily on experts in the field, 
thus presenting obstacles for companies to in­
dependently implement roadmaps due to their 
complexity.  Guidance for creating roadmaps 
specifically targeted towards MedTech com­
panies is needed, as it would allow companies 
to take charge of developing a roadmap based 
on their knowledge of their business, with the 
main goal of achieving a circular economy.
==PAGE:42==
Research Plan
With the conclusion of the RC and its main fin­
dings in mind, this thesis aimed to investigate 
the necessary factors of the circular economy 
and how they could be implemented in a sup­
port framework - which was what the DRM 
framework is designed for - to facilitate the 
circular transition for the Danish MedTech in­
dustry. This led to the main thesis goal:
Develop a support framework that will faci­
litate the development of a circular economy 
roadmap for the Danish MedTech industry 
with the goal of reducing the amount of wa­
ste produced by SUDs. 
Thereby, the output was a support framework 
that would facilitate the development of a 
roadmap, meaning that the Danish MedTech 
companies would have a tangible action plan 
to follow for the circular transition - something 
that was currently missing for the industry. A 
support was chosen as this thesis follows the 
DRM, where the main goal is to suggest a sup­
port tool that can improve a set situation (Bles­
sing and Chakrabarti, 2009), and the framework 
type was chosen as it considers existing litera­
ture, their gaps and expands the findings into 
something useful, in this case, a support fra­
mework for the circular transition in MedTech. 
The purpose of the thesis goal was to increase 
the likelihood of MedTech companies embar­
king on the transition journey and increase 
their circularity potential, which would con­
sequently reduce the amount of waste being 
produced by the industry - especially by SUDs 
- through redesign of the medical devices to 
allow for more circular practices. The scope of 
this transition and this thesis was solely focu­
sed on the environmental aspects rather than 
the economics of the circular transition. 
Research Questions
The findings from RC fostered new topics and 
questions to be explored in the DS-I phase to 
gain a further understanding of what aspects 
should be taken into account to successfully 
develop a framework for the MedTech industry.
RQ1: What considerations must MedTech 
companies make in transitioning to a circular 
economy?
This included research into lifecycle thinking 
and how the employment of such an approach 
can assist in the circular transition. During RC, 
it was found that the success of circular ini­
tiatives is only possible if considerations are 
made for the device’s entire lifecycle from the 
beginning.
RQ1.1: How can the employment of lifecyc­
le thinking assist in the transition toward the 
circular economy?
It also examined why a collaborative stake­
holder network is necessary for the successful 
transition, as it had been found that this was 
one of the three main pillars of the circular 
transition. Therefore, it was also necessary to 
identify who should be a part of such a net­
work.
RQ1.2: Why is the collaborative stakeholder 
network necessary for the circular economy 
transition for the MedTech industry?
Another pillar for the successful circular tran­
sition was the circular product design or rede­
sign as found through RC. Therefore, this aspe­
ct was investigated in detail in terms of what 
factors and considerations MedTech compa­
nies must make when designing their circular 
medical devices and what support systems are 
necessary to sustain these new initiatives.
RQ1.3: What factors should MedTech compani­
es consider when designing their medical de­
vices and supporting systems in the transition 
toward a circular economy?

The second phase of the Design Research Methodology was Descrip­
tive Study I. This phase aimed to investigate the main factors, consi­
derations, and actions that MedTech companies must implement to 
transition to a circular economy in order to determine which factors 
to include in the support framework. As identified during the Research 
Clarification phase, the circular transition involves a lot of diverse aspe­
cts across a large assortment of categories. Therefore, due to time con­
straints, this phase will focus on the aspects with the most significant 
environmental impacts and potential for improvements, as identified 
through literature, interviews, and a readiness assessment conducted 
with the case company. The phase focused on the following research 
questions:
RQ1: What considerations must MedTech companies make in transi­
tioning to a circular economy?
•	
RQ1.1: How can the employment of lifecycle thinking assist in the 
transition toward the circular economy?
•	
RQ1.2: Why is the collaborative stakeholder network necessary for 
the circular economy transition for the MedTech industry?
•	
RQ1.3: What factors should MedTech companies consider when de­
signing their medical devices and supporting systems in the transi­
tion toward a circular economy?
As found during the Research Clarification stage, the circular transition 
involved the considerations and implementation of “purposeful de­
sign, innovative circular business models and collaborative stakehol­
der network,” and the success of the transition included all three aspe­
cts. Therefore, this phase will focus on these three aspects and their 
importance concerning the success of the circular transition within the 
MedTech industry.

28
Descriptive Study I
Moving away from the traditional take-make-
use-waste mindset is necessary when adop­
ting a circular economy approach. Therefore, 
one must embrace the concept and strategy 
of lifecycle thinking (LCT), which is defined by 
Barthel et al. (2017) as: 
“Life Cycle Thinking is about going beyond the 
traditional focus on production site and ma­
nufacturing processes to include environmen­
tal, social and economic impacts of a product 
over its entire life cycle.”
The concept of LCT, in relation to the circular 
economy, implies that during the design and 
development of new products, all impacts th­
roughout the entire product lifecycle should 
be considered rather than just focusing on 
manufacturing. LCT is considered to be a 
key element in supporting the transition to a 
circular economy (Patti, 2023) and is necessary 
to prevent unintentional impacts from being 
shifted further down the lifecycle. By imple­
menting circular initiatives from the outset, re­
sources can be kept in use for as long as pos­
sible, thus reducing the overall impact across 
the product's lifecycle. LCT is also known to 
be integrated into policies to ensure a holistic 
cradle-to-cradle lifecycle of the products (Pat­
ti, 2023). The Extended Producer Responsibili­
ty (EPR) policy, as outlined by Jensen (2024), 
works into this concept and is based on the 
idea of transferring responsibility from consu­
mers and local governments back to the pro­
ducers. Although the main focus of the policy 
is waste management, it also encourages en­
vironmentally friendly product design and effi­
cient resource use, consistent with the LCT ap­
proach. By internalizing environmental costs, 
producers are incentivized to create more 
circular products that are easier to recycle, re­
pair, and reuse. This ultimately reduces the to­
tal amount of waste created (Jensen, 2024) and 
thereby supports the circular transition. 
When looking into the literature to identify 
how companies employ LCT in practice, the 
number of cases is increasing (Carey and Koch, 
2019) due to the opportunities for new busi­
ness strategies. However, no examples of how 
MedTech employs LCT can be found in the lite­
rature. The only available example was a study 
conducted in the US in 2001, looking into wa­
ste handling in the healthcare sector (Kaiser, 
Easan, and Shaner, 2001). They found a need 
to handle medical waste more sustainably af­
ter use, as the current practice was to incine­
rate it. However, they found that the opinion 
within the value chain was “an ounce of pre­
vention is worth a pound of cure” (Kaiser, Easan, 
and Shaner, 2001), meaning that risks should 
be evaded when the uncertainty is high and 
knowledge is limited (Bodansky, 1991). Which 
relates to alternative practices of handling me­
dical waste due to its hazardous labeling. This 
opinion was despite the fact that they found 
that with proper waste management, medical 
waste could be reduced to 15% (Kaiser, Easan, 
and Shaner, 2001). Previous analyses and the 
study mentioned could indicate that the gap 
concerning LCT in MedTech is due to regulati­
ons saying that medical waste has to be treated 
through incineration because of its labeling as 
hazardous and infectious. However, because of 
the amount of waste created by the MedTech 
industry, there is a need for the MedTech indu­
stry to consider LCT to reduce the amount of 
medical waste produced. 
The Importance of Lifecycle Thinking
==PAGE:47==
29
Descriptive Study I
Value Chain Network
Shifting focus from considerations concerning 
the product’s lifecycle to the value chain is ne­
cessary, as it allows for a comprehensive view of 
the stakeholders needed to be involved to make 
the employment of LCT feasible. Having a sound 
understanding of the value chain helps pinpoint 
the key actors that require attention in terms of 
stakeholder engagement (Barthel et al., 2017). 
The scope of the value chain is determined by 
the product’s lifecycle, with manufacturers, re­
tailers, customers, and suppliers serving as links 
in the value chain and their interactions with 
one another, determining their relationships 
(UNEP, 2007). SUDs follow a linear cycle, which 
means that the value chain, from the perspec­
tive of the MedTech companies, concludes with 
providing the products to the retailers (Figure 
2.1). In the linear economy, the importance of 
value chain actors focusing on extending a pro­
duct's lifetime and recovering its value at the 
end of its use phase is often overlooked, resul­
ting in lost value (Pigosso, 2023a). This is due to 
the fact that the linear economy does not in­
clude “maintenance and repair services” or “va­
lue recovery companies” (UNEP, 2007), who are 
responsible for carrying out recovery processes 
intended to prolong the lifecycle through re­
circulation on a product, part, or material level 
(Eisenreich et al., 2022).
This is why collaboration across the value chain 
is crucial to the success of implementing a 
circular economy that includes the principles of 
LCT. To achieve this, a transformation towards a 
value network is necessary (Evans et al., 2017) 
(Figure 2.2). These networks are organized dif­
Figure 2.1: Value Chain for the Linear and Circular Economy
ferently from the traditional linear value chains, 
with all stakeholders communicating and colla­
borating towards a shared purpose, considering 
each stakeholder's interests and values (Cap­
gemini, 2015). Thereby, an LCT mindset can be 
adopted, and circular practices can be more ea­
sily implemented. However, reaching this point 
is not without challenges. 
To successfully implement circular practices, 
extensive partnering, networking, and com­
munication are necessary, as highlighted by 
Evans et al. (2017). Engaging comprehensively 
with stakeholders to build trust in the propo­
sed circular business models is crucial, as these 
changes require significant infrastructural chan­
ges within the current system. This has emer­
ged as a major challenge for companies, as the 
circular transition and its circular solutions re­
quire major shifts in a company’s current busi­
ness model, which can lead to high levels of risk 
and uncertainty within the company and its va­
Box 2.2
Who must be a part of the value network:
•	
Material extractors and providers
•	
Manufacturers of components and 
sub-assemblies
•	
Product manufacturers
•	
Logistics providers across the product 
lifecycle
•	
Product retailers and providers
•	
Maintenance and repair services
•	
Products, parts, and material-level va­
lue recovery companies
Need for a Collaborative Stakeholder Network
==PAGE:48==
Figure 2.2: The value network necessary for the circular economy transition with the individual MedTech 
company in the middle, responsible for its establishment and engagement 
lue chain (Eisenreich et al., 2022). Emphasizing 
the need for circular supply and value chains. 
Companies can establish these value networks 
by leveraging their existing supply chain mana­
gement practices and implementing LCT, and 
it can serve as a tool to ensure alignment, in­
novation, and knowledge sharing (UNEP, 2007). 
Therefore, identifying and understanding which 
stakeholders the MedTech industry as a whole 
must engage in the circular economy transition 
journey is necessary for its success.
Stakeholder Analysis
The complexities of designing solutions that 
have to last for multiple life cycles and the com­
plex interconnectedness with various stakehol­
ders render it difficult to predict the effects of 
a circular economy on organizations (Eisenreich 
et al., 2022). Therefore, a three-hour workshop 
was conducted with representatives from the 
case company to understand the stakeholder 
30
Descriptive Study I
landscape and the barriers it faces in the tran­
sition toward circularity. The framework behind 
the workshop was based on the first steps of 
the CIRCit Workbook 6 (Blomsma et al., 2020), 
looking into the aspects of “What’s in it for the 
stakeholders?” utilizing the Business Ecosystem 
Map and the Stakeholder Value Map. The com­
plete protocol and results from the workshop 
can be seen in Appendix Con. 1, and the overall 
purpose can be seen here.
Purpose of the workshop: to gain an under­
standing of the MedTech industry’s stakehol­
der landscape, its actors, the main barriers and 
drivers for the circular transition, and the op­
portunities for value capture within a circular 
economy (by taking a starting point in the case 
company) 
Figure 2.3 shows the developed stakeholder 
landscape for the Danish MedTech industry, in­
==PAGE:49==
31
Descriptive Study I
Figure 2.3: Business Ecosystem for devices distributed in Denmark developed through the workshop, combined 
with literature findings, and structured using De Weck, Roos, and Magee’s (2011) system levels. The stakeholder 
colors indicate when in the product’s lifecycle they are involved: Pre, During, Post, or All. The flows between the 
stakeholders are also differentiated using colors: economic, informational, and material/service
cluding its value chain, ecosystem, and their in­
ternal relations with the MedTech company. The 
ecosystem includes stakeholders outside the 
value chain that are part of the collected stake­
holder network and impact the MedTech com­
panies and their practices. This includes actors 
such as policymakers, the financial network, 
and users. All the identified stakeholders in the 
figure are necessary to establish a collaborative 
stakeholder network.
Lack of bargaining power. During the works­
hop, it was stated that the case company faces a 
challenge in creating a value network (Appendix 
Con. 1) due to their large number of suppliers. 
As each supplier contributes only a small part to 
the final medical device, getting them on board 
with the circular transition becomes difficult. 
This is due to the company’s limited bargaining 
power with their suppliers, making it challen­
ging to ensure their buy-in, responsibility, and 
willingness for the transition, which is deemed 
the greatest barrier to the transition (Eisenreich 
et al., 2022). This is also a common phenomenon 
in highly regulated industries (UNEP, 2007), and it 
ties in with the value chain's willingness to adopt 
and co-innovate for circularity (Eisenreich et al., 
2022; Franco, 2017). To tackle this issue, indu­
stries have simplified their value chains (Hazen 
et al., 2021), and shared responsibility and risk 
across them (UNEP, 2007) to be able to promo­
te LCT and circular initiatives. This strategy can 
==PAGE:50==
Box 2.3
A collaborative network of value chain 
and ecosystem stakeholders can aid in 
the transition through:
•	
Sharing knowledge and resources
•	
Preparation of policymakers for new 
circular strategies from the onset
•	
Continuous alignment with legislati­
on and requirements
•	
Fulfillment of consumer requirements
•	
Participation in the co-development 
of effective circular medical devices 
•	
Co-development 
of 
innovative 
circular economy business models
•	
Co-creation of requirements for the 
public tenders
32
Descriptive Study I
be adopted by the MedTech industry to facilita­
te a transition to a circular economy. However, 
companies would need to restructure their va­
lue chain, focus on larger suppliers, or outsource 
production to one supplier (UNEP, 2007) to exert 
enough bargaining power to enforce circular 
strategies during the transition. The need for the 
support of the value chain is essential (Franco, 
2017; Arlbjørn, de Haas, and Munksgaard, 2011; 
Lee and Kim, 2011), as the success of the circular 
economy and its practices depend on the com­
petencies of its suppliers and their abilities to in­
novate as well as their willingness to transition 
(Shahbazi et al., 2016).
Financial network. Through the workshop, it 
was found that the financial network plays a 
pivotal role in driving the transition towards a 
circular economy for MedTech companies (Ap­
pendix Con. 1). The tender offers put forth by the 
governmental part of the network incentivize in­
novation and sustainability among the compani­
es, and as the focus on circularity and sustaina­
bility grows, the companies will have to innovate 
to continue thriving financially. As a participant 
in the workshop stated (Appendix Con. 1): “We 
[Coloplast] will not move towards the circular eco­
nomy on our own. We need to be pushed. Either by 
policymakers or the government, as lack of com­
pliance would result in business lost, as the majori­
ty of the income [in Denmark] originates from the 
sales through the tenders”. However, no company 
wants to be the first to transition, as it involves 
the risk of losing money (Kirchherr et al., 2018). 
Therefore, they require external factors to push 
them towards the transition. The effectiveness 
of the implementation of "green" purchasing re­
quirements in public procurements to encoura­
ge companies towards the circular economy has 
gained momentum both within the EU and inter­
nationally (Kaiser, Eagan, and Shaner, 2001; UNEP, 
2007; European Commission, 2010). In Denmark, 
the Regional Collaborative Procurement (RFI) is 
the largest contributor to the financial income of 
MedTech companies, procuring medical devices 
for hospitals and municipalities (RFI, 2018). Ulti­
mately, the financial network remains the driving 
force behind the circular transition as compani­
es must meet tender requirements to maintain 
their continuous governmental cash flow.
Policymakers. Another crucial factor identified 
during the workshop was the policymakers, 
who could potentially slow down the transiti­
on process for MedTech (Appendix Con. 1) by 
not adapting legislation and requirements to 
ensure the feasibility of the new circular stra­
tegies. To mitigate potential barriers presen­
ted by policymakers, it is important to involve 
them from the early stages of the transition 
process (Jeffery, 2009) and introduce them to 
planned strategies. Failure to do so could crea­
te distance between stakeholders within the 
system and cause them to be reactive instead 
of proactive (RTI, 2022) in complying with re­
gulations such as the MDR.
Uncaptured Values
Based on the stakeholders mapped in the Bu­
siness Ecosystem map in Figure 2.3, the uncap­
tured values across the stakeholder landscape 
were mapped using the Stakeholder Value map 
(Figure 2.4) (Appendix Con. 1). In the follow­
ing, the main uncaptured values are presen­
ted using Yang et al.’s (2017) classifications, to 
get a better understanding of the value recap­
turing opportunities. The uncaptured values 
were identified using Guzzo’s (2023) definition 
of systemic waste: “Systemic waste represents 
potential value that has not yet been captured 
by value chain actors stakeholders, companies, 
customers, citizens.”
==PAGE:51==
33
Descriptive Study I
Figure 2.4: Stakeholder value map developed through the workshop combined with literature findings. The Value 
opportunities are blank due to confidential information. The complete map can be seen in Appendix Con. 1
Lack of collaboration. The lack of effective col­
laboration, communication, and partnerships 
among stakeholders was identified as one of the 
most significant uncaptured values (missed/ab­
sence) in the workshop. Research suggests that 
close collaboration within the supply chain and 
ecosystem, including customers, suppliers, po­
licymakers, non-governmental organizations, 
and both same-sector and cross-sector com­
panies, can help companies transition to more 
circular practices through sharing information 
and resources and building a network of know­
ledgeable partners (Eisenreich et al., 2022). How­
ever, despite the widespread agreement on the 
importance of stakeholder collaboration, there 
is still a lack of guidance on successfully carry­
ing it out (Eisenreich et al., 2022). Moreover, it 
was found that the MedTech industry, as well as 
pharma, is known to be a secretive industry that 
is unwilling to share information outside their or­
ganization and closest providers (Stepniewska, 
2024). Thus making partnerships and collabo­
rations with external providers and companies 
challenging and thereby hindering the transition 
toward a more collaborative stakeholder land­
scape. Thereby instigating the need for cultural 
change within the industry to allow for change 
and collaboration (Stepniewska, 2024).
Collaboration with external stakeholders, inclu­
ding end-users, is crucial in developing circular 
solutions and designing effective medical de­
vices (Hofmann and Jaeger-Erben, 2020). These 
stakeholders should play an active role in the 
co-development and creation process, levera­
ging their expertise in production and device 
usage. It is equally important to engage with 
other key players in the ecosystem - such as 
financial networks, policymakers, and research 
institutions - who also possess valuable inputs 
that must be considered and implemented for 
circular solutions to succeed (Eisenreich et al., 
2022). This interplay between stakeholders is 
crucial, and if not considered, it can have detri­
mental consequences for the circular economy 
implementation (Eisenreich et al., 2022).
==PAGE:52==

Organizational barriers in the transiti­
on to a circular economy:

Descriptive Study I
Underutilized resources. The greatest uncap­
tured value (destroyed/missed) by MedTech 
companies producing SUDs is the underutili­
zed resources. Due to the SUDs’ short lifecycles, 
they contribute to 90% of the total waste ge­
nerated in the industry (I’Ons, 2020). As there 
are no clear national guidelines for cleaning or 
recycling SUDs (KPMG, 2023) to allow for value 
recovery, they are currently incinerated after 
use. This means that harmful toxins are relea­
sed into the air during the incineration process 
(I’Ons, 2020), making it a less-than-perfect stra­
tegy to deal with the volume of waste, as you 
only recover energy but lose the resource (Step­
niewska, 2024). Another issue with underutili­
zation is the lack of life-extension strategies in 
place. Historically, the high cost of sterilizati­
on and reprocessing has prevented compani­
es from transitioning away from SUDs (I’Ons, 
2020). However, to recapture this value, there 
is a need to prolong the lifecycle through de­
sign for circularity and disassembly (I’Ons, 2023; 
McDonough and Braungart, 2002), transforming 
SUDs into reusable devices. However, the scru­
tiny the reusable devices will face is a major 
challenge as they need to fulfill the same MDR 
regulations as brand-new devices (I’Ons, 2020). 
Thereby setting requirements for both the de­
sign and the MedTech companies as well as 
the policymakers to adapt their regulations to 
better suit the more circular initiatives (Stepnie­
wska, 2024). Looking at this uncaptured value, 
it becomes clear that the means to recapture 
value are tied to the LCT approach. Companies 
need to consider all upstream and downstream 
processes to reduce the environmental impacts 
and reap the economic benefits of transitioning 
to a circular economy, which as stated by Ngo 
(2020), can save businesses upwards of 30%.
Organizational Barriers
One crucial factor for a successful transition to 
the circular economy identified in the workshop 
was the organization's commitment, particular­
ly from top management. Managers should en­
courage the adoption of circularity and its bu­
siness models across the value chain, facilitate 
the growth of new competencies, and provide 
economic resources for the transition (Eisen­
reich et al., 2022). The decision to transition 
from linear to circular models demonstrates the 
commitment of top management toward the 
transition process  (Guldmann and Huulgaard, 
2020) and can help empower company emplo­
yees to adopt the approach as well. 
 “It is a balance between moral and ethics vs. fi­
nancial gain.” 
- Workshop participant (Appendix Con.1)
Investors and top-level management acknow­
ledge the environmental impact of their SUDs 
and the waste they generate and recognize 
the need to reduce it (Eisenreich et al., 2022). 
However, the alternative approach must also 
be economically viable to motivate them to im­
plement the necessary changes, stressing the 
importance of the financial networks' support. 
According to the Ellen MacArthur Foundation's 
(2015a) research, adopting circular economy 
principles could bring significant economic be­
nefits to European businesses. They estimated 
a profit of 1.8 trillion euros by 2030, which is 
0.9 trillion euros more than the linear econo­
mic path, emphasizing the considerable finan­
cial gain that can be achieved. These numbers 
highlight the economic benefits of a circular 
economy and can help gain support across the 
internal organization to transition. The Stake­
holder Engagement Standard (AccountAbility, 
2015) has identified the most common organi­
zational risks relating to organizational changes, 
which include wasting time and monetary re­
sources, losing control of problems, and failing 
to comply with internal and external legislation 
and policies. As these issues can lead to pro­
blems within the company, it is crucial to ad­
dress them proactively (AccountAbility, 2015) 
and demonstrate the benefits of the transition 
==PAGE:53==
35
Descriptive Study I
process. By addressing these, a positive trajec­
tory can be developed and help indicate where 
there is a need for additional assistance. Which, 
in turn, can assist companies in controlling their 
progress while ensuring compliance with legi­
slative and political instruments.
Conclusion on Stakeholder Analysis
The analysis revealed that for a successful tran­
sition towards a circular economy, a collabora­
tive network is necessary. This network should 
prioritize close relationships and co-develop­
ment with value chain and ecosystem stakehol­
ders to facilitate effective communication and 
support for implementing circular practices and 
innovative circular medical devices. To ensure 
the transition's success, companies must have 
sufficient market share and bargaining power 
within their value chain to enforce these initi­
atives. Policymakers and regulators must also 
be involved from the onset to ensure their sup­
port. As the financial network will likely be the 
main driver for the circular transition through 
its effective green purchasing strategies, they 
also play a key role in the MedTech industry. 
Therefore, they must be part of the collaborati­
ve network, along with the rest of the MedTech 
stakeholder landscape. These findings contri­
bute to the thesis objective of establishing a 
comprehensive framework that supports the 
development of a roadmap towards a circular 
economy. This framework will assist companies 
in determining which stakeholders to involve 
from the outset and continuously engage with 
and why, as well as targeting the uncaptured 
values to ensure a successful transition process. 
The workshop's findings and considerations are 
crucial for any company seeking to transition to 
circularity and will be incorporated into the PS 
phase of the framework's development.
Engagement of Stakeholders
Effective management and engagement of sta­
keholders are critical for the successful transi­
tion and implementation of circular initiatives 
(Gupta et al., 2019; Buch et al., 2018), where the 
focus is on how to engage them successfully 
rather than the stated importance of it (Jeffery, 
2009). To do this, trust must be established at 
the outset and maintained throughout the sy­
stem's sustainment to ensure that stakeholders 
continue to contribute to the network and its 
relationships (Evans et al., 2017). When stake­
holders are not involved from the beginning, 
it can lead to negative consequences such as 
defensive dialogue, hostility, and loss of trust 
within the value network (Jeffery, 2009). Howe­
ver, it is important to note that stakeholder en­
gagement is a continuous process (Heikkinen, 
Kujala, and Blomberg, 2023), not just an activity 
at the onset of the transition process. To achie­
ve effective engagement, it is vital to identify 
key stakeholders and classify them based on 
the necessary level of engagement, followed 
by devising interaction strategies for each one 
(AccountAbility, 2015) - similar to a traditional 
communication plan. 
The Stakeholder Engagement Standard (Ac­
countAbility, 2015) presents a comprehensive 
model for mapping stakeholders based on their 
needs, capabilities, and expectations. Compa­
nies can effectively manage their relationships 
and ensure success by identifying the appro­
priate levels and methods of engagement re­
quired for each stakeholder in the model. By 
combining the engagement model with the 
study conducted by Salvioni and Almici (2020) 
on stakeholder groups and their required levels 
of involvement in the circular economy tran­
sition, the mapping of the MedTech industry's 
collaborative stakeholder network has been 
developed and can be seen in Figure 2.5. The 
resulting diagram includes the relevant enga­
gement strategies needed for a thriving circular 
economy business model. In the figure, key ac­
tors are placed in the upper right corner and 
require frequent communication and engage­
ment efforts (AccountAbility, 2015), while it can 
be seen that there are no stakeholders in the 
lower left corner, which is due to the inclusive 
culture of the circular economy (Salvioni and 
Almici, 2020) and the value network. 
When looking at the top right part of the map, 
an active and high level of engagement should 
be achieved through the involvement of the 
customers, which can lead to innovation and 
safeguarding against reluctance for environ­
mental improvements. Moreover, the close col­
laboration between the company and its sup­
pliers can lead to the implementation of new 
systems and RL, together with sharing know­

Initiatives and Factors to Consider when Tran­
sitioning Toward the Circular Economy
With the prerequisites for the circular transiti­
on in place concerning the collaborative stake­
holder network and how to engage them in the 
process, the focus shifted toward the design of 
the medical devices and their supporting busi­
ness models, as identified in RC, to be the main 
aspects of the transition besides the engaged 
stakeholder network. However, to ensure that 
these findings extended to the case company 
and, more importantly, to the overall Medtech 
industry and that they were indeed facing pro­
blems relating to these areas, the ready2LOOP 
assessment tool (Pigosso and McAloone, 2021) 
was employed.
Identifying MedTech’s Impro­
vement Potentials
The tool allowed for a comprehensive un­
derstanding of the company's and industry’s 
strengths and weaknesses and assisted in iden­
tifying the dimensions that require improve­
ments concerning the circular economy tran­
sition. The evaluation was sent across the case 
company's departments to ensure a holistic and 
comprehensive assessment. A thorough analy­
sis of the results can be found in Appendix Con. 
2, and the readiness profile for the case com­
pany can be seen in Appendix Con. 3. The over­
all result can be seen in Figure 2.6.
The ready2LOOP tool allows for a detailed exa­
mination of readiness across various dimen­
sions, where each is assigned a unique sco­
re based on the average response rate. These 
scores provide valuable insights into the case 
company's strengths and weaknesses. They also 
reveal where the company directs most of its 
efforts and which aspects are being overlooked, 
as seen in Box 2.6. This comprehensive under­
standing of the company’s readiness is crucial 
for planning and implementing effective strate­
gies for the circular economy transition.

pharmaceuticals. Nevertheless, this was deemed 
the best fit for benchmarking purposes. Figure

case company is slightly more prepared for the 
circular economy than its industry peers.

==PAGE:57==
solutions and piloting initiatives to strengthen 
partnerships throughout the value chain. Howe­
ver, these aspects were not discussed during the 
value chain workshop and interviews, and the 
discrepancy may be attributed to misalignment 
within the company. The value chain workshop 
revealed a need for improvement in understan­
ding the value and supply chain, making it chal­
lenging to ensure the business is robust for the 
circular economy transition. The benchmarking 
results show similar tendencies, corroborating 
the need for a collaborative stakeholder network 
across the industry's companies to further the 
circular transition.
The assessment also found that the case com­
pany received the lowest scores in the categories 
of "Takeback & End-of-Life strategies” and "Pro­
duct & Service Innovation." This was not surpri­
sing given that the company produces SUDs with 
a linear lifecycle, which are not intended for mul­
tiple lifecycles or recycling (Appendix A; Appen­
dix B; Appendix F). The assessment highlighted 
the importance of RL and EoL strategies, product 
and service innovation, life extension strategies, 
and repair and support services, none of which 
are currently a focus within the industry. The 
results from the ready2LOOP assessment and 
benchmarking proved that the findings from the 
evaluation were not only case-specific but an in­
dustry-wide problem. However, it could be seen 
that the case company performed slightly worse 
in these two dimensions compared to the indu­
stry, especially relating to PSS and life extension. 
This tied in with findings from literature regar­
ding the prioritization of high-value devices for 
circular practices (Damha et al., 2019; Fargnoli 
et al., 2018), leaving out low-value devices in re­
lation to circularity. This explains the case com­
pany’s performance in these dimensions, as the 
SUDs are not economically viable for sustaining 
life extension processes, as they are designed 
and produced right now. This means that imple­
menting circular strategies concerning product 
innovation and value recovery processes is chal­
lenging when focusing on SUDs.
The results also revealed the need to adopt LCT to 
make significant improvements in waste reducti­
on and resource sourcing for MedTech companies. 
And emphasized the need to incorporate LCT into 
the device's design at the beginning of its lifecycle 
to achieve effective recycling, value recovery po­
tential, and circularity - aspects that are necessary 
to improve the lowest-performing dimensions in 
the industry. This highlights the urgent need for 
greater emphasis on circular practices throug­
hout the development process and value chain, 
as well as the need to establish an RL system to 
sustain these initiatives. The implementation of 
these circular initiatives relating to the product’s 
design can moreover significantly help reduce its 
environmental impact, as 80% of a product's total 
environmental impact over its lifecycle is determi­
ned during the early product development phase 
(European Commission, 2016).  
So by targeting the device's design with empha­
sis on multiple lifecycles and employment of LCT, 
the underperforming dimensions can be addres­
sed, uncaptured values can be recaptured, and 
the lifecycles can be further prolonged through 
potential services and RL throughout the use 
cycle. Resulting in the overall environmental im­
pact being reduced, the value recovery potential 
increased, and circularity promoted.
Conclusion: Focus on Circular Pro­
duct Innovation
The evaluation and benchmarking found that 
the greatest potential for improvements within 
the MedTech industry regarding the circular 
transition concerns the device itself in terms of 
circular product innovation, encompassing sup­
porting services, systems, and EoL considerati­

ons, and this would, therefore, be the primary 
focus for the remainder of this thesis. However, 
it is crucial to recognize that other dimensions 
are just as essential and should be incorporated 
and addressed concurrently, as they are inter­
dependent and their modifications have conse­
quences on one another. This includes the sta­
keholder network-related dimensions that have 
already been addressed, as well as the suppor­

product innovation - concerning circular busi­

The success of circular production systems relies 
on a combination of the identified dimensions 
in Box 2.8. However, knowledge of how to ac­
tively combine them to facilitate the transition 
to a circular economy has proven difficult, and 
literature on the subject is scarce (Franco, 2017). 
It has, however, been proven that an interdisci­
plinary framework including these factors could 
offer opportunities for the improvement of the 
current production-consumption system. Each 
factor brings its own unique methods and tools 
that can benefit the transition process (Pinhei­
ro et al., 2019), emphasizing the need to imple­
ment all three simultaneously into a combined 
support framework.
First factor - Circular Product 
Innovation
The most effective way to address the circular 
transition regarding circular product innovation 
is by using the New Product Development (NPD) 
approach (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 
2017; Pinheiro et al., 2019; de Pauw, Karana, and 
Kandachar, 2013; Aguiar et al., 2022). This ap­
proach allows for a complete transformation 
Box 2.8

•

•

•

•

of the current product's design considering its 
lifecycle from the beginning of life to the EoL 
while allowing for innovative solutions without 
being forced to fit into predetermined molds 
and existing processes, not made for circularity 
(Pinheiro et al., 2019). 
The Role of New Product Develop­
ment in the Circular Transition
The NPD approach is widely discussed in litera­
ture, but authors use different names for their 
stages, and their order and content also vary. 
This makes it difficult to precisely outline what 
they entail. However, for the support framework 
and roadmap, a structure was needed to outli­
ne what companies need to do in the transition 
process. Therefore, the Stage-Gate model (Coo­
per, 2010) was chosen as a foundation that pro­
vided the most precise overview regarding the 
contents and activities necessary for each pha­
se. Aspects from Ulrich and Eppinger's (2012) 
and Koen et al.'s (2002) models were also im­
plemented to allow for a more detailed process 
with considerations for the environment, sta­
keholders, and the organization. The different 
phases of the combined NPD process can be 
seen in Figure 2.8.
When considering NPD in the context of a 
circular economy, Bocken et al. (2016) and 
Mendoza et al. (2017) emphasize the need for 
concurrent considerations for business model 
development and circular product design, as 
they are interrelated. However, as shown in 
the combined NPD model in Figure 2.8, the bu­
siness model and business case development 
are their own activity, developed after the pro­
duct concept - but before the product is detai­
led and further specified. This ensures the fea­
sibility of both the circular product design and 
its supporting business model. However, even 
though the Stage-gate model is a sequential 
model, it also includes concurrent aspects, as 
the concept is continuously developed with 
scoping and the development of the business 
model (Cooper, 2010). However, based on the 
time restraints for this thesis, it is not possible 
to go into detail about business model devel­
opment (the third stage of NPD). During DS-I, 
this aspect will only be touched upon in terms 
of how it can assist as well as support circular 
40
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:59==
product innovation and what overall conside­
rations companies need to make in this regard.
With the overall NPD process in place for get­
ting a product to market, the next focus was on 
what factors companies need to consider when 
designing their new devices for circularity.
Circular Product Design: a combi­
nation of approaches
The circular economy aims to "close the loop" 
(Bocken et al., 2016), where resources are cap­
tured at EoL through circular strategies and 
put back into the resource flow. This approach 
speaks to the cradle-to-cradle (C2C) strategy, 
incentivizing product development methods 
that allow for EoL considerations so that pro­
ducts' parts can be broken down and used in 
new cycles (de Pauw, Karana and Kandachar, 
2013). The idea of C2C is closely related to 
eco-effectiveness - which aims to eliminate the 
concept of waste - and together, they act as 
alternative design strategies against the more 
traditional cradle-to-grave (C2G) and eco-ef­
ficiency approaches (Braungart, McDonough, 
and Bollinger, 2007). Eco-efficiency is about 
“doing things right,” minimizing the negative 
impact, and looking into short-term improve­
ments, while eco-effectiveness concerns itself 
with “doing the right things” and considering 
long-term quality and performance improve­
ments (Jacobsen, 1999). Figure 2.9 illustrates 
the two strategies.
The two strategies are not considered opposi­
tes but instead different ways of reducing the 
environmental impacts within the product's li­
fecycle, and both are necessary to reap the full 
potential of circularity. Eco-efficiency focuses 
on reducing aspects within the circular eco­
nomy, including consumption, design for phy­
sical durability, and life extension (Braungart, 
Figure 2.8: A combined NPD process developed for the support framework
41
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:60==
Figure 2.9: The output in terms of environmental impact for eco-efficiency and eco-effectiveness over time. 
Figure made based on Braungart, McDonough, and Bollinger, 2007 and Toxopeus, de Koeijer, and Maij, 2015
McDonough, and Bollinger, 2007), aiming tow­
ards the goal of zero waste through reducti­
on and minimization strategies. This again ties 
in with the main principles of eco-design that 
utilize the waste hierarchy (European Commis­
sion, 2009) as a way to guide its practices to 
strive for the prevention of waste through the 
prioritization order of prevent over reuse, reu­
se over recycling, and recycling over disposal 
(den Hollander, Bakker and Hultink, 2017). 
However, eco-efficient products are commonly 
hybrid products made up of multiple material 
types that cannot be separated and are, the­
refore, only downcycled or cascaded to other 
product cycles (McDonough and Braungart, 
2002). Eco-effectiveness, on the other hand, 
aims towards a C2C cyclical flow of materials 
and does not focus on the longevity of the pro­
ducts as long as the products can be upcyc­
led into the cyclical loop after EoL (Braungart, 
McDonough, and Bollinger, 2007). Therefore, 
eco-effective products are designed with a 
long-term strategy in mind, meaning that the 
product can be disassembled into separate 
materials and upcycled to new lifecycles within 
the same system (McDonough and Braungart, 
2002). However, reducing product complexity 
to fewer components and using less diverse 
materials to allow for eco-effectiveness has 
posed a significant challenge for industries due 
to inadequate research and a lack of simpler 
alternatives (Franco, 2017). 
Circular product design strives to close the loop 
and follow a C2C lifecycle similar to the eco-ef­
fectiveness strategy. However, it also encom­
passes elements of the eco-efficiency appro­
ach by prolonging the lifecycle and utilization 
of products and materials. This highlights the 
necessity of considering both eco-effectiveness 
and eco-efficiency in circular product design, 
as it is the absolute approach (Faber, Jorna, and

==PAGE:61==
Figure 2.10: Mapping eco-efficiency and eco-ef­
fectiveness into Bocken et al.’s model (2016). 
It shows the current system and the goal of 
utilizing circular product design to develop 
the circular transition support framework as 
a combination of a slowed, narrowed, and 
circular loop system
von Engelen, 2005; den Hollander, Bakker, and 
Hultink, 2017), which maximizes the potential 
for transitioning towards a circular economy. 
However, achieving this requires designing pro­
ducts with circular design principles in mind 
from the onset, ensuring an LCT approach, 
which is crucial given that 80% of a product's 
total environmental impact is determined du­
ring the early stages of product development 
(European Commission, 2016). 
When applying the theory of Bocken et al. 
(2016), the eco-effectiveness strategy focuses 
on closing the loop, which is done by imple­
menting the new circular economy business 
model. However, eco-efficiency focuses on 
prolonging and narrowing the loop (Bocken et 
al., 2016), attempting to minimize the impacts 
across the linear lifecycle of the product. So, to 
achieve the optimal version of Bocken et al.'s 
(2016) model of the slowed, narrowed, and 
closed resource flows, there is a need to com­
bine the two aspects of eco-effectiveness and 
eco-efficiency, as illustrated in Figure 2.10, to 
achieve effective circular product design. Im­
plementing the circular product design appro­
ach will allow for the most effective transition 
toward a circular economy. In the following 
sections, guidelines and practices on how to 
achieve this will be presented.
Circular Design Strategies
Stahel proposed in 2010 with his Intertia princip­
le a guideline for circular design:
“Do not repair what is not broken, do not rema­
nufacture something that can be repaired, 
do not recycle a product that can be remanufac­
tured. Replace or treat only the smallest possible 
part in order to maintain the existing economic 
value of the technical system”. 
- Stahel (2010, p. 195)
Translating this principle into the design, it rela­
tes to a product's integrity and durability, me­
aning the degree to which a product remains 
true to its, from the factory, original state over 
time (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017). 
Consistent with the principle of Inertia, the ob­
jective is to maintain the product as near to its 
initial state as feasible, minimizing the environ­
mental effects by preserving or regaining the 
product's increased monetary value over time 
(den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017) - si­
milar to the thinking of the zero waste hierarchy 
(ZWIA, 2022). Ideally, based on the Inertia prin­
ciple, nothing would ever have to be recycled 
or become waste. While this may not be enti­
rely possible in reality, striving for this goal will 
keep the product and its materials in use for as 
long as possible and subsequently reduce the 
amount of waste being produced.
The concept of the circular economy is cente­
red around the life cycle of products, and it is 
important to have control over the inflow and 
outflow of the system to achieve a closed-loop 
system. However, the lifetime of a product is 
often cut short as many products are discarded 
before they stop functioning. This is where the 
concept of product obsolescence comes into 
play, which refers to the point at which a pro­
duct loses its usefulness to the user or consu­
mer (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017; 
Burns, 2010), resulting in a loss of perceived 
value. Circular practices, strategies, and design 
43
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:62==
can help reclaim value and prevent obsole­
scence through multiple use cycles. However, 
if the obsolescence cannot be rebounded, the 
only option is to recover value through mate­
rial recirculation, which ultimately destroys the 
product's integrity and brings its lifetime to an 
end (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017).
By connecting the principle of Inertia with the 
idea of obsolescence, it becomes clear that the 
most critical aspect of a product's lifecycle is its 
Lifecycle of the Case Company’s Devices
Shifting the focus to the case company provides 
further insights into why the use phase holds the 
most significant potential and also arguments for 
why efforts when designing for circularity should 
concern how to extend the lifespan of medical de­
vices.
For the case company's SUDs within the business 
area containing ostomy care products, it was 
found that the products have very short lifespans. 
These lifespans depend heavily on the composition 
of the bag itself, whether it is a one- or two-piece 
solution and the type of ostomy the user has (Ap­
pendix B). As seen in Figure 2.11, a large amount of 
waste is being produced weekly due to current use 
recommendations. However, these SUDs still func­
tion as intended beyond reaching their re­
commended obsolescence but are discar­
ded for the sole reason of them being full.
However, these numbers pertain exclusi­
vely to Denmark, where the advanced he­
althcare system covers the cost of ostomy 
products for users, resulting in shorter 
product use cycles (Coloplast, 2017). Due 
to costs, the usage trends in developing 
countries differ (Appendix A). Notably, 
a comprehensive study done in the US 
(Richbourg, Fellows, and Arroyave, 2008) 
revealed that the average wear time for 
urostomy and ileostomy bags was five 
days, and for colostomy bags, four and a 
half days. Wear times significantly longer 
than the Danish recommendations. This 
study suggests that the interest in longer wear ti­
mes extends beyond cost consideration and repre­
sents an opportunity to be explored. This is corro­
borated by Henning Granslev, chairman of COPA 
(Appendix G), who asserts that Danish users are 
also interested in prolonging the lifespan of their 
products. Granslev reports wearing his ileostomy 
bag for two days but acknowledges that others 
wear the same bag for up to six days, provided that 
the baseplate, bag, and skin are compatible. This 
suggests that there is potential in allowing ostomy 
bags - and SUDs in general - to be rethought with 
a longer use cycle in mind, given that users are al­
ready accustomed to the practice. This would pro­
long and narrow the resource loop, which could be 
achieved through circular product design, trans­
forming the SUDs into reusable medical devices.
use phase and how to prolong it. In a literature 
review of 140 research articles on NPD in the 
context of the circular economy, Pinheiro et 
al. (2019) found that the use phase was consi­
stently regarded as the most significant and im­
pactful phase, emphasizing the need to extend 
it through circular design strategies. However, 
the use phase was also mentioned in correlati­
on with the pre- and post-use phases in 43% of 
cases, emphasizing the importance of conside­
ring all aspects of a product's lifecycle simulta­

baseplates

Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:63==
neously through the use of LCT to successfully 
implement circular economy practices covering 
the entire lifecycle.
Aguiar et al. (2022) conducted an extensive sy­
stematic literature review investigating what 
strategies are proposed in literature when at­
tempting circular product design as a means for 
the circular economy transition. In this review, it 
was found that the foundation of what is known 
as circular product design is heavily guided by 
the article by Bocken et al. (2016). In this ar­
ticle (Bocken et al., 2016), the proposal for the 
categorization of narrowing, slowing, and clo­
sing the resource loop is presented, which was 
found to have been cited 623 times  (Aguiar et 
al., 2021), emphasizing the broad application of 
the approach to circular design. Moreover, the 
literature review also mentions Bakker et al.’s 
(2014) article regarding product life extension, 
which Bocken et al.'s (2016) article builds upon 
and is cited 259 times as the second most ci­
ted article in relation to circular product design. 
Lastly, the article by den Hollander, Bakker, and 
Hultink (2017) was cited 150 times across the 
literature in the literature review. Hence, given 
the widespread application of these strategies in 
literature, these three articles will establish the 
basis for the content and considerations in the 
subsequent sections. They will adhere to Bocken 
et al.'s (2016) framework for the categorization 
of Narrowing, Slowing, and Closing the resour­
ce loop in relation to circular design strategies, 
incorporating the previously identified conside­
rations for eco-efficiency and eco-effectiveness.
Narrowing the Loop
Narrowing the loop focuses on maximizing re­
source efficiency (Bocken et al., 2016). This in­
volves minimizing the resources utilized in both 
the production process and the product itself, 
following the traditional C2G lifecycle, and pri­
oritizing eco-efficiency (McDonough and Braun­
gart, 2002). Unlike the other two approaches, 
narrowing the loop does not aim to influence 
the rate of resources exiting the system (Bocken 
et al., 2016). This approach can be implemen­
ted without causing significant disruption to the 
current linear economy system, which is why it is 
the most widely adopted strategy in the industry 
(Bocken et al., 2016; Bakker et al., 2014) as it low­
ers the economic costs, and is considered good 
practice (Bakker et al., 2014). However, the aspe­
cts and considerations of narrowing the loop will 
not be included in the support framework in the 
PS, as it was previously found that most indu­
stries have already implemented such strategies. 
Slowing the Loop
Based on the Inertia principle by Stahel (2010), 
the aim should be to prevent a product from ever 
reaching obsolescence, and when recirculation 
of the product is necessary, it should occur at 
the highest possible level to ensure the pro­
duct's integrity and maintain the value within 
the system. When designing circular products, 
it is essential to incorporate these EoL conside­
rations into the design process from the begin­
ning to enable the creation of durable products, 
ensuring the recirculation strategies' feasibility 
and effectiveness through subsequent activiti­
es. This is due to that once product specificati­
ons have been established, making significant 
changes becomes challenging as the necessary 
resources, infrastructure, and actions have al­
ready been determined (Bocken et al., 2016). 
The strategy of slowing the resource loop focu­
ses on extending the use phase of the products, 
which Bocken et al. (2016) suggest can be done 
through two approaches: the development of 
products made to last and the extension of the 
product's life. 
Long-life products. The concept of long-li­
fe products suggests a new approach toward 
product design, which considers emotional and 
physical durability (Bocken et al., 2016; den Hol­
lander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017) that allow for 
longer utilization periods, which den Hollan­
der, Bakker, and Hultink (2017) refer to as re­
sisting obsolescence. Physical durability refers 
to a product's capability to withstand damage 
without breaking down and is connected to its 
reliability, the trust in the product that it will not 
break down unintendedly (Bocken et al., 2016; 
den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017). Emo­
tional durability aims to maintain a product’s 
relevance and desirability over time, making it 
less likely to be replaced (Bocken et al., 2016; 
den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017) th­
rough design for attachment and trust. 
45
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:64==
The concept in design stresses the importan­
ce of involving end-users in the new product 
development process to ensure their support 
for any changes. This is reinforced by Pinheiro 
et al. (2019), who found that in almost half of 
the papers reviewed, customers were identified 
as key stakeholders in the adoption of circular 
practices. Also, the case company (Appendix 
Con. 1) highlighted the need to involve exper­
ts such as healthcare professionals (HCPs) and 
end-users, as they hold significant influence 
over which products are chosen and thus wield 
purchasing power in the Danish MedTech indu­
stry. Involving them in the process can facilitate 
education and change consumers' perception 
of the transition process, dispel misconcepti­
ons about the technical process and sterility 
level of reusable medical devices, and promo­
te awareness of the environmental benefits of 
circular strategies (Eisenreich et al., 2022). Ulti­
mately, this can make consumers more ame­
nable to accepting circular strategies and acti­
vely choosing circular medical devices, making 
the strategy of designing for attachment and 
trust highly desirable.
Life extension. The second approach sugge­
sted for slowing resource loops is design for 
life extension (Bocken et al., 2016), focusing on 
extending the use phase of the product's life­
cycle through services. When considering the 
waste hierarchy (European Commission, 2009), 
this approach focuses on the “reuse” aspect of 
the model, which den Hollander, Bakker, and 
Hultink (2017) refer to as strategies aiming to 
postpone or reverse the obsolescence of the 
product. Postponing refers to prolonging the 
time to which the product becomes obsolete, 
while reversing refers to the product being ta­
ken back to a state where it is again useful or 
significant to the user. The suggested strategi­
es to postpone and reverse obsolescence can 
be seen in Figure 2.12 (Bocken et al., 2016; den 
Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017; Bakker et 
al., 2014). They follow the principles of Inertia 
(Stahel, 2010), the zero waste hierarchy (ZWIA, 
2022), and the Butterfly model (EMF, 2013a), 
meaning that the strategies listed (Figure 2.12) 
first require the least amount of rework and are 
found to be the most environmentally and eco­
nomically sustainable. 
It is worth noting that extending the lifespan of 
a product requires more than just implemen­
ting strategies for life extension (Franco, 2017). 
The design must also prioritize durability, con­
sidering the recovery processes and incorpo­
rating high-quality materials (Eisenreich et al., 
2022). Additionally, the product must be de­
signed for easy dis- and reassembly to allow 
for multiple use phases (Bocken et al., 2016). 
Modular design further facilitates disassembly 
and component-level deconstruction, making 
remanufacturing and reprocessing more feasi­
ble (Eisenreich et al., 2022). Reprocessing is li­
mited to the medical device context and refers 
to the process of restoring a used device to a 
state that allows for its safe reuse. This is done 
through decontamination processes based on 
the device’s criticality level, as suggested by 
Spaulding (1971), to ensure patient safety. It 
also includes testing and other procedures re­
quired to ensure the medical device's technical 
and functional usage (European Commission, 
2021a). This process is a necessary prerequisite 
for life-extension processes, such as refurbis­
hment and remanufacturing, to return it to a 
usable state and allow for new use cycles. The 
decontamination process is moreover a neces­
sary prerequisite for recycling as well, as to en­
sure safe handling.

==PAGE:65==
Figure. 2.12: Slowing the resource loop, including Postponing, resisting, and reversing obsolescence design strate­
gies ordered following the principles of the waste hierarchy (ZWIA, 2022) and Stahel (2010), with the least radical 
processes on top and the most resource-consuming in bottom
Maintenance (retain value): den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink (2017) define maintenance as "the 
performance of inspection and/or servicing tasks at regular intervals to retain a product's functio­
nal capabilities and/or cosmetic condition." Maintenance is considered a preventative strategy, as 
opposed to repair, which is seen as a corrective measure. The maintenance servicing task involves 
making necessary adjustments to the product's settings and removing foreign elements, such as 
dust, to ensure proper product operation (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017)
Upgrading (enhance value): Upgrading aims to improve the current product beyond its original 
state to allow for potential future expansion and modification (Bocken et al., 2016; den Hollander, 
Bakker, and Hultink, 2017).
Repair (restore value): Repair is referred to as corrective maintenance (den Hollander, Bakker, and 
Hultink, 2017), and it refers to the restoring process of the product back to its usable state after it 
has been subject to deterioration or damage (Bakker et al., 2014; Bocken et al., 2016). Commonly, 
repair does not restore the product to its original state, but instead, a lower quality and the repair 
only includes the damaged part, not the entire product (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017; 
Bocken et al., 2016).
Repurposing/recontextualizing: In literature, repurposing and recontextualizing refer to the same 
strategy: "to utilize a product or its components in a role that it was not originally designed to per­
form" (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017). The only difference is that recontextualization 
also considers the context in which the product or component is used, not only its role.
Refurbishment/reconditioning: Refurbishment or reconditioning is restoring an obsolete product 
to a sufficient functional working condition that is inferior to its original condition. This can be 
done through rebuilding, repairing, or replacing all larger components that do not function as in­
tended or are close to failing - even in instances where the customer has not noticed the faults yet 
(Bakker et al., 2014; den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017)
Remanufacturing: Remanufacturing refers to the process of restoring an obsolete product to its 
original or even better performance level than that of a brand-new product (Bakker et al., 2014). 
This involves a series of procedures carried out by the original manufacturer or a third party con­
tracted by the manufacturer. The product is disassembled, and its parts are carefully examined 
and repaired using functional used components and, when necessary, new factory components to 
ensure that the product meets the same quality standards as a brand new product (den Hollander, 
Bakker, and Hultink, 2017).

Costs of SUDs
The potential for implementing these types of 
strategies is not solely to benefit the environment; 
it is also an economic saving for companies, he­
althcare facilities, and consumers. Considering 
the use pattern of the case company's ostomy 
bags, as seen in Figure 2.11, the amount of waste 
produced by the SUDs is significant, emphasizing 
the need for circular design practices. Looking 
into the cost of the devices, the potential becomes 
even more evident. Table 2.1 shows the costs of 
Coloplast's Sensura Mio products for a use peri­
od of a month when purchased from a wholesa­
ler (here Mediq, 2009) - the provider selling the 
product to the customers through the municipal 
license. The full overview of the cost calculations 
is in Appendix H, with the parameters utilized for 
the "worst-case-use scenario".
According to the analysis, it was discovered that 
the one-piece colostomy bag is the most expen­
sive option among the available types. This is 
mainly because of its short lifespan, which leads 
to a higher daily consumption rate. On the other 
hand, ileostomy and urostomy bags have a lon­
ger recommended wear time of one day, making 
their overall costs significantly lower than the co­
lostomy bag. The two-piece options are even more 
cost-effective as they can be partially disassemb­
led, thus increasing their lifespan. The investigati­
on into the present DfX practices (design for dis­
assembly and modularity) at the case company 
revealed that the economic benefits of design for 
life extension outweigh the environmental bene­
fits alone and can be considered a viable business 
strategy. This highlights the fact that adopting 
DfX strategies is a conceivable approach towards 
circular product innovation and a significant step 
towards a circular economy. Especially if multiple 
DfX strategies are implemented simultaneously 
to allow for reuse, the potential of reversing pro­
duct obsolescence is increased, enhancing the 
benefits of the circular economy.
Table 2.1: Purchasing cost of Coloplast’s ostomy products over a duration of a month for 
the consumer, paid by the municipal license - using external (the wholesaler’s) prices
The goal of the outlined strategies is to help bu­
sinesses understand how to create long-lasting 
products and extend the life of their products 
in order to slow down the resource loop. The­
se strategies take into account the waste hie­
rarchy and the Inertia principle, which means 
that companies are encouraged to implement 
only what is necessary for the benefit of both 
the economy and the environment. Furthermo­
re, these strategies emphasize the need for LCT

from the outset, including strategies to resist,

developed through this thesis enabled compa­
nies to create their individual roadmaps, selec­
