specific needs.
Closing the Loop
In order to effectively close the resource loop, it 
is essential to consider EoL considerations ear­
ly on in the design process using LCT. However, 
many companies, including those in the Med­
Tech industry, struggle to develop closed-cycle 
solutions because they neglect to prioritize re­
covery-focused design from the onset (Eisen­
reich et al., 2022). This is in part due to the com­
position of products and medical devices, as well 
as their potential for material-level recycling, as 
discussed in terms of both eco-efficiency and 
eco-effectiveness. To enable effective recycling, 
it is crucial to design products and devices with 
recyclability and disassembly down to their ma­
terial level in mind, avoiding "monstrous hybrids" 
composed of multiple components (McDo­
nough and Braungart, 2002) and minimizing the 
use of adhesives, complexity, and component 
count (Eisenreich et al., 2022). However, this task 
is challenging for companies, particularly tho­
se in the MedTech industry (Franco, 2017). The 
strategies proposed for the technological cycle 
are very similar to those outlined in the secti­
on on eco-efficiency and recycling, emphasizing 
upcycling over downcycling (McDonough and 
Braungart, 2002) and maintaining material pro­
perties in the next lifecycle through primary or 
closed-loop recycling (Bocken et al., 2016).
However, simply closing the loop is not enough; 
companies must also adopt strategies to slow 
the loop and thereby maximize the potential of 
circular economy practices. EoL considerations 
must be incorporated from the outset, including 
design for disassembly at both component and 
material levels, which will enable prolonging 
and resisting obsolescence and facilitate upcy­
cling to keep materials within the same system. 
Only by implementing both slowing and clo­
sing-the-loop strategies can the desired version 
of Bocken et al.'s (2016) model be achieved. This 
means that both aspects were included in the 
development of the support framework.
Circular Practices in the Industry
The MedTech industry increasingly focuses on 
the circular economy and its practices (Ishaq et 
al., 2024). However, companies still face chal­
lenges in implementing these practices due to 
a lack of understanding of how to successfully 
initiate or navigate the transition (Ishaq et al., 
2024), which is the gap intended to be filled 
by the support framework developed through 
this thesis. Given that MedTech companies 
play a significant role in healthcare, they are 
well-positioned to lead the circular economy 
transition (Ishaq et al., 2024). By incorporating 
innovative circular solutions in the design and 
production of medical devices, the source of 
the problem is targeted directly. This can en­
hance resource efficiency, reduce the environ­
mental impact, boost resource efficiency, and, 
most importantly, reduce the large amount 
of waste being produced (Ishaq et al., 2024; 
Eisenreich et al., 2022), especially regarding 
SUDs. These circular practices can moreover 
contribute to economic cost savings for Med­
Tech companies, healthcare facilities, and con­
sumers as the cost per product over its entire 
lifetime decreases (Ishaq et al., 2024).
Several MedTech companies have already suc­
cessfully achieved these goals, as investigated by 
Ishaq et al. (2024). They found that implemen­
ting circular principles, as suggested by Bock­
en et al. (2016), enabled the creation of devices 
that were designed to be reused without com­
promising patient safety or functionality (Ishaq 
49
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:68==
Figure 2.13: Findings from the case studies presented by Ishaq et al. (2024) categorized into the circular stra­
tegies presented in the earlier sections
et al., 2024; Aguiar and Jugend, 2022). However, 
these processes required the establishment of 
circular supply chains (Ishaq et al., 2024), col­
laborative value networks, and circular busi­
ness models in tandem with circular product 
innovation to redirect resources back into the 
technological cycle and lower the environmen­
tal impact through RL. In Figure 2.13, the ten­
dencies from the case study can be seen, where 
the strategies employed by the companies have 
been translated into the strategies for circular 
product design.
From the analysis of the case studies, it is clear 
how various MedTech companies are trying to 
extend the lifespan of their devices and resist ob­
solescence. However, each company has its own 
methods for doing so, ranging from minimal re­
pair to complete replacement of parts for rema­
nufacturing. The level of recirculation required 
depends on the device's damage, and different 
companies have implemented multiple different 
strategies to extend their devices' lifespan most 
effectively. The analysis also highlights the cor­
relation between circularity strategies and the 
need for supportive structures, as the majority 
of companies have implemented designs for li­
fe-extension and EoL, RL to recapture their pro­
ducts and resources after the use phase (Ishaq 
et al., 2024). Nevertheless, challenges still exist in 
the transition towards a circular economy, par­
ticularly in handling low-value devices and the 
willingness of the supply chain to collaborate on 
RL, which is necessary for the value recovery of 
the devices (Ishaq et al., 2024). Another barrier is 
the lack of closed-loop supply chains, which can 
be addressed through the employment of value 
networks that allow for the collection and reuse 
of device materials at EoL, emphasizing the im­
portance of collaborative stakeholder networks 
in maintaining circular product strategies across 
the MedTech industry's value chain (Ishaq et al., 
2024). The analysis concludes that moving tow­
ards a circular economy is attainable even in a 
highly regulated industry such as MedTech. No­
netheless, the study highlights a disparity in how 
MedTech companies producing SUDs can adopt 
circular product design strategies, emphasizing 
the need for a support framework to facilitate 
a successful transition while accounting for all 
pertinent factors.
Second Factor - Business Mo­
del Innovation
To achieve successful circular product design, 
it is essential to incorporate both life-extensi­
on and EoL strategies. This sets requirements 
for specialized manufacturers or providers who 
can recover the value of the products through 
RL (den Hollander, Bakker, and Hultink, 2017) 
for either recirculation of the products, parts, or 
materials. Redesigning the products alone is not 
enough; the development and establishment 
of circular business models and reverse supply 
chains are equally crucial to ensure the feasibili­
50
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:69==
Figure 2.14: Circular Economy Business Model Framework (Pieroni et al., 2020)
51
Descriptive Study I
ty and viability of the circular transition (Bakker 
et al., 2014). Therefore, once the optimal circular 
lifecycle for the device is established, devel­
oping a sustainable supporting business model 
becomes the next step, where it is essential to 
consider the implications of the business mo­
del on the design itself (Bakker et al., 2014). As 
the transition to the circular economy is a new 
way of thinking for companies and requires dra­
matic changes for them, the development pro­
cess of the product should be connected to the 
development of the business model to ensure 
the company's economic success in relation to 
its long-term strategies and capturing of value 
(Bocken et al., 2016). 
The goal of a circular business model is to "[...] 
enable economically viable ways to continu­
ally reuse products and materials [...]" (Bocken 
et al., 2016). Companies, in collaboration with 
their value chains, can achieve this by modify­
ing how they create, deliver, and monetize their 
value propositions and bridge the gap between 
circular innovation strategies and financial per­
formance (Mendoza et al., 2017). However, 
the lack of actionable guidance on implemen­
ting circular business models and product de­
signs makes the transition process challenging 
(Mendoza et al., 2017). Two approaches that can 
help companies transition towards a more inno­
vative future state are the top-down approach of 
"backcasting" and the bottom-up approach that 
explores different guidelines for implementing 
circular strategies and business models (Mendo­
za et al., 2017). When these two approaches are 
combined, they can assist companies in setting 
long-term goals and identifying actionable steps 
to achieve them. 
The successful transition to a circular economy 
for companies depends on the joint implemen­
tation and realization of strategic top-down and 
bottom-up approaches in one complete fra­
mework with support from relevant stakehol­
ders across the ecosystem and value chain (Li­
eder and Rashid, 2016). The support framework 
developed through this thesis implements both 
business model and product innovation, using 
both a top-down and a bottom-up approach 
to allow for a comprehensive framework for the 
MedTech industry.
However, as stated earlier concerning the sta­
ges of NPD, the development of the supporting 
business model and business case will not be 
covered in detail in the support framework but 
will only be included in terms of the importance 
of carrying out the activity to support the enti­
re circular solution. In the framework, a circular 
economy business model framework (Piero­

Third factor - A prerequisite: 
Reverse logistics
For circular design strategies to be feasible and 
business models to be viable, a crucial prere­
quisite is the establishment of RL (Eisenreich et 
al., 2022). RL encompasses a series of activiti­
es to efficiently manage the collection and re­
covery of products at their EoL, from retrieving 
the product from the customer to the value 
recovery site (Eisenreich et al., 2022; Mallick 
et al., 2023). However, it has been found that 
the development of RL systems has proven 
challenging for companies to implement due 
to the need to establish value recovery pro­
cesses at the EoL to collect obsolete products 
and their resources (Eisenreich et al., 2022). 
Therefore, a circular supply chain is the main 
prerequisite for RL to allow for the coordina­
ted management of the products and the re­
circulation of resources that are necessary for 
the circular transition (Mallick et al., 2023). And 
for a company with a currently linear business 
model, the first step for them is to establish 
RL’s partners and practices from scratch (Eisen­
reich et al., 2022). Although this can be done 
internally, many companies (Ishaq et al., 2024), 
including the case company (Appendix Con. 1), 
would choose to outsource RL and overall va­
lue recovery processes to external value chain 
partners to avoid the start-up costs, risk, and 
responsibility associated with the establish­
ment of such facilities and services as well as 
insurance of the devices' performance and sa­
fety level (Eisenreich et al., 2022). These colla­
boration partners allow for the establishment 
of maintenance and repair services and value 
recovery processes, which are currently mis­
sing from the linear value chain but need to 
be included within the value network for the 
circular economy.
The success of value recovery strategies hinges 
on the collaboration of various departments 
52
Descriptive Study I
Figure 2.15: The necessary aspects to consider when designing reverse logistics (Mallick et al., 2023)
==PAGE:71==
Box 2.13
Requirements for the establishment of 
reverse logistics
•	
Strong collaboration and partnerships 
with the value chain
•	
Establishment of a service provider for 
maintenance & repair and/or value 
recovery as part of the value network
•	
Establishment of collection services/
providers
•	
Considerations for the necessary steps 
for reverse logistics: collection, trans­
portation, storage, sorting, and re­
covery of the medical devices
53
Descriptive Study I
and stakeholders throughout the product's li­
fecycle. These stakeholders must capture the 
residual value of products through value re­
covery processes, but first, they must collect 
the products through the RL system, which in 
turn allows them to provide consumers with 
as-new products, ultimately lowering mate­
rial costs for companies and ensuring long-
term economic benefits (Bocken et al., 2016). 
However, when implementing RL, it is essen­
tial to consider the users' motivation to return 
their used products at EoU/EoL, which is due 
to users holding power in determining the RL 
system's success (Eisenreich et al., 2022). The 
greatest challenge across industries is the un­
certainty associated with users returning pro­
ducts. If products are not returned on time, it 
can slow down the value recovery processes to 
transform the used products into as-new ones, 
leading to revenue loss for companies (Eisen­
reich et al., 2022). Therefore, it can be bene­
ficial to incorporate incentives as drivers for 
consumer engagement (Shumon, Ahmed, and 
Ahmed, 2016) in the form of economic incenti­
ves, information and awareness strategies, and 
convenience aspects to help get consumers in­
terested and invested in the circular economy 
transition (Mallick et al., 2023). Figure 2.15 
shows the steps and aspects necessary when 
designing a reverse logistic system, emphasi­
zing both consumer and value network enga­
gement. 
Design of reverse logistics. Mallick et al. 
(2023) have introduced a comprehensive fra­
mework outlined in Figure 2.16 that companies 
can use to plan and implement RL effectively to 
Figure 2.16: Conceptual framework for planning reverse logistics as suggested by Mallick et al. (2023)
==PAGE:72==
Figure 2.17: The reverse logistics system of Returpen as suggested by Mallick et al. (2022)
support the circular transition’s initiatives. This 
framework incorporates the key considerati­
ons presented in Figure 2.15 and outlines the 
necessary steps for successful implementati­
on. This includes 1) the product characteristics, 
meaning what aspects relating to the product 
need to be considered when designing the RL 
system, 2) the main drivers and motivations 
for why companies should consider RL, 3) the 
establishment of necessary partnerships and 
collaboration within the value chain, 4) the 
potential barriers for the RL system stated by 
the regulators and lastly 5) the design of the 
actual RL system considering the necessary in­
centives, governance and performance mana­
gement aspects to ensure its viability (Mallick 
et al., 2023). This framework will also serve as a 
valuable tool within the support framework for 
RL planning for MedTech companies, as it is a 
prerequisite for the circular transition.
 
A Practical Example of Reverse Lo­
gistics Regarding SUDs
When looking at RL systems and examples from 
the industry, the Returpen system by Novo 
Nordisk comes to mind. Novo Nordisk's insulin 
pens can be categorized as SUDs, and the Re­
turpen system is, therefore, a great example of 
what an RL system could look like for low-va­
lue devices. This is, however, rare in literature, 
as investigated by van Boerdonk, Krikke, and 
Lambrechts (2021). They acknowledged that 
these initiatives mainly apply to high-value 
devices due to the added value recovery and 
RL costs, making them viable only for products 
worth sustaining. This is further substantiated 
by an extensive study conducted by Buschak 
et al. (2011) examining 12 Medtech companies 
and their implementation of circular initiatives 
and RL, where all 12 of the case studies revolve 
around high-value medical devices designed 
for reuse. Thus, underlines that the viability of 
circularity practices depends on the device's 
value and whether it can sustain the economic 
costs related to the circular strategies, emp­
hasizing the necessary shift from SUDs to re­
usable devices.
Even though the insulin pens remain SUDs, their 
aim was to collect them and take responsibility 
for the EoL through recycling, thereby recaptu­
ring the value of their products and employing 
LCT (Mallick et al., 2022). Even though the re­
turn rate of the pens was only 13% across the 
six months of the pilot (Mallick et al., 2022), the 
case study of the Returpen suggests the feasi­
bility of a RL system for medical devices and 
especially SUDs, which is the main focus for this 
54
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:73==
thesis. In the study by Mallick et al. (2022), the 
RL system for the Returpen is suggested, as can 
be seen in Figure 2.17. This is a valuable source 
of inspiration for designing an RL system for 
low-value medical devices in Denmark and can 
guide other MedTech companies in exploring 
collection principles and strategies when de­
veloping their roadmap. A support framework 
will help facilitate these findings and their pra­
ctical application.
Evaluation of Circularity Per­
formance
When the circular solution, including its design, 
business model, RL, and supporting collabora­
tive stakeholder network, is in place, it can be 
beneficial to benchmark it against the current 
linear solution to assess the circularity perfor­
mance of the new solution and identify po­
tential improvement opportunities. Since this 
tool can be necessary during the developmen­
tal stage of the circular solution to determine 
the viability of the initiative, the data available 
for the new solution will be mostly qualitative 
in nature. Consequently, the assessment tool 
must also be based on this data type. This type 
of evaluation is commonly known as a sustaina­
bility screening (Kravchenko et al., 2020), which 
provides valuable insights into the potential 
performance of a new initiative and helps top 
management decide whether to implement it.
Upon reviewing the literature, it is apparent 
that a multitude of tools are available to as­
sess the performance of circular initiatives, and 
Table 2.2 provides a comprehensive overview 
of all the reviewed tools. The European Com­
mission (2018) and Saidani et al. (2017) suggest 
a total of 17 tools. However, only five tools are 
designed for a company-wide scope, and upon 
examining these tools (A to E), only one (tool 
C) is considered applicable. However, due to 
its limited scope, it is difficult to conclusively 
determine if the circular strategy proposed is 
superior to the baseline. The Danish Techno­
logical University has, however, proposed two 
more promising measurement tools (F and 
G). However, the Sustainability Screening tool 
(Kravchenko et al., 2020) (tool G) was deemed 
inapplicable due to its large number of steps 
and its high complexity, which was not feasi­
ble for companies to complete without expert 
assistance, which was a requirement.
The Circularity Assessment tool (CAT) (tool F), 
as recommended by Shahbazi et al. (2020), ap­
pears to be the most promising option for as­
sessing circular economy transition strategies. 
It directly compares at least two solutions using 
a simplistic Excel spreadsheet based on a list 
of design guidelines. However, a prerequisite 
for utilizing this tool is that the company must 
possess a comprehensive understanding of 
circular design strategies and business models, 
enabling them to select relevant guidelines 
from the list. Fortunately, a support framework 
was available to facilitate this process and sug­
gest relevant strategies for implementation in 
the transition roadmap. Through the inputs in 
the tool, a winner was identified, and indicati­
ons of areas for potential improvements were 
pointed out. Overall, the Circularity Assess­
ment tool (tool F) was selected as the preferred 
method for assessing and comparing solution 
performances due to its broad, customizable 
scope and simple interface that companies can 
efficiently utilize without assistance.
55
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:74==
Table 2.2: Assessment and applicability of the investigated measurement tools. (Tool A: EMF, 2015b, Tool B: Evans 
and Bocken, 2013, Tool C: Ganbatte World, 2017, Tool D: European Commission, 2018, Tool E: Cayzer, Griffiths and 
Beghetto, 2017, Tool F: Shahbazi et al., 2020, Tool G:  Kravchenko et al., 2020)

Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:75==
Conclusion
This marks the conclusion of the Descriptive 
Study I phase. Throughout this phase, the most 
pressing factors that require attention for the 
PS phase have been identified and addressed. 
The implementation of circular economy pra­
ctices in the MedTech industry requires the 
establishment of a collaborative stakeholder 
network that includes the new circular value 
network and ecosystem stakeholders. Esta­
blishing such a network will enable benefici­
al collaboration through co-development and 
knowledge sharing, mitigate risks, and ensure 
support amongst the stakeholders for the 
circular transition to ensure the successful im­
plementation of circular initiatives in MedTech 
companies. With the collaborative stakeholder 
network, MedTech companies can continuous­
ly engage and communicate with their stake­
holders to ensure their willingness to partici­
pate in these initiatives and thereby increase 
the feasibility of the circular initiatives. This 
includes the presence of a significant market 
size and bargaining power within the value 
chain to ensure that the MedTech companies 
have enough enforcement power to demand 
the new circular initiatives. If these factors are 
absent, then restructuring the value network 
by centralizing production or externalizing it 
should be considered to obtain the necessa­
ry market share and enforcement power. Mo­
reover, in the ecosystem, policymakers were 
also found to play crucial roles in the transiti­
on process, and involving them in the network 
from the beginning can help prevent potential 
hindrances from emerging later on in terms of 
regulatory compliance aspects. The financial 
network can moreover provide the necessary 
push for MedTech companies to transition tow­
ards circular practices, as green procurement 
strategies can incentivize companies to fulfill 
circular requirements, making them another 
asset necessary for the network. Therefore, the 
collaborative stakeholder network can help 
with support and knowledge and create the 
necessary incentives for the MedTech industry 
to transition toward the circular economy. In 
contrast, without it, the feasibility and viability 
of the initiatives are dramatically reduced.
Based on the ready2LOOP assessment conduc­
ted, it was found that the area where the indu­
stry was facing the most significant challenges 
was within circular product innovation, thus 
was chosen as the focus point. However, as 
the implementation and feasibility of circular 
product design required a collaborative stake­
holder network to sustain the radical organi­
zational changes and RL system as well as a 
supporting circular business model to sustain 
the circular design strategies, these too ne­
eded to be considered. The development of the 
new circular solution followed the NPD process 
due to the large number of changes required 
to the current system and assisted in ordering 
the structure for the support framework in the 
PS. Even though this approach included the 
development of the business model and busi­
ness case, it was decided not to focus on it due 
to the scope and time constraints of this the­
sis. Instead, it focused on its importance and its 
correlation with circular product design. 
For the support framework, the focus for the 
circular product innovation was on the aspects 
relating to slowing the resource loop concer­
ning postponing, resisting, and reversing pro­
duct obsolescence, and strategies focusing on 
closing the loop. It had been found that these 
aspects were the ones the industry faced the 
greatest challenges in implementing, especially 
concerning low-value devices. It was moreover 
found that in order to obtain the best solution, 
companies should consider both slowing and 
closing the loop strategies in order to do effec­
tive circular product design. All of these would 
require the need for LCT, where considerations 
for the product’s entire lifecycle are considered 
during the device’s development to mitigate 
potential unforeseen consequences later on in 
its lifecycle. This meant that if LCT were emplo­
yed, the device would be designed to undergo 
its value recovery processes and thereby en­
sure the feasibility of its circular strategies and 
the circular redesign. 
An essential prerequisite for the circular eco­
nomy was establishing RL to support value 
recovery. However, effective RL requires esta­
blishing partners and providers within the 
extended value network, including mainten­
ance and repair services and value recovery 
companies, to accommodate these parts of 
the product's lifecycle. Additionally, succes­
57
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:76==
sful collection and logistics related to product 
retrieval were necessary for a viable business 
model that achieves the goal of closing the re­
source loop, which can be further made pos­
sible through policies such as the EPR and the 
LCT approach, where responsibility is shared 
across the product's life and that EoL practices 
are considered at the development of the pro­
duct itself.
Keeping the research question in mind, this 
phase’s findings indicated that companies 
must consider various factors to transition to 
a circular economy successfully. These fac­
tors included incorporating LCT into circular 
product design, developing a business model 
that could sustain the circular design strate­
gies, and establishing RL to support the value 
recovery processes of the devices. However, 
for any of these steps to be feasible, a collabo­
rative value network, including value recovery 
companies, was a prerequisite emphasizing 
the importance of stakeholder engagement. 
These considerations are essential for devel­
oping a support framework to determine the 
necessary factors for a company's successful 
transition to a circular economy. Additionally, 
identifying the necessary steps for the tran­
sition and the order in which they should be 
executed is crucial, as the actionable plan for 
reaching a circular economy is the most sig­
nificant challenge companies face during this 
process, which was assisted by the Stage-gate 
model. The collected findings of the phase can 
be seen in Figure 2.18.
Figure 2.18: Necessary factors to include in the support framework in relation to circular product design
58
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:77==
End of Descriptive Study I
With the findings gathered throughout DS-
I, there was a need to refine the thesis goal 
to focus on product innovation as found th­
rough the ready2LOOP assessment to be the 
main problem area within the industry relating 
to the circular transition. This refinement was 
done to scope the development of a support 
framework properly, meaning that the support 
framework should consider all the impacts re­
lating to the entire device’s lifecycle and sug­
gest how to mitigate them through circular 
strategies and its supporting structures.
Develop a support framework that will faci­
litate the development of a circular economy 
roadmap for the Danish MedTech industry 
with the goal of reducing the amount of wa­
ste produced by SUDs through circular pro­
duct innovation.
Through the RC phase, it was discovered that 
MedTech companies are encountering ob­
stacles in their efforts to transition to a circular 
economy due to a lack of actionable guideli­
nes. They require a roadmap that outlines the 
steps necessary to achieve their goals. This was 
further confirmed during DS-I, which revealed 
that while there is an abundance of literatu­
re outlining what should be considered, the­
re is very little on how to put this into prac­
tice. When examining case studies of MedTech 
companies that have successfully made the 
transition, it was found that they only applied 
the circular strategies to high-value devices, as 
there were significant financial incentives to 
do so. However, there is currently no literature 
available on how to apply these strategies to 
low-value devices such as SUDs, which are the 
primary contributors to the waste produced by 
the industry. This highlights the gap that this 
thesis aims to address by developing a support 
framework that will facilitate the creation of a 
roadmap for Danish MedTech companies. This 
will ultimately reduce the amount of waste 
Figure 2.19: Depiction of the current, the desired future state and the gap that the support framework developed 
through this thesis aims to close
59
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:78==
produced by promoting circular device design. 
The gaps covered by this thesis and the sup­
port framework are illustrated in Figure 2.19.
DS-I highlighted multiple vital areas to con­
sider when transitioning to the circular eco­
nomy. However, due to the limited timeframe 
of this thesis, it was not feasible to delve into 
each of these areas with the same level of de­
tail. Therefore, the focus remained primarily on 
circular product design and EoL management, 
as this area was found to be the one where 
the MedTech industry was having the greatest 
challenges when transitioning, as seen through 
the ready2LOOP benchmarking. However, the 
collaborative stakeholder network was propo­
sed as a prerequisite, and the aim was to sug­
gest who should be included in such a network 
and how to ensure their support. It moreover 
assisted in the feasibility of the design of the RL 
system to support the value recovery proces­
ses suggested by the circular design strategies.

non-negotiable and must be fulfilled, while the 
nice-to-haves are beneficial to have been in­
cluded in the solution but not mandatory for 
the framework’s success.

The third phase of the Design Research Methodology was Prescriptive 
Study. For this thesis, the goal of this phase was to develop a sup­
port framework to facilitate the development of a roadmap to assist 
MedTech companies in transitioning toward the circular economy. This 
phase included the design process, iterations, and the final solution. 
It also included how the aspects from the Descriptive Study I were 
included in the support framework and translated into a roadmap to 
fill the identified gaps. 
•	
The phase included the following sections:
•	
The background for the support framework
•	
The development of the support framework through three iterations
•	
The final support framework, including the support and roadmap
•	
The Evaluation Plan for Descriptive Study II
For the readability of this phase, it is important to note that the support 
framework includes both a support (and support steps) and a roadmap 
(and its activities), meaning that when the support is mentioned, it is not 
the support framework being referred to, but only the support that facili­
tates the development of the roadmap.
Prescriptive Study
63
Prescriptive Study
==PAGE:82==
Background for the Support Framework 
Figure 3.1: When to use the support and how far the support and roadmap will assist the company 
in the process (adapted from Cooper, 2010)
64
Prescriptive Study
The support framework aimed to simplify the 
decision-making process for Danish MedTech 
companies looking to implement circular pro­
duct design and its supporting structures into 
their operations. Completing the support would 
help them develop a roadmap for transitioning 
towards a circular economy focusing on these 
aspects. To ensure inclusivity for a broad ran­
ge of companies within the MedTech industry, 
the framework was kept at a higher level but 
also allowed for the development of an effec­
tive customizable company-specific roadmap. 
The support framework targeted the redesign of 
medical devices and, more specifically, SUDs, as 
the SUDs’ linear lifecycle meant that no circular 
practices were currently in place, meaning that 
the companies had to start from scratch with the 
redesign for circularity. This also meant that the 
support framework did not cater to what was al­
ready being done in terms of circularity but only 
focused on the potential of what could be done 
and how to implement it. Moreover, during the 
previous phases' research, it was beneficial to 
focus primarily on non-critical and semi-critical 
devices (Spaulding, 1971), as the critical devices 
were subject to more strict regulations and po­
licies that could further complicate the imple­
mentation of circular practices. However, Med­
Tech companies producing critical devices could 
still use the support framework, provided they 
considered the additional relevant regulations 
themselves. 
When can the Support Fra­
mework support the Circular 
Economy Transition? 
As stated in DS-I, the aim was to follow the adap­
ted Stage-gate model’s phases (Cooper, 2010) for 
NPD (Figure 3.1) for the support framework. The 
support had the purpose of assisting in comple­
ting the main tasks of the Discovery phase, which 
included examining the environment, engine, 
organization, and initial steps of concept devel­
opment. It also looked into the overarching goal 
and vision, main barriers, and drivers and thereby 
laid the foundation for the project’s business mo­
del. The Scoping phase was also covered by the 
roadmap, but only to help companies define the 
project and product, leaving out the economic 
initiatives relating to it. The Business Case devel­
opment phase was included in the roadmap as 
well, to ensure the feasibility and viability of the 
circular design strategies. However, it provided 
less detail on implementing the business model 
in practice. 
==PAGE:83==
value network. However, most of the tools only 
mention their importance but not how to imple­
ment them, or they only focus on one aspect and 
miss mentioning the importance of their correla­
tions. The support framework developed through 
this thesis fulfilled both these, as it considered all 
three aspects, although in varying degrees, re­
commended how to develop and establish them 
through an actionable roadmap and took into ac­
count their interdependencies. Thereby bridging a 
significant gap in the literature.
Another gap was identified in the 
reliance on experts. This meant 
that the tools investigated either 
required an expert in the tool or 
an expert in the circular economy to understand 
and apply them. This tied in with another pro­
blem as the tools suggested by literature pro­
vided limited control to the company themself. 
This was either due to external experts dictating 
the decisions or the level of generic guidance of­
fered. However, the support framework presen­
ted various options to the company, and the fi­
nal decision on what to prioritize for their circular 
transition path rested solely with them as the 
framework was designed to be completed inde­
pendently by the companies, as all the necessa­
ry information was made available to effectively 
complete both the support and roadmap.
Lastly, the review showed that the 
existing tools were either extremely 
generalized and high-level to be ap­
plied across industries but lacked tan­
gibility or were very company-specific and could 
only be effectively utilized by the company the 
tool was made for. This support framework was 
developed with the MedTech industry in mind, 
meaning it included the higher-level aspects to 
cater to a broader audience, but with the com­
pany’s inputs, it could be customized to target 
the individual company and their needs. 
With the framework, multiple gaps identified du­
ring the systematic literature review were brid­
ged, providing new knowledge relating to the 
circular transition within MedTech and how to 
most effectively guide them through the practi­
cal process.
For the Development phase, the roadmap hel­
ped detail the circular product, as well as how 
to carry out prototyping and testing of the so­
lution and establish the supporting structures, 
including RL and its supporting stakeholders. The 
last two phases, related to the solution's launch, 
were also covered by the roadmap, but only in 
terms of suggestions on what to consider when 
doing piloting and full-scale commercialization. 
Thereby, the support framework covered the 
entire NPD process in varying degrees, empha­
sizing circular product design and its supporting 
systems. It aimed to assist companies in getting 
an overview, order, and structure of the process 
and all the relevant factors to consider when 
transitioning from a linear to a circular economy. 
The support framework particularly emphasized 
the actionability of the steps relating to circular 
product innovation, which was found to be the 
main gap in RC and DS-I and was to be filled by 
the framework.
What differentiates the Sup­
port Framework from existing 
Tools?
During the RC phase, the systematic literature 
review uncovered valuable findings on the avai­
lable resources for companies seeking to shift 
towards a circular economy. While a few gaps 
were identified in existing tools, the support fra­
mework was found to be able to fill those gaps.
It was found that the tools 
focused on the most common 
areas: product design, busi­
ness model development, and

In DS-I, the needed contents of the framework 
were identified; thus, the first step of the PS was 
to define the support’s structure and how and 
where to include all the key aspects. This pro­
cess was assisted through the literature revie­
ws from both the RC phase, where an extensi­
ve review of frameworks, guidelines, tools, and 
methods was conducted, and from the DS-I 
phase, through the deep dive into circular de­
sign strategies and supporting structures. Both 
of these included publications outlining how 
to transition in dramatically different ways and 
with various contents. However, they helped to 
determine the most logical structure and order 
of the steps for this support framework.
The first iteration focused on the development 
of the support and how these would assist in 
the development of the roadmap, but not on 
the development of the roadmap itself. The 
steps within the support were developed by 
taking inspiration from the overall steps sug­
gested by Mendoza et al. (2017). They were, 
however, adjusted to better encapsulate the 
key findings and factors from the DS-I phase, 
and additional steps were added to ensure a 
holistic support framework that was easy for 
MedTech companies to utilize. In Figure 3.4, 
the support structure can be seen, as well as 
the overlaps with Mendoza el al.’s BECE fra­
mework (2017). The process behind and the 
detailed contents of each step from iteration 1 
can be seen in Appendix I. 
As also mentioned in DS-I, the NPD (Cooper, 
2010) process was taken into account when 
developing the support framework. This was 
done to ensure that the process would not 
only allow MedTech companies a successful 
product development (Cooper, 2010) but also 
==PAGE:87==
69
Prescriptive Study
allow them a successful transition towards a 
circular economy (den Hollander, Bakker, and 
Hultink, 2017). 
Prototype 1
The first iteration’s goal was to determine the 
level of comprehension of the support by en­
suring that the order and connections between 
the steps were understandable to users. Thus, 
due to the focus on usability and understan­
dability of the steps rather than usefulness, a 
low-fidelity paper prototype was developed, 
made to be completed by hand. 
To provide context and the necessary know­
ledge for the test subjects to fill in the sup­
port, the participants were given a case with 
a MedTech company, a medical device, and its 
characteristics. They were instructed to follow 
the support step-by-step and provide conti­
nuous feedback on their understanding of the 
contents. They were also encouraged to think 
aloud and indicate if they knew what they had 
to do based on the explanation of the step. The 
test providers observed the process and only 
intervened if the test subjects encountered dif­
ficulties - as the support was intended to be 
completed without expert assistance.
Test results
The overall testing did not go as planned, and 
the complete test findings can be found in Ap­
pendix J. Despite having four individuals parti­
cipate in the test, all participants stopped at or 
after the step about circular product design. It 
became apparent that the test subjects did not 
feel confident in their ability to complete the 
support without continuous assistance. They 
lacked the necessary knowledge about Med­
Tech and the medical device presented in the 
case and thus felt they could not provide va­
luable feedback. Consequently, it was conclu­
ded that for the next test in the second iterati­
on, participants with a background in MedTech 
were necessary to ensure effective framework 
utilization and to collect valuable feedback.
One issue that arose during testing was the 
excessive amount of text within the steps of 
prototype 1. One participant even asked, "Do 
I have to read it all?". While the original inten­
tion was to ensure that those unfamiliar with 
the circular economy could still use the tool 
- without assistance - the abundance of text 
ultimately hindered completion. In response, 
the amount of text and explanations in the 
steps was significantly reduced, with only the 
bare minimum remaining to facilitate under­
standing and guide the user. Figure 3.5 details 
the identified challenges and how they were 
tackled for the next iteration. Nevertheless, the 
initial iteration successfully fulfilled its purpose 
of providing a logical structure and order for 
the support and the user's understanding.

Second Iteration
71
Prescriptive Study
The identified challenges needed to be addres­
sed in the second iteration in relation to the 
cumbersome nature of the support and to en­
sure the usefulness, usability, and a common 
thread across each step. This change was cove­
red for all of the steps and mitigated through 
pop-up attributes and the shortening of the 
introductions’ explanations and instructions 
to make the steps more accessible. The over­
all structure also changed, as some of the steps 
were either combined or divided to make it 
more convenient and understandable for users 
to utilize the support. The full explanation of the 
second iteration can be seen in Appendix K.
Format
The first larger decision was to choose a new 
format for the second iteration. As one of the 
requirements for the support framework was to 
choose a familiar and easily usable format, Ex­
cel seemed to be a straightforward and reliable 
option based on previous research (Blomsma et 
al., 2019). With the new format, it made sense to 
separate each step of the support into individu­
al sheets in Excel and link to the next step when 
the users needed to shift between them to en­
sure cohesiveness between the sheets/steps. 
Moreover, visual guidelines were included to 
assist users in identifying when and where to 
input information, and error messages with de­
scriptions were added when an incorrect input 
had been submitted. Another impactful aspect 
of the new interactive format was only to show 
information that was relevant to the company 
based on their previous inputs and hide unne­
cessary aspects that could seem overwhelming.
Roadmap
With the second iteration of the support's de­
velopment complete, the roadmap needed to 
be developed. The roadmap needed to incor­
porate all the inputs from the support’s steps. 
Therefore, an appropriate type of roadmap was 
needed to encapsulate the NPD process along 
with a circular mindset. In the RC phase, vario­
us types of roadmaps were explored, and it was 
found that a hybridized roadmap was deemed 
the best fit for this thesis, including the strategic 
and process planning type (Phaal, Farrukh, and 
Probert, 2003) (Figure 3.6). 
Roadmap Structure
Through the literature reviews conducted in 
the previous phases, it was found that the 
overall high-level factors to consider for the 
circular transition remain the same across the 
MedTech industry. Based on the data gathe­
red throughout RC and DS-I, the order of the­
se factors was synthesized into the one seen 
in Figure 3.7. In the figure, it can be seen that 
the roadmap has been divided into four sepa­
rate lanes: Organization, Design, Value Chain 
& Ecosystem, and Business Model Innovation. 
The three factors found in RC: circular product 
design, business models, and collaborative sta­
keholder networks, were implemented into the 
three last lanes within the roadmap, with the 
addition being the Organization lane. The need 
for an Organization lane was identified, as 
alignment and resource allocation within the 
organization is needed for a successful circular 
transition (Mendoza et al., 2017),  supported by 
the findings from DS-I. 
As seen in Figure 3.7, the lanes included the 
previously discovered elements making up 
a roadmap: the “know-how,” “know-why,” 
“know-what,” and “know-when” elements 
(Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2004). It can be 
seen that the visualization depicts how the 
different lanes cover the mentioned elements. 
The “know-how” focused on the Organization 
and Design lanes, while the “know-why” inclu­
ded the Value Chain and Ecosystem and Busi­
ness Model Innovation. However, it can be seen 
that the “know-what” was missing from the vi­
sual representation of the roadmap. This was 
because the “know-what” was the overarching 
goal(s) of the roadmap, in this case, the tran­
==PAGE:90==
Figure 3.6:  Overview of the roadmap’s elements taken from two types of roadmaps: strategic planning and pro­
cess planning (Phaal, Farrukh, and Probert, 2003)
In this case, strategic planning focuses on addressing 
and navigating the challenges and opportunities of 
transitioning to more circular practices.
The process planning involves a structured approach 
to driving the transition towards more circular orga­
nizational practices. 
They both focus on the same goal of reaching a circular economy within the chosen technology. Since, for this pro­
ject, the roadmap was a hybrid of the two types, different elements were selected based on the scope or goal of the 
roadmap’s type.
Strategic planning type: Implement the idea of 
steps to achieve long-term business goals, such as 
product innovation, organizational transformati­
on, or value chain adjustments. The level of detail of 
the roadmap will follow the strategic type, meaning 
high-level steps. 
Process planning type: The specific operational 
areas within an organization, such as manufactu­
ring processes, supply chain logistics, or user enga­
gement, were included. 
Since both types target various audiences, the aim was to create a roadmap for both operational and functional 
teams, who would then present the output to higher management.
Figure 3.7: Layout of the roadmap following the NPD stages within the 4 lanes: Organization, Design, Value 
chain and Ecosystem, and Business Model Innovation
72
Prescriptive Study
==PAGE:91==
73
Prescriptive Study
sition to the circular economy of a MedTech 
company. The “know-when” aspect within the 
roadmap was left up to the company filling out 
the framework, as it will be them who need to 
plan out and follow the activities within their 
organization. The only element added within 
the roadmap was to showcase short-term and 
long-term activities.   
The activities within the roadmap followed the 
six stages of the NPD development process, 
and remained in the same visual format for all 
companies utilizing the framework, regardless 
of the inputs in the support’s steps. However, 
how the company intended to target the acti­
vities would differ based on the choices made 
throughout the support. Each activity would 
present the relevant result, including a detailed 
explanation of how to carry out the activities, 
along with why its implementation was neces­
sary for the circular transition.
Prototype 2
With the roadmap activities in place, the im­
provements implemented, and the transition 
from the low-fidelity prototype to a digital Ex­
cel Workbook completed, the support was te­
sted. The goal of iteration two was to improve 
the support based on feedback from previous 
tests and then test its usefulness. Based on the 
results and inputs in the support, it was possi­
ble to track the development of the roadmap.
The detailed test procedure can be seen in Ap­
pendix L, along with the questionnaire the test 
subjects were instructed to fill out to document 
their immediate feedback. For the testing, two 
employees from the Danish MedTech consul­
tancy firm Technolution were asked to com­
plete the support, and their tests were timed to 
document the duration it took to fill out each 
step of the tool. Both test subjects had experi­
ence with sustainability, circular economy, and 
product development, so their feedback on the 
support was invaluable to the project. The fe­
edback and observations derived from the test 
can be seen in Figure 3.8.
Test results
Based on testing feedback (Appendix L), the 
purpose of the second iteration had been fulfil­
led. In terms of the usefulness aspect of the te­
sting, both of the test subjects stated that they 
saw the potential of the framework in aiding 
MedTech companies in the circular transition. 
They found all of the steps of the support rele­
vant and did not feel like anything crucial was 
missing. However, one of the participants voi­
ced that more guidance should be given to the 
company before filling out the support. What 
was meant by that was to include when and 
who from a company would be needed to fill 
out each step and how long it would take them 
to fill out the steps. This would ensure the like­
lihood of the company filling out the support 
and mitigating potential confusion. 
Regarding usability, the scores were gathered 
for each step of the support, and it can be sta­
ted that the usability and understandability of 
the support were improved, as they were rated 
at 4,6, and 4,3 on average accordingly (on a 
scale from 1-5). The digital format made the in­
teractivity with the support easier, facilitating 
the successful development of the roadmap’s 
activities and thereby further fulfilling the ite­
ration’s purpose of tracking the roadmap’s de­
velopment. The digital format helped to tack­
le the issue of large amounts of text by using 
pop-up attributes, which assisted in reducing 
the overwhelming nature of the support. The 
overall layout of the support was deemed intu­
itive and logical, and the completion duration 
of the support’s steps was timed to take less 
than an hour, thereby fulfilling the set requi­
rement. 
The testing aspects and identified challenges 
can be seen in Figure 3.8, which would be im­
plemented in the next iteration.

Box 3.4
Purpose of Iteration 3
Improve the usability of the support ba­
sed on feedback during the second ite­
ration and test the usability and useful­
ness of the roadmap
Third iteration
75
Prescriptive Study
The testing phase of the second iteration sole­
ly focused on assessing the support. Therefo­
re, for the third iteration, the focus was shifted 
toward evaluating the roadmap aspect of the 
Excel sheet. This involved verifying the logical 
development of the roadmap and ensuring that 
the recommendations and activities provided 
were pertinent and applicable to the MedTech 
industry. Before undertaking this, the feedback 
and insights gathered during the second itera­
tion testing (Figure 3.9) were considered and 
incorporated accordingly due to their impact 
on the development of the roadmap. 
Prototype 3
For the roadmap testing, it was decided that 
the test subjects would not fill out the support 
steps due to the long duration it took in the 
previous iterations. Instead, the steps would 
be filled out in advance, and the participants 
would quickly go over the steps to understand 
the context. The test was conducted with three 
participants who had knowledge about Med­
Tech and the circular economy and were cur­
rently in the process of developing a medical 
device for CAMES. For the test, the participants 
were briefed about the case company, the 
chosen device (an ostomy bag) that the sup­
port had been filled out based on, and the fra­
mework and were instructed to act as if they 
were the team that now had to carry out the 
roadmap activities. The testing of the roadmap 
was done in a way that the participants went 
through each activity, after which they were 
provided with an evaluation sheet to assess 
the usability and usefulness of the activity. For 
more details about the testing protocol and re­
sults, see Appendix M. 
Test results
Based on the feedback from the testing, the 
iteration successfully achieved its purpose. The 
usability of the support was enhanced, and the 
roadmap received ratings of 4,7 for usability 
and 4,2 for usefulness, demonstrating its effe­
ctiveness. All participants acknowledged the 
potential of the support framework, its applica­
tion, and its relevance to the MedTech industry 
in the transition process. Although they were 
unable to practically implement the activities, 
their feedback reflected the theoretical useful­
ness of the framework. Considering that the 
actual implementation of the activities would 
span across several years, the theoretical us­
efulness was deemed sufficient to affirm that 
the framework was useful and relevant within 
its context overall, thus supporting the achie­
vement of the goal of the thesis.
The feedback on the roadmap's usability emp­
hasized the need for the option to revisit or re­
vise previous steps, as participants stressed the 
importance of traceability and transparency, al­
lowing users to see where the outputs origina­
ted from. Additionally, the support framework 
should be considered a "living document," 
enabling continuous iteration and revision af­
ter its completion in response to new insights. 
This approach would alleviate concerns about 
initial input approval and encourage explorati­
on and reiteration as users progress.
In addition to the three test subjects, guidance 
and feedback were provided by a circular eco­
nomy expert regarding the usefulness and ap­
plicability of a roadmap to MedTech companies 
during a supervision session, which brought up 
an important issue. The experts voiced that the 
business model development should either be 
excluded from the framework due to the cur­
rent scope or be further elaborated upon as it 
was closely tied to product development. The­
refore, it was chosen that the business model 
development activity should include guidance 
on how to develop a supportive circular busi­
ness case/model for the chosen design strate­
gies but not focus on the actual implementati­
on as a compromise between the two.
The full list of feedback and observations made 
during the testing can be seen in Figure 3.9.
