Figure 3.10: Structure of the Support Framework and how it ties in with the adapted Stage-gate model 
(Cooper, 2010)
Final Support Framework
77
Prescriptive Study
With the improvements implemented from 
the third iteration, the support framework 
had completed its development. The final fra­
mework and its contents will be presented in 
the next sections, and it can be accessed th­
rough Appendix N.
The support framework was created to aid 
Danish MedTech companies in transitioning 
to the circular economy, focusing on circular 
product innovation. Companies input their re­
sponses and choices into the support, and the 
roadmap functions as a result of these inputs 
by explaining how to execute and apply the re­
sults in the circular transition process. Meaning 
that the roadmap’s content was customized to 
the individual MedTech company and the de­
vice they intended to redesign. It is, however, 
important to note that the support framework 
was just that - a support. Although it provides 
guidance, companies must execute the activi­
ties themselves and reflect on the suggestions 
and recommendations related to the possibili­
ties of their specific medical device. However, 
it is suggested that companies take full advan­
tage of the support framework and push the 
limits of what they believe is feasible for their 
device design within the current setting.
Figure 3.10 depicts the final support fra­
mework's structure, which includes five sup­
port steps and eight roadmap activities. The 
figure also shows how the adapted stage-gate 
model corresponds to the developed support 
framework, where it can be seen that the fra­
mework covers all of the NPD phases, though 
in varying detail. 
Completion of the Support 
Framework
The support framework was designed to assist 
companies interested in making the switch to 
a circular economy. To guarantee its success, it 
was suggested that a team of representatives 
from various departments, including a project 
manager, product designer, business devel­
oper, sustainability lead, and R&D representa­
tive, be involved in its development. However, 
these representatives were expected to partici­
pate at different stages, as illustrated in Figure 
3.11, to ensure the framework's optimal com­
==PAGE:96==
Figure 3.11: Who to include for the different steps within the support and roadmap
78
Prescriptive Study
pletion. The project manager was a valuable 
addition to the team, as discovered during the 
iterations. They were assigned with planning, 
controlling, and task-, time-, and stakeholder 
management tasks as it was within their pur­
view. As such, they were responsible for deter­
mining which departments should be involved 
at which stages of the project and managing 
the overall project. The necessary departments 
were identified through discussions with both 
the case company and MedTech professionals.
The project manager was responsible for fa­
cilitating the actual execution and completi­
on of the support framework for the relevant 
department, ensuring a seamless and efficient 
experience for the team. Therefore, it was re­
commended that the project manager become 
familiar with the support in advance in order to 
facilitate the process more effectively.
Support
The purpose of the support was to facilitate the 
development of the roadmap through the in­
puts made by the company across five steps, 
each with its own focus. The support included 
an elaborate introduction that provided the 
MedTech company with the necessary backg­
round to understand the support framework as 
a whole and how to complete it. Both the pre­
vious phases and iterations found that this was 
necessary for users to utilize the support and 
roadmap effectively. The support steps made 
up the majority of the Discovery phase (Cooper, 
2010) of the NPD process as it investigates the 
environment, the engine, and the opportunities 
(Koen et al., 2002) possible for the circular tran­
sition, thereby laying the groundwork for the 
roadmap’s activities.
Step 1: Applicability Decision Tool
The initial step aimed to ensure the support 
framework aligned with the company's con­
text and the particular device they intended 
to focus on. This was vital in guaranteeing that 
the support framework would effectively assist 
them with their circular transition, as it mainly 
targeted non-critical and semi-critical devices 
(Spaulding, 1971) with the greatest potential for 
SUDs and excluded critical devices due to legi­
slative restrictions. To further ensure the effica­
cy of the support framework, a set of questions 
(European Commission, 2020) was used to de­
termine whether the chosen product had the 
potential to benefit from the circular redesign 
process, as the support framework was desig­
ned for the complete redesign of medical de­
vices that follow a linear lifecycle and not for 
devices that already employ circular initiatives. 
While this might have been possible in future 
iterations, it was not a priority in the current 
scope. The decision-making process, including 
==PAGE:97==
79
Prescriptive Study
these aspects, was simplified using a decisi­
on tool, making it easy for users to determine 
whether the support framework applied to their 
individual company.
Step 2: Motivation, Drivers & Barriers
Based on the research by Mendoza et al. (2017) 
and Koen et al. (2002), the second step aimed 
to aid the company in clarifying its motivation 
and assessing the primary drivers and barriers 
to achieving a circular transition. This process 
enabled the company to identify its reasons for 
pursuing circularity, its desired outcomes, po­
tential obstacles, and strategies for overcoming 
them. Ultimately, this step was included to faci­
litate effective communication with other stake­
holders and management later on, ensuring the 
successful implementation of the circular tran­
sition. This step thereby assisted in developing 
the overarching goal and other crucial factors 
that were part of the Discovery phase to assist 
in the development of the business model.
Step 3: Circularity Assessment of 
Current Medical Device
The third step was included to assess the cho­
sen medical device’s circularity performance 
and thereby identify areas with the greatest 
potential for improvement in circular product 
innovation. This was done by adapting the 
Circular Product Design Maturity Matrix devel­
oped by Aguiar and Jugend (2022) to align with 
the framework’s scope. The adaptation process 
entailed removing, adding, and rephrasing mul­
tiple dimensions of the tool, resulting in eight 
highly relevant dimensions centered on the de­
sign process and circular product design. Users 
would then use the tool to rate the device’s 
performance across each of these eight dimen­
sions. Then, based on the inputs, the support 
would visualize the results and provide insights 
on areas requiring improvement and where the 
company should focus when selecting circular 
design strategies for the new circular solution, 
which was the next step of the support process.
Step 4: Circular Product Design 
The fourth step aimed to aid MedTech compa­
nies in choosing circular design strategies based 
on their device’s criticality classification (Spaul­
ding, 1971), the waste hierarchy, and their own 
preferences. This particular step had undergone 
numerous changes across its iterations due to 
its inherent complexity. It includes a total of 14 
design strategies that are categorized into three 
distinct categories focusing on: Long Life Pro­
ducts, Life-Extension, and Recycling. The first 
two categories focused on slowing down the 
resource loop (Bocken et al., 2016) and promo­
ting eco-efficiency (McDonough and Braungart, 
2002), which involved postponing, resisting, 
and reversing obsolescence (den Hollander, 
Bakker, Hultink 2017). The third category con­
centrated on closing the resource loop (Bocken 
et al., 2016) and promoting eco-effectiveness 
(Braungart, McDonough, and Bollinger, 2007). 
As discovered in DS-I, circular product design 
encompasses eco-efficiency and eco-effecti­
veness strategies. Therefore, Kane, Bakker, and 
Balkenende’s (2018) matrix was adapted to sug­
gest not just one strategy based on the criticality 
of the device but both a strategy for life-exten­
sion (eco-efficiency) and a strategy for EoL re­
cycling (eco-effectiveness) - since Kane, Bakker, 
and Balkenende's (2018) definition implies that 
value includes more than just economic worth. 
The outcomes of this step significantly impacted 
the activities in the roadmap. This was logical 
since it had been found through DS-I that the 
design decisions for the device affected the en­
tire system through the employment of LCT and 
due to the aspects’ inherent correlation with 
each other. With this step complete, the com­
panies should have an idea of how they intend 
to propose and deliver value to their customers, 
thereby further assisting in the creation of the 
business model.
Step 5: Collaborative Stakeholder 
Network
The final step of the support involved identi­
fying and engaging key stakeholders needed 
to transition to a circular model successfully. 
Through the iterations, it was determined that 
this step should be focused solely on the pro­
ject manager due to their role and expertise 
in this area. In this step, the project mana­
ger would compile a list of individuals across 
multiple departments within the organization 
(Mendoza et al., 2017) who would be neces­
sary for the transition's success. Additionally, 
the step included considerations on whether 
==PAGE:98==
Figure 3.12: Connections between inputs from the support steps and the results in the roadmap activities
80
Prescriptive Study
to keep value recovery and maintenance and 
repair services in-house or if they should be 
outsourced, which was found to be the most 
common choice due to responsibility and risk 
(UNEP, 2007). Lastly, a comprehensive commu­
nication plan was developed for all stakehol­
ders, including those within the value chain 
and ecosystem. What set the circular engage­
ment plan apart from a traditional one was the 
level of engagement required. For the circular 
type, there was no one-way communication, 
and the frequency and type of involvement of 
the stakeholders were more engaging and in­
volving to ensure buy-in and support toward 
the circular transition and its initiatives. This 
step was included in the roadmap to ensure 
that the communication plan was followed th­
roughout the transition process and that the 
stakeholder’s needs were met.
Result - Roadmap
Based on the inputs from the support’s five 
steps, the company would have a roadmap 
consisting of eight activities with actionable 
tasks. An overview of which steps’ inputs ties 
with which activity can be seen in Figure 3.12.
The purpose of the roadmap was to assist Med­
Tech companies and give them an overview of 
the necessary actions for a successful circular 
transition and product development. The visu­
al representation of the activities of the road­
map can be seen in Figure 3.13.
Activity 1: Concept development 
The first activity of the roadmap focused on the 
Discovery stage of NPD, with emphasis on the 
conceptualization of the device. The purpose 
of this activity was for the company to create a 
concept for their redesigned device that would 
be driven by the chosen design strategies. Mo­
reover, a company would need to consider 
mandatory design strategies, which were sup­
porting strategies required to ensure the suc­
cessful implementation of the chosen strategi­
es from step four. An example of such a case 
would be if a company chose a life-extension 
strategy such as remanufacturing, mandatory 
strategies such as design for dis- and reassem­
bly would follow. Moreover, the company was 
prompted with ways of addressing the sugge­
sted strategies to know what should be con­
sidered in the design process. Based on the 
==PAGE:99==
Figure 3.13: Final roadmap representation with a total of eight activities within four lanes
81
Prescriptive Study
medical device’s criticality (Spaulding, 1971), 
the company was provided with the required 
type of decontamination process to allow for 
life extension and recycling strategies. This was 
deemed crucial in the concept development 
process as to ensure that the initiatives were 
possible to implement, the design itself must 
be able to withstand the required decontami­
nation and value recovery processes.  
Activity 2: Top Management Support
The purpose of the second activity was to com­
municate the solution to top management, 
which was a part of the Discovery and Scoping 
stages of the NPD process. In this activity, the 
company was given a summary of the chosen 
device, the design focus, design strategies, and 
potential barriers. The overview was given to 
the project manager, mainly, to present the new 
circular solution and all its aspects to top mana­
gement. Communication with top management 
would ensure their support, alignment of goals 
and objectives, securing resources, mitigating 
risks, and, most importantly, their commitment 
to the circular economy transition.
Activity 3: Project Definition
With support from top management, the next 
activity focused on defining the project, fol­
lowing the Scoping stage of the NPD. This ac­
tivity consisted of defining and scoping the 
project, planning the roadmap’s activities, and 
determining the project’s team members to 
drive the transition (Mendoza et al., 2017),. The 
output of this activity was for a company to 
have a detailed and finished project plan for 
the circular transition according to their speci­
fic scope and goal(s). 
Activity 4: Consideration of Policy­
makers
The last activity in the scoping stage focused 
on policymakers' inclusion. Policymakers are an 
essential element that sets and guides existing 
regulations regarding medical devices. There 
is a lack of two-way communication between 
MedTech companies and policymakers, along 
with no national guidelines concerning low-va­
lue medical devices (Gantzhorn and Bjerrum, 
2021). 
Communication 
with 
policymakers 
could help MedTech companies in the transiti­
on by staying ahead, informed and influencing 
the regulatory background concerning medical 
devices (Eisenreich et al., 2022; Stepniewska, 
2024). Thus, the goal of the activity was to en­
courage companies to reflect upon previously 
inputted regulatory conditions that might hin­
der the transition process and to include po­
==PAGE:100==
82
Prescriptive Study
licymakers and regulators in the stakeholder 
network to mitigate potential barriers.
Activity 5: Business Model Devel­
opment 
For the development of a solution, a suppor­
ting business model is crucial to ensure the 
success of it (Bocken et al., 2016). As menti­
oned previously, the Business case develop­
ment stage of the NPD process was outside the 
scope of this thesis. However, it was important 
to underline to MedTech companies its impor­
tance in relation to the product development 
process. Therefore, in this activity, the Circular 
Economy Business Model Framework (Pieroni 
et al. 2020) was adopted, along with require­
ments to consider when developing a suppor­
ting business model based on the developed 
concept in activity one and its circular design 
strategies. The business model framework faci­
litated the development and elaboration of a 
circular business model by considering recom­
mendations on how to structure combinations 
of offerings of products and services, revenue 
streams, value delivery and creation processes, 
key partnerships, and their advantages. 
Activity 6: Establishing a Collabo­
rative Stakeholder Network
Activity six emphasized establishing a stakehol­
der network and restructuring the current value 
chain of the MedTech companies. As many Med­
Tech companies opt for many suppliers, it crea­
tes a problem of reduced market power, which 
results in low bargaining power (UNEP, 2007; 
Hazen et al., 2021). All stakeholders should be 
involved and engaged in the process from the 
onset (Jeffery, 2009; Hofmann and Jaeger-Erban, 
2020), thus this activity lets companies reflect 
on their current value chain and the developed 
engagement plan. The implementation of the 
engagement plan would plan out various types 
of communication, partnerships, and relations 
with collaboration, support, and co-develop­
ment opportunities from the stakeholders to 
ensure the viability of the circular initiatives.
Activity 7: System development
The following sub-activities focused on the De­
velopment stage of the NPD process. They focus 
on the product development stage, by including 
relevant stakeholders throughout the process to 
ensure support, engagement, and co-develop­
ment opportunities with primary users and the 
value network. Moreover, the activities across 
the lanes would happen concurrently, meaning 
that product development would occur simulta­
neously with the establishment of an RL system.
Through this activity, constant communication 
with relevant value chain partners and ecosy­
stem stakeholders was needed. By involving 
stakeholders, valuable insights could be gained, 
along with the identification of new opportuni­
ties and further strengthening the company’s 
relation within the upstream and downstream 
value chain (Jeffery, 2009).
Product development and testing. The 
sub-activity of product development focused 
on detailing, testing, prototyping and iterating 
on the developed concept, where it was under­
lined that a company should ensure alignment 
with the developed circular business model in 
order for successful and seamless integration. 
The product development process is an iterati­
ve process where the product is adapted based 
on stakeholder feedback and insights (Cooper, 
2010). Product prototypes can give a company 
insights into usability and user needs before 
full-scale production (Ries, 2011). This can be 
achieved by involving relevant stakeholders to 
ensure that users would show interest in the 
“new product” (Hofmann and Jaeger-Erban, 
2020). For MedTech companies, the involve­
ment of the main users of their devices, such 
as HCPs and patients, would be done during 
this activity through testing sessions. However, 
the testing part of this activity focused sole­
ly on suggestions following the Lean Startup 
approach (Blank, 2013), as companies already 
have established ways of testing and prototy­
ping their devices, as discovered through the 
iterations. The output of this sub-activity would 
be a detailed device that was usable, user-fri­
endly, and where the chosen and mandatory 
circular design strategies were integrated. 
Establish reverse logistics and necessary sta­
keholders. For the design of RL, a framework by 
Mallick et al. (2023) was provided for the com­
panies to utilize in the roadmap. The need for RL 
was deemed important when a company wants 
==PAGE:101==
83
Prescriptive Study
to recover value at the EoL of their devices. This 
activity focused on the actual design of the RL 
system, and to ensure its effectiveness, which 
included incentives, as they function as the dri­
vers for users to actually return the product at 
EoU. For MedTech companies, only two types 
were included: “Information and Awareness” 
and “Convenience” (Shumon, Ahmed, and Ah­
med, 2016), due to the Danish regulatory back­
ground of economic incentives. After designing 
the RL system, the next step of the activity was 
to actually develop the system by establishing 
new collaborations with needed stakeholders 
as well as the primary users of the device. 
With the product developed and its supporting 
systems designed and implemented, the com­
pany was prompted to assess its new circular 
solution against the baseline device using an 
adapted CAT framework (Shahbazi et al., 2020).
Activity 8: Implementation
The last activity was the implementation pha­
se following the Testing and Validation, and 
Launch stages of the NPD process. Conducting 
pilot studies is important to mitigate risks and 
identify potential improvements before mo­
ving on to production (Pearson et al., 2020). 
Therefore, activity eight focused on first plan­
ning and carrying out a small-scale pilot, where 
emphasis was put on the newly established RL 
system due to its complexity. Pilot projects can 
be repeated based on feedback from the sur­
rounding system, which should be considered 
before scaling up (Beets et al., 2021). Similarly 
to the business model activity, activity eight in­
cluded suggestions and reflections rather than 
actionable tasks based on inputs from support’s 
steps. This was done as companies have already 
established ways they want to handle integrati­
on and scaling processes. Nevertheless, this ac­
tivity would result in a system that was ready for 
a successful full-scale implementation. 
==PAGE:102==
Evaluation plan
Figure 3.14: Completion of the support framework. The colored steps/activities are to be completed by a team 
of individual departments, while the white steps/activities are mainly for the project manager
The support framework was intended to assist 
MedTech companies in transitioning toward 
circularity; however, its effectiveness remained 
uncertain. To clarify this, in DS-II, an evaluation 
of the framework’s usefulness and relevance 
was conducted with the case company to en­
sure its usefulness for the industry. The evaluati­
on process is explained in the following. 
Ideally, representatives from multiple depart­
ments within the same business area would 
complete the support together as a team. This 
approach would provide a wider range of know­
ledge and expertise. Alternatively, as it had been 
found through previous communication with 
the case company that this approach was not 
feasible, the evaluation would involve individual 
sessions with the participants to ensure a com­
prehensive assessment and feedback from mul­
tiple departments, preferably in person. 
For the evaluation, the focus was on the useful­
ness and usability of the framework as a whole. 
Participants were asked to provide feedback on 
the relevance and usefulness of the framework 
to their company and whether the steps and 
activities were actionable enough for them to 
successfully carry them out and gain value from 
them. Usability was evaluated based on how 
easy it was for participants to use and navigate 
the framework, understand the steps required to 
complete it, and whether the participants could 
complete the support framework independently. 
However, as seen in Figure 3.14, it was not possi­
ble to fully assess the usefulness of the roadmap 
due to the lengthy process involved in bringing 
a medical device from concept to approval, as 
studies have shown that this process takes from 
three to seven years to complete (TWI, 2020). 
Therefore, participants were asked to fill out the 
support as intended, which was estimated to 
take around an hour, and were then asked to re­
flect on the usefulness of the roadmap without 
actually completing it. The feedback from par­
ticipants was documented through a survey, 
which included questions about the usefulness 
and usability of the entire support framework.
Based on the evaluation provided by the case 
company, it would be able to determine how 
well the requirements and goal of the support 
framework had been fulfilled and, thereby, 
whether it solved the identified gaps relating to 
the circular transition.
84
Prescriptive Study
==PAGE:103==
Discussion of Prescriptive Study
85
Prescriptive Study
The final version of the support framework 
would, in theory, assist MedTech companies in 
their transition toward the circular economy 
by facilitating the decision process regarding 
circular product design. It would include all the 
necessary factors and aspects to consider to 
make the transition feasible and, finally, a road­
map on how to accomplish it. However, whether 
this was also the case in practice was unknown. 
The Usefulness of the Roadmap
Although the support framework had under­
gone three iterations, it was still unclear how 
relevant and useful the roadmap was for the 
MedTech industry due to various factors. The 
first factor was the lack of access to experts 
within the MedTech industry. It became evident 
after the first iteration that the specificity and 
customization of the MedTech industry made 
it impossible for people outside the industry 
to effectively utilize the framework. However, 
identifying experts outside of the case com­
pany proved to be difficult. 
The second factor concerned the sample si­
zes of the iterations. For the second iteration, 
experts were identified, but as experts were 
hard to come by, the sample size was automa­
tically small. During this iteration, participants 
provided valuable feedback. However, due to 
the amount of feedback on the support, no 
time was spent on providing feedback on the 
roadmap, and the usefulness of the roadmap 
thereby relied solely on the feedback from the 
third iteration. To save time for the third itera­
tion, the support was filled in advance, so the 
focus remained only on the roadmap. Howe­
ver, this meant that the first time the support 
framework would be tested all at once would 
be with the case company for the evaluation, 
which was not optimal, and an iteration after 
the evaluation might have been necessary. 
However, due to time limitations, this was not 
possible within the timeframe of this thesis. 
Thereby, there were some uncertainties rela­
ting to the usefulness of the developed fra­
mework for the industry, which could only be 
evaluated during DS-II with the case company.
Another issue with the roadmap was its time­
frame. As the process from concept to product 
approval can take around seven years, it was 
doubtful whether MedTech companies would 
utilize the recommendations and results pre­
sented in the roadmap during the transiti­
on journey. There was a risk that the support 
framework would be forgotten due to the 
time passed or the natural turnover of peop­
le within the organization. To address this is­
sue, the third activity in the roadmap was the 
project definition, where the project manager 
developed a project plan that incorporated all 
the activities proposed in the roadmap, along 
with their tasks and considerations, which 
could then follow the project and its members 
throughout its duration.
==PAGE:104==
Conclusion
86
Prescriptive Study
The goal of the completed Prescriptive Study 
phase was to develop a support framework 
that could assist the Danish MedTech industry 
in creating a customized roadmap with actio­
nable activities. This was achieved by synthe­
sizing the findings from previous phases into 
a support framework. Companies input their 
reflections and decisions into this framework 
through a five-step support, providing them 
with a roadmap with eight actionable activiti­
es. These activities showcase the outputs from 
the support and guide companies on how and 
in what order to target them in the product 
development process. 
The support framework was tested through 
three iterations, with both people outside the 
MedTech industry and experts within it provi­
ding valuable insights and feedback that were 
analyzed and implemented into the framework. 
The final version was an interactive Excel Work­
book that Danish MedTech companies could 
use independently to support their circular 
transition, focusing on circular product rede­
sign. It also included other crucial aspects to 
support the transition, including the establis­
hment of a collaborative stakeholder network 
and the design of an RL system. These were 
identified as necessary in the previous phase to 
support circular initiatives simultaneously. 
The developed support framework filled the 
identified gaps in driving and facilitating the 
circular transition by including all the neces­
sary factors and considerations while also 
being industry-specific. Moreover, it allowed 
for customization for individual companies, 
providing a comprehensive solution to support 
the circular transition for the Danish MedTech 
industry and its companies through the road­
map’s actionable activities.

87
Prescriptive Study

==PAGE:107==
The fourth and final phase of the Design Research Methodology was 
Descriptive Study II. The focus for this phase was to evaluate the use­
fulness and usability of the framework as a whole and thereby assess 
whether the framework fulfilled its overarching purpose of facilitating 
the circular transition for the Danish MedTech industry. This encom­
passed the application and evaluation of the developed support fra­
mework on the case company using the evaluation plan developed 
through the Prescriptive Study phase. Based on the evaluation and 
feedback, the support framework was reflected upon in terms of its 
limitations, potential suggestions for improvements, and its fulfillment 
of the requirements stated in Descriptive Study I. Thereby, the phase 
addressed the second research question:
RQ2: How useful is the developed support framework in supporting 
the transition towards the circular economy, and what can be done 
to ensure its success?
The phase revolved around the following sections:
•	
Application of the developed support framework
•	
Evaluation of the framework 
•	
Discussion of the framework
Descriptive Study II
89
Descriptive Study II
==PAGE:108==
Application of the Support Framework 
90
Descriptive Study II
To follow the evaluation plan developed in 
Prescriptive Study, emails were sent out across 
the case company’s organization to the de­
partments necessary for the successful com­
pletion of the support framework. In total, 16 
representatives were contacted and invited 
to participate in the framework evaluation. As 
no responses were received within the speci­
fied timeframe, a revised evaluation plan was 
proposed. Instead of having a team complete 
the framework in collaboration, the alternative 
evaluation protocol was employed, where the 
framework was completed individually by the 
company representatives. However, this did 
not reap any results, and the plan needed to be 
revised again. Representatives from the com­
pany saw it as too resource- and time-con­
suming to complete the framework, as it was 
estimated to take a maximum of three hours 
to complete and provide feedback - also when 
offered the opportunity to split the evaluati­
on into smaller sessions. Therefore, to accom­
modate this and ensure that feedback was col­
lected for the evaluation, the framework was 
emailed with an elaborate step-by-step guide 
on how to complete it and evaluate it through 
an attached evaluation survey focusing on its 
usefulness, relevance, and usability.
Responses. Within the set timeframe, only 
three representatives responded. One com­
pleted the framework independently, filled 
out the evaluation sheet, and emailed it back 
along with the filled-in framework, whereas 
the other two completed it in person during 
two separate individual meetings. Despite the 
lack of response from the case company, it was 
still achieved to have at least one representa­
tive from each of the most necessary depart­
ments within the company when considering 
the scope of the framework. This included re­
presentatives within sustainability, lifecycle 
management who had knowledge about the 
stakeholder landscape, and someone from de­
sign, all within the ostomy care business area.
However, the fact that the representatives 
completed the framework independently and 
not as a team as recommended in the original 
evaluation plan had consequences. It was clear 
from the onset that there were aspects of the 
framework that the individual representative 
could not complete due to their specialization 
within one area. This meant that, for examp­
le, in aspects that the lifecycle management 
representative was an expert in, the designer 
had difficulty completing, while in areas about 
circularity and sustainability concepts, the life­
cycle management representative faced issues 
where both the designer and the sustainability 
representative were experts. To mitigate this 
for the evaluation process, the development 
team substituted the missing representatives 
to allow for the effective completion of the fra­
mework despite the identified obstacles. How­
ever, this was not possible for the evaluation 
over email, which resulted in frustrations that 
became visible in the evaluation survey and 
scores, as it was not possible for one person to 
have knowledge of all the aspects covered by 
the framework, which resulted in a low score 
relating to usability (Appendix O).
Ostomy Care Application
Due to the confidential nature of the inputs 
within the support framework, specific details 
will not be disclosed. Instead, a more general 
summary of the results related to the trends 
and focus from the three evaluations will be 
provided.
In the completed assessments, there was a 
consistent recognition of why the case com­
pany would be drawn to the circular transi­
tion. Each evaluator acknowledged the en­
vironmental impact of the company’s SUDs, 
its resource consumption, and the volume of 
waste generated. They all recognized the po­
tential of circularity as a means to address the­
se issues, particularly through recycling and 
the utilization of recycled materials, as well as 
a shift away from SUDs towards more circular, 
reusable solutions.
The responses from the representatives re­
garding the factors influencing the transition 
were consistent with the findings in DS-I. They 
all emphasized the necessity for significant or­
ganizational and business model changes, as 
well as the importance of stakeholder support 
for the transition through value recovery pro­
cesses and services. However, they expressed 
doubts about the feasibility of the latter due 
==PAGE:109==
91
Descriptive Study II
to the absence of current specialized provi­
ders focusing on medical-grade equipment, 
reprocessing, and recycling of them. They did 
acknowledge the potential for these to be­
come driving forces through innovation, col­
laboration, and partnerships, aligning with the 
findings from DS-I. Additionally, the represen­
tatives underscored recycling as a circular de­
sign strategy, deeming it the most viable opti­
on within the current system and the circular 
change with the least disruption to the current 
business model. They noted that this appro­
ach would not require new services during the 
use phase or a new revenue stream but rather 
necessitate the establishment of partnerships 
with a value recovery company to collect, re­
process, and recycle the devices, which, how­
ever, was considered a barrier, as the current 
lack thereof together with current legislation 
might hinder its feasibility. Nevertheless, all 
three representatives opted for this option. 
Furthermore, none of the representatives con­
sidered the design strategy recommendations 
based on the ostomy device’s semi-critical 
classification nor the suggestion to adopt both 
an EoU and an EoL strategy, citing the feasibi­
lity within the current business model as the 
primary reason. Overall, the representatives 
tended to focus on what was presently feasible 
within the existing business model rather than 
exploring its potential due to the current legi­
slative landscape and lack of suited providers 
to support the circular initiatives. This appro­
ach thereby limited the possibility of achieving 
a truly circular product design incorporating 
both eco-efficiency and eco-effectiveness prin­
ciples and fully benefiting from the actionable 
activities outlined in the roadmap, so for the 
sake of the evaluation, the two representatives 
completing it in person were also prompted to 
choose a slowing the loop strategy, to evaluate 
the reach of the framework fully.

92
Descriptive Study II
Evaluation
As outlined in the evaluation plan, the assess­
ment of the final support framework (Appendix 
N) was documented through a survey contai­
ning questions related to its usefulness and 
usability. In practical terms, this involved pro­
viding representatives with a survey to evaluate 
the overall usefulness, relevance, and usability 
of the support, as well as to offer feedback on 
potential areas for improvement after they had 
completed the support process. Subsequently, 
they proceeded through the roadmap without 
filling it out or carrying out the activities. Then, 
they were presented with a similar survey to 
prompt reflection on the potential usefulness 
and relevance of the activities to their com­
pany, as well as the usability of the roadmap 
itself. Finally, they were asked to evaluate the 
framework as a whole to determine its potential 
to assist their company in transitioning towards 
circularity, thus aligning with the objectives of 
the thesis. The full evaluation and protocol can 
be accessed through Appendix O.
Support
All three assessors rated the support's useful­
ness, relevance, and usability on a scale from 
1 to 5 (5 being the best), and the findings are 
depicted in Figure 4.1. Considering these ratings, 
the support received a marginally above-avera­
ge overall score of 3,6. The following sections 
will explain the rationale behind the scores pre­
sented in Figure 4.1.
Support - usefulness. Based on the tests, it was 
determined that assessing the usefulness of the 
support did not align with its intended purpose. 
The support was designed to gather essential 
information for the roadmap rather than to be 
directly useful or applicable on its own. It was 
intended to streamline the process of obtaining 
input from the company. This could also be seen 
in the scores in relation to usefulness, as the 
representatives had a hard time determining 
whether the steps in themselves were, in fact, 
useful and felt that some aspects were missing. 
This included the considerations for contami­
nation and the need for sterilizations and/or 
decontamination processes to return the de­
vice to a usable state, which was covered in the 
roadmap itself. Nevertheless, the feedback was 
==PAGE:111==
Figure 4.2: Roadmap evaluation. Test subject 1: Design, Test subject 2: Sus­
tainability, and Test subject 3: Stakeholder knowledge
93
Descriptive Study II
valid as the circular design strategies required 
reprocessing after use, whether it was to extend 
to new use cycles or if the device was inten­
ded to be recycled, it would need to undergo 
this process, which was currently missing in the 
support, but addressed in the roadmap. Howe­
ver, as stated, the usefulness of the support was 
deemed invalid due to its main purpose.
In terms of relevance, they all found the aspects 
covered by the support relevant to the MedTech 
industry and the circular transition. The relevan­
ce of the individual steps differed for the repre­
sentatives due to their individual backgrounds 
and expertise. Nevertheless, they did not feel 
anything was missing from the support.
Support - usability. The two representatives 
who completed the support in person rated 
the usability highly in terms of both the ease 
of filling it out and navigating within and across 
steps. They only faced smaller issues relating 
to the phrasing of some of the aspects or con­
cepts that naturally lay beyond their expertise, 
which could potentially have been mitigated if 
the evaluation was completed as a team, as the 
framework was intended, or if the framework 
developers had been better at stepping in and 
assisting with explanations and detailing of the 
concepts. However, the representative, who 
completed it independently over email, did not 
have the same experience and rated it low. Ba­
sed on the returned Excel sheet, they seemed to 
have given up completing it halfway through, 
which may be one of the reasons for their expe­
rience, as the support only functions if filled in 
correctly. Another reason could be found th­
rough their feedback relating to usability. They 
felt the support was not intended for individual 
completion due to the complexities that ranged 
beyond their expertise, which were the primary 
reason for the low rating. This feedback valida­
ted the development team’s concern relating to 
the same issue and the main reason for the need 
to complete the support as a team, as explained 
at the beginning of the phase. However, as sta­
ted previously, this option was not possible due 
to scheduling and availability issues, and it was 
deemed that having some feedback was better 
than having none.
Roadmap
Moving on to the most important part of the 
framework, the roadmap, the scores from the 
evaluation survey can be seen in Figure 4.2 for 
all three evaluators. Here, it can be seen that 
the case company representatives were overall 
pleased with the roadmap and its activities and 
that across all three aspects, the roadmap was 
rated an average of 4, indicating only smaller 
improvements. 
In terms of the evaluation sheet for the road­
map, questions inquiring about which aspects 
the representatives found the most and least 
==PAGE:112==
94
Descriptive Study II
relevant within the roadmap were included. This 
was done to make the participants further refle­
ct on the usefulness and relevance of the road­
map and its activities to ensure the validity of 
the scores. The evaluation's feedback in relation 
to usefulness and usability will be discussed in 
the following sections.
Roadmap - usefulness. As stated, the overall 
usefulness and relevance of the roadmap and 
its activities were rated high. They especial­
ly appreciated the visualized roadmap, which 
provided an overview of all the necessary ac­
tivities and the order in which they must ad­
dress them to transition toward circularity. 
Moreover, they emphasized the benefits of 
the roadmap’s lanes, which assisted them in 
understanding who and which departments 
should be responsible for which activities. They 
also liked the inclusion of the concurrent en­
gineering approach, where multiple activities 
coincide through cross-organizational colla­
boration. Something they have learned th­
rough their experience when working within 
MedTech, being the most efficient approach to 
ensure alignment across teams to reduce the 
risks of having to start over due to misalign­
ment issues. And based on the contents within 
the activities they also understood why such 
an approach was necessary for the circular 
transition, as the circular transition requires 
a complete reorganization of how a company 
functions and does business. 
They all liked the level of detail, tangibility, and 
actionability of the activities and their corre­
lations. This extended across from the support 
as one representative stated: “[...] [the support] 
helped in what to focus on first, then moved 
onto what is the company's direction/strategy 
and later how to get there [roadmap].” How­
ever, another representative stated that they 
lacked more actionability for how to get star­
ted with the roadmap and what specific steps 
the company would need to go through in re­
lation to the first activity on how to practicali­
ty go about the actual redesign of the chosen 
medical device. 
Even though they all found the roadmap over­
all all-encompassing and the activities hig­
hly relevant, they also found the sheer num­
ber of activities too high and overwhelming. 
They could see the necessity for all of them, 
but they also thought this roadmap would be 
completely overwhelming and too complex to 
initiate and carry out for less educated com­
panies regarding sustainability and circularity 
because of the number of new concepts and 
aspects. Even in the case of a company whe­
re they are educated about both, the roadmap 
was also deemed extensive. They all mentio­
ned that they did not think it was feasible for 
one project manager to control this entire un­
dertaking because of its breadth across multi­
ple departments and focus areas. Therefore, it 
could be suggested that the roadmap should 
be considered a program rather than a project, 
with individual project managers within each 
department to ensure the completion of the 
activities within each lane of the roadmap and 
a program manager to oversee the entire tran­
sition across projects.
Roadmap - usability. The feedback on the usa­
bility of the roadmap was generally positive, 
with only minor issues reported by the repre­
sentatives. Some technical difficulties with the 
roadmap and its interlinks with the support 
steps were encountered, along with unfamiliari­
ty with certain concepts, methods, and theories. 
However, it was noted that these aspects would 
be clarified and guided by a facilitator or proje­
ct manager during the implementation of the 
roadmap, effectively mitigating these concerns.
Support framework
The assessment of the support framework was 
generally positive, and it received high sco­
res for its industry relevance and usefulness. 
To provide an overview of the synthesized 
findings from the evaluation of the support 
framework, they have been categorized into 
strengths and opportunities. Strengths indi­
cated what the support framework was doing 
successfully, and opportunities were aspects of 
the framework that were not as well executed 
or missing.
Strengths. As mentioned, the representati­
ves acknowledged the framework's potential, 
particularly the comprehensive and actionable 
nature of the detailed roadmap. They viewed 
==PAGE:113==
Box 4.2
Strengths
•	
Provides a great overview of the 
order and activities needed for the 
circular transition process
•	
Outlines the necessary factors and 
considerations necessary for the 
circular transition
•	
Comprehensive detailed and actio­
nable activities
•	
Useful and relevant for the industry
•

•

95
Descriptive Study II
the roadmap as valuable for facilitating the 
industry's shift to a circular model, as it out­
lined all the necessary activities, factors, and 
considerations for the transition, along with 
their interconnections, concurrencies, and 
order - something they lacked previously. As 
one representative pointed out: “Companies 
know what they want to achieve, but not how 
to achieve it,” and this framework addressed 
that issue with its detailed, actionable activi­
ties covering all the necessary factors for the 
transition, including circular product design, 
circular business model development, design 
of RL, and the establishment of a collaborati­
ve stakeholder network involving the required 
value recovery companies to sustain the initi­
atives. This support framework served as the 
initial step in the right direction as it outlines 
and explains all the necessary steps for com­
panies. However, the actual implementation 
and execution would be the responsibility of 
the companies themselves, which was found 
to happen only if regulations or laws were en­
forced in the area, effectively accelerating the 
process. In this regard, companies would need 
guidance through the process, which the sup­
port framework would assist with (Appendix 
O). Furthermore, the evaluation indicated the 
potential for broader application across the in­
dustry, extending beyond just SUDs to devices 
already employing circular strategies. To make 
this feasible, however, the framework would 
need to be modified to function as an impro­
vement tool rather than a tool for redesign. Ba­
sed on the feedback from the evaluation, the 
representatives found this to be achievable.
Opportunities. The primary challenge for the 
companies was the actual implementation 
process. Despite having a support framework 
in place to make the process more tangib­
le and actionable, the companies would still 
struggle with initiating the process. The com­
plexity of the circular transition, as outlined in 
the visualized roadmap, surprised the repre­
sentatives. They expressed concerns that the 
transition might be too much to undertake 
without regulatory or financial incentives. This 
indicates that while the support framework 
was considered useful and relevant for the 
industry, its actual implementation might not 
occur without external motivation. Even with 
the push from the ecosystem, the MedTech 
companies themselves would need to under­
take multiple concurrent projects with each an 
individual project manager and team, overseen 
by a main program manager, to implement the 
proposed activities. This would require signifi­
cant company investment to make this possi­
ble and carry it out effectively. The investment 
aspect from top management was also tied to 
concerns regarding the feasibility of the requi­
red reorganization for the value network (Ap­
pendix O). This reorganization would extend 
beyond just one product redesign and impact 
the entire supplier network across business 
areas. Therefore, if a company chose to em­
bark on the circular transition journey, it would 
likely need to simultaneously transform all its 
business areas towards a circular approach to 
build up the bargaining power and willingness 
to allow for the value network's feasibility. The 
likelihood of this happening for a larger esta­
blished MedTech company was deemed mini­
mal, at least without an incentive or push.
The evaluation revealed that the greatest draw­
back or opportunity for the support framework 
was that it wanted too much. The framework 
focuses on circular product design, but it also 
==PAGE:114==
96
Descriptive Study II
includes circular business model develop­
ment and a collaborative stakeholder network, 
which contributes to its complex and over­
whelming nature (Appendix O). Even though 
the representatives understood the reason be­
hind why these aspects were included and ne­
cessary for the circular transition due to their 
interconnectedness, they thought they could 
have been reduced, so the focus remained 
on the framework’s main qualities. The repre­
sentatives believed that what the framework 
covered extended beyond what their product 
development department could do and that 
these aspects, therefore, might not be needed. 
One believed that the business model devel­
opment activity was irrelevant as most larger 
companies already have tools that they pre­
fer to use, and therefore, this should not have 
been included. Another had a similar com­
ment, as larger companies would prefer just to 
follow their existing business model. However, 
when they realized this was not possible due 
to the new services, partners, and new type of 
revenue stream that was needed for the new 
circular initiative, they saw the potential of the 
circular business model activity. They realized 
it also included aspects that a traditional line­
ar economy business model did not currently 
contain - thereby contradicting the first repre­
sentative’s statement. Concerning the activiti­
es of the stakeholders, one representative be­
lieved that this aspect had nothing to do with 
product development and should be left out. 
However, another representative saw this as a 
great new perspective and a new and better 
way of interacting with stakeholders, especial­
ly within the ecosystem with which they cur­
rently have very little contact. However, as all 
three representatives touched upon the cum­
bersome nature of the framework and its wide 
scope in various ways, the feedback was noted 
and needed to be further investigated regar­
ding how to improve it based on the evaluati­
on in the Epilogue.

97
Descriptive Study II
In the following section, an investigation will be 
conducted regarding the fulfillment of the de­
veloped support framework against the esta­
blished requirements from DS-I. Moreover, a 
discussion will cover the research question for 
DS-II along with the goal requirement to as­
sess the success of the developed support fra­
mework. Then, the limitations found within the 
framework will be discussed. Lastly, based on 
the evaluation’s feedback and observations, the 
sensitivity, drawbacks, and suggestions for im­
proving the framework will be reviewed.
Fulfillment of the Requirements 
The success of the support framework devel­
opment was evaluated against the established 
requirements from DS-I to assess its success. 
These requirements not only helped assess the 
support framework's success rate but also pro­
vided guidance during its development in PS. 
Due to the detailed nature of the requirements, 
they provided structure in the development of 
the support framework. 
In the following sections, requirements from B 
to J will be discussed in terms of their fulfil­
lment, and Table 4.1 provides an overview of 
their fulfillment. Requirement A and whether it 
has been fulfilled will be addressed later in the 
Discussion section. 
Applicability. Given the focus of the target 
group for the framework, it was essential to 
ensure that it was utilized following its inten­
ded purpose. Thus, to ensure the companies fil­
ling out the framework were within the target 
group, a decision tree was implemented (b1). 
The decision tree acted as a guidance and refle­
ction tool to ensure that appropriate companies 
would apply the framework (B1). 
Stakeholders. As found in RC and DS-I, a colla­
borative stakeholder network must be establis­
hed for a company to successfully transition to 
the circular economy. The support framework 
addressed this by providing the company with 
a way of engaging and communicating with re­
levant stakeholders (C1). This would ensure that 
a company progresses towards establishing a 
collaborative stakeholder network by providing 
specifics on the individuals involved, the met­
hods used, and the communication frequency. 
Moreover, one of the activities within the road­
map underlines the action to include top mana­
gement in the process (c1) to gain their support 
in the transition towards the circular economy. 
Support Framework and Roadmap. As the fra­
mework's output, the company was given a vi­
sualized roadmap with activities that followed 
the NPD process, with actionable activities and 
extensive explanations on how to carry them 
out (D1, E1, and E2). However, the visual repre­
sentation of the roadmap did not change based 
on the company’s inputs, meaning the nice-to-
have requirement concerning this aspect (e1) 
was not fulfilled. The aspect was not implemen­
ted because the roadmap aimed to provide a 
high-level visualization for the entire industry 
but would still provide detailed information for 
each activity based on the inputs. Moreover, one 
of the activities within the roadmap focused on 
==PAGE:116==
98
Descriptive Study II
the implementation stage of the NPD process. 
However, only considerations of carrying out 
this stage were included rather than actionable 
guidance on its implementation (d1). 
Product Innovation. The framework suppor­
ted both closing and slowing the resource loop 
design strategies (F1). The strategies were sug­
gested to the company based on their device’s 
criticality classification (F2). These categori­
es were ranked based on their environmental 
impact in order to guide a company to make 
an informed decision. Moreover, the strategies 
were put in an order following the Zero Waste 
Hierarchy (ZWIA, 2022), indicating a preference 
for one strategy over another within the design 
categories. However, the framework does not 
support companies to order circular strategies 
based on prioritization (f1). Based on iteration 
testing in PS and evaluation with the case com­
pany, it was discovered that the participants 
tended to already create  prioritization of their 
own when selecting strategies. During evalua­
tion, Coloplast’s representatives carefully chose 
up to three design strategies, indicating  that 
prioritization of only a few strategies would not 
have added value to the framework. 
Reverse Logistics. The framework suggested 
the necessity for the establishment of an RL sy­
stem to support the circular strategies (G1), and 
to assist them in this process, a tool was inclu­
ded in one of the activities to help them design 
the system for their business (g1).
Business Innovation. The activity concerning 
business model/case innovation, corresponding 
to the NPD business development stage, was 
out of the scope of this thesis. Nevertheless, the 
framework provided a general tool for devel­
oping a circular business model (Pieroni et al. 
2020) following the previously chosen design 
strategies (h1). The necessity for the tool was 
found during PS due to a need for a suppor­
ting business model that considered the new 
circular aspects.
Usability. The set requirements concerning 
usability (J) were all tested during the evalua­
tion with the case company, and it was found 
that both the must-haves and nice-to-haves 
requirements were fulfilled. The framework 
provided extensive explanations (J1) within 
all steps of the support and activities within 
the roadmap, making it easy for the company 
to utilize and benefit from the framework as 
intended. The framework was implemented 
in an Excel Workbook (J2) as Excel is a com­
monly used tool by companies, which ensured 
straightforward navigation within the sheet 
between steps and activities (j2).
Summary. Upon reviewing the requirements 
of the support framework, it can be seen 
that the majority of them were fulfilled. All 
must-haves have been fulfilled, meaning the 
framework has been developed successfully. 
Nevertheless, some of the nice-to-have requi­
rements remained uncertain. 
Uncertainty. The roadmap featured an assess­
ment tool to ensure that the newly developed 
product achieved a higher level of circularity 
than the linear device. By offering companies 
a means to evaluate the circularity performan­
ce of their solutions qualitatively, they would 
be able to pinpoint areas for improvement and 
ultimately refine their circular approach (i1). 
However, it was left unclear if the tool would 
assist the company in its intended purpose as 
it was not tested due to the timeframe of the 
thesis and the time it would take the case com­
pany to carry out and implement the activiti­
es. Therefore, it was not possible to determine 
if the tool would be useful in the assessment, 
thus leaving it uncertain. Moreover, as menti­
oned previously the testing was conducted on 
one department’s representative at a time and 
the filing out of the support took less than an 
hour (j1). However, it was unclear if the sup­
port's completion would take longer if comple­
ted by a team, as potential discussions within 
a team could arise regarding the framework's 
content, therefore extending the time beyond 
one hour. Thereby also rendering the fulfill­
ment of the requirement less certain.
Success of the Support Framework
Based on the previously mentioned require­
ments, the support framework fulfilled all the 
must-have requirements. However, the re­
search question of DS-II and requirement A1 
should also be considered in relation to the 
success of the framework. The requirement goal 
==PAGE:117==
99
Descriptive Study II
(A1) concerned whether the framework was 
able to facilitate the circular transition for the 
Danish MedTech industry using circular product 
innovation, which was closely correlated to RQ2, 
which focused on the usefulness of the support 
framework in the transition towards the circular 
economy. 
Based on findings from RC and DS-I, the neces­
sary factors for a successful transition towards 
the circular economy were found and taken into 
account throughout the development of the 
support framework. The research suggested that 
if a collaborative stakeholder network, circular 
business model, and circular product design 
were considered, companies would have a high 
chance of transitioning to the circular economy 
and navigating through various barriers suc­
cessfully (Marquet and Vetters, 2023). Since the 
framework includes the mentioned factors, it 
could be argued that it facilitates the circular 
transition for Danish MedTech companies. Mo­
reover, during the evaluation with Coloplast, the 
representatives indicated a strong interest in 
and perceived value of the framework's poten­
tial in their circular economy journey (Appendix 
O). They felt that the framework incorporated 
all the aspects essential for a circular medical 
device redesign. 
During the evaluation, the case company voi­
ced the great relevance and usefulness of the 
framework to the industry as it could act as a st­
arting point for many Danish MedTech compa­
nies. Providing a detailed explanation for each 
step and activity within the framework, pro­
vided value to the company in understanding 
how and where to start their transition. This 
meant that the support framework was found 
by the case company to be actionable. An issue 
identified through the RC phase to be one of 
the main gaps and barriers for companies tran­
sitioning toward the circular economy. More­
over, since the framework focused solely on the 
MedTech industry, it allowed for more tailored 
and detailed guidance for companies. Since the 
framework considered aspects that pertained 
to the MedTech industry such as the criticality 
of a device, regulatory background, and waste 
handling, it made it more tangible for MedTech 
companies to apply to their context and in their 
transition to the circular economy. 
Nevertheless, the actual usefulness of the fra­
mework throughout the transition to the circular 
economy remained undetermined, as the eva­
luation with the case company did not include 
the actual carrying out and the implementation 
of the roadmap’s activities due to the long pro­
duct development process. 
Even though the case company’s representati­
ves showed interest in the framework, their fe­
edback was only suggestive. The lack of group 
testing of the framework might have affected 
the received feedback,  potentially leading to 
an incomplete understanding of the framework 
due varied expertise across different areas. The 
activities were intended to be carried out by the 
different departments over the timeframe of a 
product development process, which was not 
possible to be carried out during the evaluati­
on. Thus, it is uncertain if the activities within 
the roadmap were, in fact, all-encompassing 
and detailed enough for a MedTech company 
to carry out the NPD process. It should also be 
mentioned that circular product design devel­
opment is a complex process that might differ 
from case to case. Moreover, it is undetermined 
whether the recommendations and guidelines 
provided within the framework would be fully 
utilized by individual companies to their full 
extent, as medical devices differ from one ano­
ther. Since the framework tries to focus on SUDs 
specifically, no matter the criticality or type of a 
device, the framework might be harder to adapt 
to individual companies due to the broad range 
of medical devices.
It remains uncertain whether the framework 
can effectively support a shift to circularity. This 
uncertainty arises from the absence of testing 
of the roadmap, even though the potential was 
recognized. Furthermore, the lengthy timeframe 
and the stringent regulations in the MedTech in­
dustry mean that obtaining regulatory approval 
Box 4.3
Requirement A1
Must facilitate the circular transition 
for the Danish MedTech industry with a 
focus on circular product innovation. 
==PAGE:118==
100
Descriptive Study II
can be a time-consuming process, taking sever­
al years (Stepniewska, 2024). Therefore, in order 
to assess the practical impacts of the roadmap 
once fully implemented, future work should in­
clude an iterative testing process focused on 
the product development process. Moreover, 
to ensure the success of the framework overall, 
limitations along with suggestions for improve­
ment should be taken into account, which will 
be discussed further in the following. 
Limitations of the Support Fra­
mework
Through the evaluation and the overall pro­
cess of developing the framework, aspects 
concerning the potential limitations of the fra­
mework itself emerged. In the following sec­
tions the main aspects concerning this topic 
will be addressed.
The choice behind the scope of the framework 
was established based on findings in the RC 
phase regarding the existing tools guiding the 
circular transitions. Literature focused on either 
one factor in detail or took a high-level appro­
ach focusing on their importance and correlati­
on (Mendoza et al., 2017), making the transition, 
in terms of inclusion of all the factors and acti­
onability overall, difficult to take on. This thesis 
and the developed framework aimed to tackle 
all the main factors necessary for the circular 
transition, provide a comprehensive, holistic 
view of the transition for MedTech, and provi­
de the companies with actionable activities to 
carry out independently. However, as the sup­
port framework covered all the essential fac­
tors, there was a risk of neglecting an adequate 
amount of detail on how to carry them out for 
the individual factors. This potentially results in 
the same issues as the existing tools in terms of 
their lack of detail and inability to be actually 
implemented. However, the support framework 
did go into some level of detail, as discovered 
through the evaluation with the case com­
pany, who stated that the framework’s detail­
ing and encompassing nature was impressive. 
Even though the support framework focused on 
multiple factors, it did cover adequate detailing 
within the factors. Nevertheless, it did not have 
the same level of detail as existing company- 
or product-specific tools that companies could 
employ and implement directly. Thus, the sup­
port framework represents a compromise bet­
ween highly detailed tools covering one specific 
factor and tools covering multiple factors regar­
ding their importance and correlations. 
This also ties in with the focus group for the 
framework, as it is meant to be a tool for the 
entire MedTech industry, not just one com­
pany, thereby widening the scope more than if 
it was just for one company. It can be argued 
that the level of detail for the aspects directly 
concerning circular product design, might not 
be possible to further elaborate upon, because 
of the industry-wide scope. As this would entail 
too specific considerations for how to practical­
ly redesign the devices toward circularity, that 
would not be relevant to the broader industry, 
thereby saying that the level of detail in this re­
gard had reached an adequate level of detail. 
However, there are still aspects in the existing 
framework that should be further improved 
upon for it to reach its full potential regarding 
how to establish and develop the RL system in 
practice and how to actionably go from a line­
ar business model/case to a circular approach, 
and what this would entail. Therefore, because 
of the already broad scope of the framework, it 
was chosen to limit the extent to which these 
aspects were detailed and only suggest tools on 
how to design them and what to consider.
Rebound effects. One significant limitation of 
the framework was that it did not address re­
bound effects. Rebound effects are defined by 
Pigosso (2023b) as “[...] unintended consequen­
ces driven by behavioral and systemic changes 
that undermine potential sustainability gains,” 
meaning that even when attempting to impro­
ve sustainability, unintended consequences can 
arise, resulting in the efforts being counterpro­
ductive. Therefore, it is crucial to consider the­
se potential rebound effects when developing 
circular economy transition strategies to miti­
gate such unintended outcomes. The omission 
of rebound effects in the framework was due to 
the fact that existing tools concerning mapping 
and identifying rebound effects are challen­
ging for companies to independently apply and 
might not cater to their particular industry. In 
PS, during one of the iteration tests, an existing 
tool (Metic and Pigosso, 2022) revolving around 
==PAGE:119==
101
Descriptive Study II
rebound effects was included in the testing. A 
participant attempting to use the tool found 
that it did not encapsulate all the relevant aspe­
cts of the MedTech industry. While offering va­
luable insights, the researched tools regarding 
rebound effects (Metic and Pigosso, 2022; Das 
et al., 2023) may not be ideally suited for Med­
Tech companies, as these tools were designed 
for broad applicability across various industri­
es. For example, the tool by Metic and Pigosso 
(2022) focuses heavily on the overconsumption 
of products, an issue that does not pertain to 
the MedTech industry, as medical devices are a 
necessity for the people using them and not a 
product to be overconsumed. However, it was 
deemed not crucial to develop or adapt current 
tools for this framework, due to the already bro­
ad scope of the support framework.
Sensitivity of the Support Fra­
mework
The framework's effectiveness also depends 
highly on the quality and completeness of in­
puts provided by a company, which could be 
affected by various factors. 
Overlooking recommendations. One factor 
that can affect the outputs of the support fra­
mework is not following the recommendations. 
The framework provides specific recommen­
dations designed to guide companies through 
the support’s steps - especially from steps three 
and four. If not followed, the companies might 
not address the aspects with the greatest po­
tential for improvement for their device or ob­
tain the greatest environmentally beneficial im­
pact in terms of the chosen design strategies, 
thereby missing the potential and opportunities 
covered by the framework. This was validated 
through both the iterations and the evaluati­
on with the case company, which showed that 
neither followed the recommendations as in­
tended. One possible explanation for this could 
be that implementing a single strategy can be 
more efficient and less time-consuming than 
implementing multiple strategies. However, a 
strategy like life extensions may require more 
extensive changes to the current system and 
the establishment of new systems compared to 
others. As a result, companies might opt to dis­
regard these recommendations due to the time 
and resources needed to execute and integrate 
them, which might tie in with current legislation 
regarding circularity in MedTech making these 
types of strategies less feasible.
Incomplete inputs. Filling out all the fields 
within the framework is critical for generating 
accurate and complete outputs within the ro­
admap. Missing input data in the support can 
lead to gaps within the transition roadmap, and 
this incomplete data can skew the results and 
even lead to inaccurate guidance in the product 
development process. 
Subjective reflections. Some of the steps in the 
support require subjective and qualitative an­
swers and reflections from the company. This 
can affect the results in the roadmap as those 
inputs heavily rely on the company’s perspecti­
ve. This means that the inputs rely on the com­
pany’s interpretations and views of the subject, 
thus potentially leading to skewed results that 
might not accurately represent the objective si­
tuation of the company. Thus, if the framework 
was more quantitative, it could have provided 
more consistent and objective data. Never­
theless, this could have missed capturing the 
company’s unique context or challenges. 
These elements should be considered when 
assessing the framework's accuracy and ap­
plicability. These sensitivities could possibly be 
incorporated into the support framework to in­
form users about potential misalignments and 
improve the framework's utility. 
Suggestions for Improvements
Based on the feedback gathered through the 
evaluation with Coloplast, many suggestions on 
the framework improvements arose, which will 
be discussed in detail in the following section. 
Communication of strengths. One of the 
aspects brought up during the evaluation was 
the lack of transparency of the framework's 
main qualities. As mentioned earlier, the re­
presentatives from the case company voiced 
the need for the framework to underline what 
it can help their company with. By highlighting 
the main strengths of the framework, it could 
help companies understand its unique bene­
==PAGE:120==
102
Descriptive Study II
fits. Communicating where the framework can 
help a company could create trust and confi­
dence when using it. Knowing where the com­
pany should concentrate its focus and efforts 
could ensure that it maximizes the impact of 
its initiatives. Therefore, one of the future im­
provements for the support framework should 
focus on incorporating a clear description of 
the framework’s strengths.
Business innovation. Another important aspe­
ct brought up during the evaluation was the 
business model development stage. A few case 
company representatives saw the potential of 
including a tool for circular business model de­
velopment, but they stated that the differences 
between the circular business model and the li­
near economy model should be highlighted to 
ensure that all of the necessary and new aspects 
covered by the circular version were included 
and were made clear to the companies. Omitting 
the business model development aspect could 
potentially limit the framework’s effectiveness 
in guiding companies with comprehensive and 
actionable steps for their circular transition. 
Incorporating more detailed circular business 
model development processes could help Med­
Tech companies make an informed decision re­
garding their business model and understand 
the importance of adaptation to support the 
circular economy initiatives. Nevertheless, inte­
grating business model development into the 
already extensive scope of the thesis could have 
introduced additional complexity.
Order of activities. The evaluation brought 
up another aspect that relied on established 
processes in MedTech companies. During the 
evaluation and the PS iterations testing, par­
ticipants stated that the order of the activities 
within the roadmap could vary depending on 
the internal processes of the company. Even 
though the activities followed the NPD process 
and were left high-level in the visualized road­
map, the participants voiced that this still could 
depend greatly on the processes already in pla­
ce in the companies. Thus, by tailoring the road­
map toward the company's needs, they could 
feel like the roadmap’s activities and contents 
were more applicable and relevant to their in­
dividual company’s context and processes. This 
could be achieved by making the roadmap inte­
ractive and flexible so that a company could ad­
just the order based on their needs and internal 
processes, making it more familiar and tangible 
to the company themselves. 
Usability. During the evaluation, the usability 
of the support and roadmap were rated high. 
However, as observed during the individual 
tests with the case company, the functionality 
and clarity of the steps and activities could be 
improved. The usability aspect can always be 
improved upon, which, in this case, was related 
to the coding, layout, and technical aspects of 
the Excel Workbook as a whole, which origina­
ted from the lack of experience of the devel­
opers in the format, and if these were improved, 
the framework would function more smoothly. 
Moreover, during the evaluation, a suggestion 
was made regarding the ability to have a prin­
table version of the roadmap. This would bene­
fit the companies as a printable version would 
be more accessible, meaning the users could 
communicate and share activities within me­
etings or workshops. Having a physical copy gi­
ves a tangible reference that could be used for 
discussions and distributed during meetings to 
ensure alignment between a team. 
In summary, all the above considerations should 
be incorporated into the support framework to 
reach its full potential. 
==PAGE:121==
The goal of Descriptive Study II was to evalu­
ate the overall success of the developed sup­
port framework in terms of its usability and 
relevance as well as to address RQ2 in terms 
of how useful it was and what could be done 
to ensure its success. Through the evaluation 
performed with three case company represen­
tatives, the usefulness and relevance for the 
industry were confirmed - however, in theory, 
as it was not possible to test the actual useful­
ness of the roadmap. 
The case company believed that the support 
framework was a great first step toward the 
circular transition and could assist MedTech 
companies in gaining an overview of how to 
target all the factors and activities they need 
to complete to ensure its success and viability. 
The support framework was deemed a power­
ful tool, and based on the feedback provided, 
they deemed that it fulfilled RQ2 of assisting 
the MedTech industry in the circular economy 
transition. Also, regarding overall usability, the 
support framework was rated highly as the 
representatives could complete the support 
without larger issues and had no issues navi­
gating it. However, the evaluation also iden­
tified some side effects from the framework. 
Even though the evaluation was a success, the 
case company representatives all thought that 
the comprehensive nature of the framework 
was also its downfall - it was trying to do too 
much, which made the framework overwhel­
ming. Even though they understood why the 
framework touched upon so many aspects, 
they thought it should have been made clea­
rer where its main focus was and what aspe­
cts only supported, so the focus remained on 
circular product design and product devel­
opment. Through the phase, limitations and 
improvements were identified and discussed, 
and it was found that if all these aspects were 
addressed, the framework would be further 
strengthened and its viability further ensured. 
Especially the compromise between focusing 
on one factor in detail or multiple factors more 
high-level was found to be the main limitation 
of the developed framework, as it attempted 
to fill all the gaps identified in the Research 
Clarification step. However, it could be argued 
that it instead ended up lacking sufficient de­
tail on how to actually implement and execute 
Conclusion

the framework on a more product- and com­
pany-specific level. The chosen approach was 
the compromise, as it was designed to be in­
dustry-specific rather than both company- and 
product-specific as to cater to a larger crowd,
Overall, the case company saw the potential 
of the framework and its actionable roadmap 
for the industry and believed it would be able 
to support the circular transition for Danish 
MedTech companies, at least based on the 
evaluation and subsequent reflection. Thus, as 
it was right now, it was deemed a success for 
its purpose.

process. Moreover, it ponders on how the support framework could 
be further developed in terms of future work. Lastly, an evaluation of 
the learning objectives was conducted as well as an evaluation of the 
fulfillment of the thesis goal to ensure that the thesis fulfilled its over­
