==PAGE:124==
and restructuring of the value chain, it can help 
MedTech companies gain bargaining power 
and thus promote the adoption of circular stra­
tegies in the industry (Eisenreich et al., 2022). 
Existing processes. MedTech companies have 
their pre-established ways of designing and de­
veloping their devices, but a circular transition 
would challenge those practices, and imple­
menting circular strategies involves significant 
changes in organizational processes. Integra­
ting new circular strategies into existing pro­
cesses and systems are complex, requiring sta­
keholder engagement (Gupta et al., 2019) and 
employee compliance (Eisenreich et al., 2022). 
While the organizational processes cannot be 
addressed within the framework due to specifi­
city of organizational change management, the 
framework underlines its importance and com­
plexity. It encourages companies to reevaluate 
their existing processes by innovative product 
design and business models. 
Financial considerations. Effective implemen­
tation of the framework would require time and 
resource allocation for MedTech companies. 
Allocating time for the framework completion 
and implementation could help ensure the suc­
cessful execution of each of the activities of the 
roadmap. Nevertheless, that would mean com­
panies would need to allocate funds for the en­
deavor and allocate specialized teams to carry 
it out. High investment costs associated with 
the project would strain the company's finan­
cial resources (Appendix Con. 1). Meaning that, 
financial resources could decide if the under­
taking would be carried out and considered by 
top management. This poses a limitation of the 
framework's applicability as it does not consi­
der the economic benefit of its implementation. 
Nevertheless, moving towards circularity would 
enable MedTech independence on raw materi­
als, minimize their waste costs and create new 
revenue streams. The financial landscape of the 
industry could shift when recognizing circulari­
ty not only as a sustainable choice but also as 
an advantage from a financial perspective (EMF, 
2015a; Mendoza et al., 2017). 
Implementability. Based on the findings from 
RC, it was found that companies need actio­
nable guidance with the step-by-step process 
106
Epilogue
The support framework acted as a starting point 
for MedTech companies on how to tackle their 
journey towards more circular ways. However, 
one of the challenges companies face when 
transitioning, is the actual implementation of 
circular economy strategies (Tura et al., 2019), 
as discovered in RC. Implementing circular 
strategies is challenging due to complexity of 
external and internal factors impacting the 
change (Tura et al., 2019). The following discus­
sion will delve into how the support framework 
aimed to assist Danish MedTech companies in 
addressing critical factors. While the framework 
covered many aspects, some areas remained 
beyond its scope.
Regulatory background. Navigating through 
the regulatory landscape creates complexity, 
as MedTech companies must ensure patient 
safety compliance while adapting to a circular 
economy (Kane, Bakker and Balkenende, 2018). 
Even though the support framework suggests 
actions to enable this transition within the Da­
nish healthcare sector, the change needs to 
be initiated by a larger force (Kirchherr et al., 
2018; Appendix Con. 1). Thus, by including the 
regulatory requirements that might influence 
over the product development process, the 
framework helps to ensure that the circular 
strategies are aligned with healthcare stan­
dards. While the framework can highlight are­
as where regulations should be considered, it 
cannot provide specific guidance on mitigating 
or transforming these regulations. Therefore, 
the framework underlines to companies when 
current legislation should be considered in the 
product development process, and when they 
should engage with policymakers on potential­
ly changing regulations.
Stakeholder network. For a successful tran­
sition to the circular economy, MedTech com­
panies need to move towards a stakeholder 
network (Ishaq et al., 2024). The support fra­
mework encourages collaboration and com­
munication between various stakeholders like 
policymakers, HCPs, patients etc. However, it 
cannot address the logistical challenges a com­
pany might face when aligning their circular 
principles with stakeholders, but it can offer 
guidance on who to include, when, and how. 
By establishing communication, partnerships 
Discussion
==PAGE:125==
107
Epilogue
of how to transition towards a circular eco­
nomy. Thus, by incorporating the NPD process 
in a visual representation of a roadmap, the gap 
of actionable steps was fulfilled. By providing a 
high-level overview of the circular transition, 
it can empower companies in their transition 
to more circular ways. Nevertheless, through 
the evaluations on the case company in DS-
II, it was found that what companies actually 
need is implementable steps for their product 
development process. They need guidance tar­
geted and tailored towards them, that they can 
directly apply in their context. The goal of this 
thesis was to create a more holistic and wide­
ly applicable framework for the Danish Med­
Tech industry rather than a company-specific 
process. This thesis contributes to the industry 
by offering a flexible support framework that 
MedTech companies can adapt based on their 
specific requirements. As found in RC, making 
a more company-specific solution would de­
crease the applicability of the framework to 
other companies, thus it bridges the gaps bet­
ween the broad strategic goals with details for 
each activity in the NPD process. 
Summary. The developed support framework 
aligns strategies with circular product design, 
business model, collaborative stakeholder net­
work, and delves into industry-wide challenges 
like financial and regulatory background. Whi­
le acknowledging its limitations and detailing, 
the framework bridges a gap between strategic 
goals and makes them into actionable steps 
for MedTech companies by its holistic nature.
Impact of the Support Fra­
mework
If a MedTech company ensures an effective 
utilization of the framework and its roadmap, 
it can significantly impact the engineering sy­
stem levels (De Weck, Roos, and Magee, 2011). 
System thinking is an approach to tackle com­
plex problems holistically (De Weck, Roos, and 
Magee, 2011), like circular economy transition 
by delving into three engineering levels. Since 
the developed framework is made to impact 
one of the levels of the enginning systems, it 
is crucial to also think how such change to one 
level affects the others. Thus, this section del­
ves into the various levels of the engineering 
system in order to emphasize the impact a 
MedTech company circular shift could have on 
strategic changes within the company, for the 
industry and its surrounding environment.
Artifact level. The transition of a MedTech 
company toward circularity influences the de­
sign, production and lifecycle management of 
the medical device. By utilizing the support 
framework, MedTech companies can integrate 
long-life, life extension, and EoL practices. They 
would need to adopt LCT where they would 
take responsibility for their devices throughout 
their entire lifecycle and make considerations 
for EoL in the early development stages.  They 
would need to also consider various strategi­
es and implement it into the device's design 
in order to undergo value recovery processes. 
From various perspectives, the transition advo­
cates for companies to prioritize design for du­
rability, not only to enable strategies involving 
reprocessing but also to decrease the necessity 
for replacements and reducing material need 
(Braungart, McDonough, and Bollinger, 2007). 
Moreover, it advocates for modular design 
which enables for repair, maintenance and 
upgrades (Eisenreich et al., 2022). But it would 
also help to integrate EoU/EoL considerations 
like recycling, remanufacturing or reuse (Eisen­
reich et al., 2022). The LCT approach ensures 
that sustainability is embedded throughout 
the entire lifecycle of a medical device, from 
initial design to final disposal.
Complex System level. At a complex level, 
the framework impacts a broader scope of the 
MedTech company, including its business mo­
del, operational processes, and its value chain. 
The framework emphasizes the need to transi­
tion from a linear to a circular business model, 
which, combined with circular product design, 
can facilitate the creation of durable products 
with value recovery possibilities and new reve­
nue streams. This transition, however, depends 
on a well-structured supply chain. By utilizing 
the framework, a company must reflect on its 
current value chain and transform it into a col­
laborative value network. This transformation 
entails fostering knowledge-sharing opportu­
nities, close collaboration and larger say with 
partners and suppliers to align sustainability 
goals and increased trust (Evans et al., 2017). 
==PAGE:126==
108
Epilogue
Moreover, establishing new partnerships and 
extending the value chain sets requirements to 
systems like RL, which are essential for recove­
ring the value of a device at the EoL (Tukker, 
2004). Additionally, decreasing the amount 
of existing suppliers can increase bargaining 
power of a MedTech company enabling them 
to create a bigger change in sustainability and 
circularity (Eisenreich et al., 2022). 
Socio-technical Engineering System level. 
Looking into the engineering system level, the 
implementation of the support framework in a 
company could promote large changes within 
the industry and encourage other companies 
to adopt similar practices. Raising awareness 
about the viability of the circular transition in 
the industry could help facilitate other stake­
holders in understanding and adapting to the 
circular economy (Kirchherr et al., 2018), resul­
ting in increased implementation of circular 
strategies not solely within the industry. Mo­
reover, the framework reflects upon the tran­
sition through regulatory changes as that has 
been found to be a major barrier for MedTech 
companies. By encouraging collaboration with 
policymakers and other external stakeholders, 
the circular practices within the industry could 
be eventually standardized, making it easier to 
adopt and integrate into common practice (Ei­
senreich et al., 2022). Transitioning towards a 
circular economy would effectively tackle the 
primary challenge faced by the healthcare se­
ctor, being waste generation, by implementa­
tion of value recovery processes (Kane, Bakker 
and Balkenende, 2018). Consequently, Med­
Tech companies could contribute to a more 
circular and sustainable future. The MedTech 
sector could be more towards environmentally 
friendly sectors, as of right now they are one of 
the most polluting sectors (Schneider, 2023). 
The circular transition could help them move 
away from SUDs towards more durable and 
multiple-use devices, and thus minimize the 
amount of waste generated by the SUDs. 
Discussion of the Thesis Goal
Reflecting on the overarching goal of this thesis, 
it should be discussed whether or not the goal 
was met. The goal of the thesis was as follows:
Develop a support framework that will faci­
litate the development of a circular economy 
roadmap for the Danish MedTech industry 
with the goal of reducing the amount of wa­
ste produced by SUDs through circular product 
innovation.
The primary goal of the thesis was to develop 
a support framework to facilitate creation of a 
circular economy roadmap for the Danish Med­
Tech industry. As seen throughout the phases, 
the framework and roadmap was developed 
successfully. Based on findings in RC and DS-I, it 
incorporated all the crucial factors for a succes­
sful transition to a circular economy. However, 
looking into the second part of the thesis goal, 
it focuses on the waste reduction that would be 
an effect of implementing the framework. This 
goal of waste reduction still remains uncertain in 
terms of its success. Due to the long-term pro­
cess of implementing the roadmap’s activities, 
it was not possible to test within the timeframe 
of this thesis. Therefore, the impact of waste re­
duction by SUDs cannot be determined. More­
over, since the implementation of circular pro­
duct design was not tested upon, the practical 
effects of the framework also remain uncertain. 
Without conducting tests on the actual imple­
mentation with a MedTech company, it cannot 
be determined whether the support framework 
effectively facilitates circular product design in 
the Danish MedTech industry. 
In conclusion, although all essential factors were 
included in the developed support framework, 
its effects and success for a circular economy 
transition cannot be determined, along with 
the goal of waste reduction which remains 
unknown as well due to the lack of implemen­
tation testing. 
==PAGE:127==
109
Epilogue
Through the development, testing, and evalu­
ation of the support framework, a hypothesis 
was developed based on the feedback given 
and the very nature of the framework itself. The 
framework does not directly specify that it is 
made for SUDs but instead states that it is made 
for the redesign of a current medical device to 
make it more circular. Based on the feedback 
given by MedTech professionals in regard to 
the potential they saw for the framework to be 
used for the broader MedTech industry, there is 
potential to further develop the framework to 
capture a broader audience. With this in mind, 
the following hypothesis was developed:
H0: Danish MedTech companies can use the 
support framework to help them improve their 
medical devices' circularity potential.
This hypothesis focuses on the improvement 
aspect, thereby inherently saying that the sup­
port framework can assist companies that al­
ready employ some circular strategies in their 
devices’ design, and through the framework, 
they would be able to improve their circulari­
ty potential further. Initially, it was intended to 
test this hypothesis within the timeframe of this 
thesis, and therefore, company representatives 
working at MedTech companies specializing in 
reusable medical devices were contacted th­
rough email. The companies were not intended 
to go through the entire framework meticu­
lously, but instead, they would need to reflect 
on whether they could see the framework as 
being useful and relevant for their company 
and their devices in improving their circularity. 
However, it was not possible to get in contact 
with any potential test subjects within the ti­
meframe of this thesis.
Nevertheless, some adaptations to the current 
framework would be necessary to even make 
it possible to cater to the broader industry. It 
would need to consider which strategies the 
companies already have in place and whether 
the companies already have RL and value re­
covery companies in place, meaning it would 
need to establish a baseline before attempting 
to suggest how to improve the device’s design. 
In this regard, it would be beneficial if the fra­
mework could suggest different strategies in 
varying degrees of intervention with the cur­
Future work 
rent system. This means it could suggest de­
sign strategies that the current design could 
easily adapt to without a radical redesign but 
also suggest strategies with the greatest eco­
nomic and environmental benefit, even though 
this would entail a larger redesign process that 
might be similar to that of an NPD process like 
for the SUDs. Even with the framework as it is 
currently, companies specializing in reusable 
devices would still be able to utilize and benefit 
from the roadmap's activities and considerati­
ons, but they would be required to determine 
their relevance to the circular strategies already 
in place.
Circular product design from the onset. 
Moving beyond the redesign aspect that the 
support framework was designed for, another 
consideration made during the development 
process and evaluation was whether it could be 
used for designing a new device from scratch. 
One of the test subjects during the iterations 
made this comment, as they saw the poten­
tial of using this framework as the backbone 
when designing a new product with circularity 
in mind from the onset. They saw the potential, 
especially for smaller, less-established compa­
nies, that have the ability to be more flexible 
and adapt toward circularity more quickly to 
benefit significantly from the framework, as it 
outlines everything they need to do and how 
to do it. Because of their size, these companies 
would see the circular transition as the perfect 
opportunity to be first on the market and bene­
fit from the business opportunity that the fra­
mework can assist them in reaching. However, 
evaluating this would not be possible for the 
same reasons as the usefulness of the roadmap 
is not quantifiable, being the time it takes to 
develop and launch a medical device (3-7 years 
to approval). 
Following the evaluation and iterations, the 
majority of participants recognized the fra­
mework's potential and applicability across the 
broader MedTech industry. Therefore, expan­
ding its utilization across the industry will be a 
top priority in future endeavors. This will help 
generate interest and disseminate knowledge 
about the circular transition within the Danish 
MedTech industry, where awareness is current­
ly limited.
==PAGE:128==
110
Epilogue
Circularity beyond borders. The current focus 
of this support framework is on the Danish 
MedTech industry. However, there is a need to 
consider the potential implications of expan­
ding this framework to other countries. Ac­
cording to the case company (Appendix Con. 
1) and academic research, the legislation con­
cerning medical devices varies from country to 
country, making it challenging to accommoda­
te multiple countries' industries simultaneously. 
Nevertheless, Danish MedTech companies tend 
to pilot new products in developed countries, 
particularly Scandinavian countries. So, sup­
pose this support framework was to be further 
developed to ensure the circular initiatives' 
feasibility in other countries and to enable the 
devices developed using the framework to be 
marketable and viable outside of Denmark. In 
that case, it is essential first to incorporate the 
legislation of the Scandinavian countries. Addi­
tionally, circular initiatives have a higher chance 
of success in developed countries, as evidenced 
by Pinheiro et al. (2019) and supported by the 
case company (Appendix A), which highlighted 
that developed countries are more focused on 
the environmental impact due to their financi­
al capabilities. Therefore, the logical next step 
beyond improving reusable devices and in­
troducing new circular products should be to 
expand to the Scandinavian countries.
==PAGE:129==
111
Epilogue
Looking back at the process of this thesis, 
some aspects could have been done different­
ly; therefore, the larger subjects will be reflec­
ted upon in the following paragraphs.
Collaboration with the case company. The 
first concern revolves around the collaboration 
with the case company. While the thesis has 
greatly benefited from the case company’s in­
sights and industry knowledge, accessing this 
knowledge has posed certain challenges. Due 
to the company's size, identifying the rele­
vant individuals and scheduling meetings has 
proven to be more challenging than initially 
anticipated, significantly impacting the proje­
ct. This included determining the appropriate 
recipients for the ready2LOOP assessment and 
planning its execution, which was originally 
intended to be conducted in person with the 
relevant departments. However, due to the 
unavailability of a suitable meeting time in­
volving multiple participants, the assessment 
was ultimately distributed via email. Upon 
receiving the assessment results, it became 
evident that some misinterpretations led to 
skewed scores in certain dimensions, which 
could have been avoided with expert facilita­
tion during the assessment. Another challenge 
arose in the form of delayed responses when 
seeking information from the company, as 
the sole communication channel was through 
email. When it came to evaluating the final 
support framework, emails were sent well in 
advance to the previously contacted individu­
als. Still, only two representatives responded 
and provided evaluations after multiple fol­
low-up attempts over three weeks. Despite the 
company supervisor's efforts to identify addi­
tional company representatives, the response 
rate remained a significant obstacle. 
The main lesson learned from this experience 
of working with a larger company is the im­
portance of initiating a project with a meeting 
involving various department heads from the 
company. This initial meeting should outline 
the project's purpose and expectations from 
both the company and the project itself to 
ensure alignment of expectations from the 
start. Although scheduling challenges may ari­
se, this step is crucial in fostering willingness 
and collaboration between the project team 
Reflection 
and the company. Additionally, it became evi­
dent that stakeholders should have been more 
consistently engaged throughout the project, 
receiving regular updates and being informed 
about when their input was needed. While 
some efforts were made in this regard, it was 
insufficient, particularly in the evaluation pro­
cess. Moreover, as the project's scope was sig­
nificantly redefined after the initial milestone 
with the DTU supervisors, stakeholder mana­
gement took a back seat to accommodate the 
necessary changes. Nonetheless, this experi­
ence emphasized the critical importance of 
stakeholder management and engagement in 
any project, highlighting the need for a com­
prehensive plan for stakeholder involvement 
from the project's outset to ensure alignment 
and meet expectations.
Evaluation. In retrospect, the evaluation pro­
cess with the case company could have been 
approached differently. Despite the challen­
ges in recruiting participants, there was also 
room for improvement in the evaluation pro­
tocol. Rather than assessing the framework 
as a whole, receiving feedback on individual 
activities could have been more beneficial. It 
was noted that valuable feedback may have 
been lost when evaluating the activities col­
lectively, as the participants seemed reluctant 
to examine the insights on the activities unless 
prompted directly, making it difficult to gauge 
their perceived relevance. However, the cho­
sen approach aimed to strike a balance bet­
ween resource utilization and effectiveness, 
considering that previous thorough tests had 
been time-consuming. Given the case com­
pany's limited availability to participate, op­
ting for overall feedback helped expedite the 
evaluation process. This decision, while effi­
cient, had implications for the comprehensive 
evaluation of the framework. A proactive ap­
proach to stakeholder management, planning, 
and alignment could have mitigated these 
challenges. Clear expectations and advanced 
preparation might have allowed for a more 
detailed evaluation, but this was not feasible 
at the time of the assessment.
Iteration testing. The third aspect pertained 
to the development process of the framework 
itself. As outlined in the PS, the absence of in­
==PAGE:130==
112
Epilogue
dustry experts posed a significant challenge 
in gathering essential feedback on the fra­
mework's utility and relevance. The project 
team realized belatedly that testing the PS on 
the case company was not viable, as it might 
prime (Valgeirsdottir and Onarheim, 2017) the 
participants' ability to impact the framework's 
development and subsequently evaluate it. 
This led to a time crunch in recruiting MedTech 
industry experts within the set timeframe for 
the PS phase, leaving the project with limi­
ted options for recruitment. During the initial 
round of tests with experts (Iteration 2), the 
project assessed the solution with the MedTech 
consultancy company Technolution. While va­
luable insights were provided, the participants 
found it difficult to place themselves in the 
shoes of the companies they consulted for to 
provide comprehensive feedback. This was due 
to the fact that the consultancy company they 
worked for did not produce actual devices, as 
they were solely a consultancy firm. Therefo­
re, it was not an ideal testing scenario, as they 
were not directly involved in the production of 
medical devices for MedTech companies.
During the second iteration with experts (Itera­
tion 3), the project encountered a shortage of 
experts in the field. However, the project was 
aware of individuals working at CAMES, a com­
pany that develops medical training equip­
ment used by surgeons and other HCPs for 
training purposes. This equipment can be clas­
sified as medical devices as it indirectly aids in 
the diagnosis of diseases. Although this was 
not an ideal scenario, the participants from 
CAMES possessed valuable knowledge related 
to MedTech and were able to offer construc­
tive feedback. This meant the first time the 
framework was tested by a MedTech company 
producing medical devices was with the case 
company itself. The potential issue of not te­
sting the framework with the intended type of 
MedTech companies may have led to missing 
out on valuable feedback specific to this con­
text. The participants from the two iterations 
did not have a complete overview of the vari­
ous aspects necessary to embark on an ende­
avor such as the circular economy transition, 
as they were specialized in a particular area or 
phase of the design process, resulting in a lack 
of a holistic overview. However, it was discove­
red that the case company had only minor 
suggestions for improving the framework ba­
sed on the evaluation and found it to be both 
useful and relevant for the industry. For future 
projects, it would be advantageous to identify 
testing participants early in the process to en­
sure their relevance and adequate knowledge 
within the project’s scope, thereby ensuring 
that the initial evaluation is not the final one.
Methodology. The final aspect pertains to the 
DRM itself. Prior to this thesis, the project team 
had not utilized this methodology, which posed 
some challenges. The unfamiliar structure and 
reporting style of this methodology required 
some time to become accustomed to, leading 
to the need to redefine the scope of this the­
sis as the original did not align with the DRM. 
Despite the challenges in adopting and imple­
menting the approach, it also offered benefits. 
It differed significantly from other methodolo­
gies previously used by the project team, pro­
viding a structured approach to reporting and 
being more academically substantiated than 
previous methods. In particular, the inclusion 
of the systematic literature review allowed for 
more thorough research and analysis, resulting 
in substantial findings. Reflecting on the pro­
cess, the main drawback of this methodology 
was the limited focus on the process itself, res­
embling a research article with an emphasis on 
results rather than the process behind them. 
This marked a significant departure from the 
main focus of previous projects in the study 
program. While the lack of emphasis on reaso­
ning and reflections during the iterations was 
a downside, the methodology's greatest bene­
fit lay in the detailed research for RC and DS-I, 
laying a strong foundation for developing the 
support framework for the PS phase.
==PAGE:131==
113
Epilogue
The thesis aimed to meet the defined learning 
objectives that were set at the beginning of the 
project, and therefore this section will outline 
how each objective was achieved.
1. Plan, organize, and carry out a thesis pro­
ject involving diverse stakeholders from the 
academic institution and the case company 
while meeting their expectations towards the 
deliverable(s).
This thesis was structured around the DRM 
methodology and its four phases, each with its 
own deliverables. These phases served as the 
overall framework for organizing the project, 
and the completion of each phase was marked 
by a milestone presentation for the DTU super­
visors to ensure that the project was progres­
sing as planned. Task and time management 
tools were utilized to monitor and execute the 
project, ensuring that it met the agreed-upon 
deadlines and requirements. Throughout the 
project, supervision and support were provi­
ded by both DTU supervisors and representa­
tives from the case company, with the goal of 
aligning the expectations of both organizati­
ons regarding the thesis deliverables.
2. Review and analyze relevant literature and 
data related to the MedTech industry and 
the circular economy concept to extract and 
synthesize the findings and understand the 
potential challenges of implementing the 
Circular Economy in this industry.
The literature review in RC encompassed in­
vestigations into the MedTech industry, the 
circular economy concept, and the challenges 
faced by the MedTech industry in transitioning 
to a circular model. The review revealed that 
the primary obstacle is the industry's empha­
sis on patient safety, which makes it difficult 
to prioritize sustainable and circular practices 
that could potentially compromise patient sa­
fety. However, it was also discovered in DS-I 
that several companies in the industry are al­
ready adopting circular practices, indicating 
the potential for the transition process. Ano­
ther challenge highlighted in the review was 
the lack of comprehensive literature on the 
transition process, particularly the absence of 
actionable tools outlining the necessary steps 
Fulfillment of the Learning Objectives
for companies. The review suggested that a 
roadmap could address this by incorporating 
essential transition factors and actionable acti­
vities to guide companies through implemen­
tation. This investigation assisted in the devel­
opment of the thesis goal.
3. Identify the key stakeholders across the 
value chain and the surrounding ecosystem 
for the MedTech industry and discuss their re­
lations and impact on transitioning toward a 
Circular Economy.
Based on academic research and empirical 
data gathered during a workshop with the case 
company, it was determined that the key sta­
keholders in the current MedTech landscape 
include value chain stakeholders from mate­
rial providers to product retailers, as well as 
ecosystem stakeholders such as users, HCPs, 
government financial networks, and policy­
makers. However, there is a significant absence 
of crucial actors related to value recovery and 
inputs from the remaining ecosystem. Further­
more, it was observed that communication 
and relationships within the landscape are 
predominantly one-sided, with MedTech com­
panies mainly informing their stakeholders 
without fostering beneficial two-way commu­
nication. This lack of effective communication 
hinders the adoption of a circular transition, as 
it is essential for MedTech companies to have 
the willingness and support of stakeholders to 
ensure the feasibility and viability of the transi­
tion. Academic research suggests that the suc­
cess of the circular transition depends on esta­
blishing a collaborative stakeholder network 
to facilitate collaboration, knowledge sharing, 
and co-development of new circular medical 
devices, as well as the involvement of new va­
lue network stakeholders to manage value re­
covery processes.
4. Devise a recommendation for stakeholder 
engagement within the MedTech industry for 
the transition toward a circular economy.
Research in DS-I found that MedTech compa­
nies should redirect their focus towards buil­
ding collaborative stakeholder networks. This 
shift involves implementing a more engaging 
communication strategy to support the tran­
==PAGE:132==
sition to a circular economy. The proposed 
plan emphasizes two-way communication and 
increased engagement with stakeholders th­
rough workshops, co-development sessions, 
and knowledge-sharing across the value chain 
and ecosystem. This approach aims to enhan­
ce current practices, facilitate mutual lear­
ning, and enable the development of optimal 
products by incorporating input and experti­
se from multiple stakeholders. The suggested 
engagement strategies, developed by Salviona 
and Almici (2020), along with the framework 
designed by AccountAbility (2015), offer tan­
gible and practical examples to guide compa­
nies in developing effective communication 
plans for the circular economy transition.
5. Measure the circularity readiness of the 
case company and discover the improvement 
opportunities with the greatest potential for 
the circular economy.
A circular readiness assessment was carried 
out on the case company using the readiness 
assessment tool, ready2LOOP, resulting in a 
readiness score of 33%. The assessment revea­
led both the strengths and weaknesses of the 
company. Upon comparison with other compa­
nies in the same industry, it was observed that 
these trends were prevalent across the entire 
industry, not just limited to the case company. 
The assessment highlighted that both the case 
company and the industry as a whole scored 
the lowest in the areas related to "Takeback & 
End-of-Life strategies" and "Product & Service 
Innovation". As a result of this evaluation, the­
se two aspects were identified as the key focus 
areas for this thesis, with the aim of helping 
MedTech to significantly enhance its potential 
for circularity. 
6. Investigate the relevant factors and circular 
strategies that can assist MedTech companies 
in transitioning to a circular economy priori­
tized through benchmarking and supported 
through academic literature.
According to the ready2LOOP assessment, the 
key factors to address were related to product 
design. As part of DS-I, an in-depth investiga­
tion into these factors, along with supporting 
elements such as the design of RL and circular 
114
Epilogue
business model design, was carried out. The 
research revealed that in product innovati­
on within the circular economy, the focus lies 
on the NPD process, as the level of changes 
needed to redesign a medical device is simil­
ar to that required for a new product. When 
companies undertake product redesign for 
circularity, they must take into account vario­
us aspects of circular product design, including 
eco-efficiency considerations such as design 
for product postponing, resisting and reversing 
obsolescence strategies, as well as strategies 
for long-life and life extension. Additionally, 
eco-effectiveness considerations related to 
closing the loop through design for recycling 
are also essential. All these considerations are 
vital for effective circular product redesign, as 
well as for the design and establishment of va­
lue recovery companies, RL, a circular business 
model, and a collaborative stakeholder net­
work – all of which are prerequisites for the 
success of the circular transition.
7. Employ the findings and factors from the 
literature to develop the support framework.
With the findings from the systematic literatu­
re review in RC and the research into the re­
levant factors in DS-I, all of the findings were 
synthesized into a number of logical support 
steps and roadmap activities in PS, that encap­
sulated the findings from the previous phases, 
thereby outlining all the necessary considera­
tions and actions, MedTech companies need 
to carry out in order to successfully transition 
toward the circular economy. 
8. Develop a support framework to facilita­
te the successful development of a roadmap 
to assist MedTech companies in transitioning 
toward the circular economy.
The support framework was developed th­
rough three iterations, during which the fra­
mework and its parts were tested, improved, 
and iterated upon using feedback from Med­
Tech professionals and people outside the in­
dustry. The support framework consisted of 
a support, where companies were meant to 
input their choices and considerations to re­
design their current medical device and a ro­
admap with actionable activities, where the 
==PAGE:133==
115
Epilogue
previous inputs are put into context in a num­
ber of logical steps. The roadmap’s activities 
were thereby developed based on the inputs, 
and followed the NPD process, to provide the 
MedTech companies with an overview of the 
steps they need to carry out, and in which or­
der in order to transition toward the circular 
economy. Using this framework the transition 
process will be facilitated for the MedTech in­
dustry, as the roadmap functions as an actio­
nable guide on how to redesign their devices 
for circularity and restructure their current sy­
stem to support the new initiatives.
9. Evaluate the usability and usefulness of the 
support framework by testing it with the case 
company.
The support framework was evaluated in DS-II 
by testing it with the case company, with an 
emphasis on usability, relevance, and useful­
ness. Even though the original evaluation plans 
were not possible, it was possible to test the 
final framework with three company represen­
tatives, from different departments across the 
organization, providing a broad evaluation of 
the framework. Overall the support framework 
was scored highly in terms of both usabili­
ty and usefulness in the evaluation sheet the 
participants were asked to fill out. Especially 
the roadmap - the most important part of the 
framework - scored an average of 4 out of 5 
in terms of relevance to the industry and its 
applicability. They all saw the potential of the 
framework and could see it being valuable for 
the industry in the transition journey due to 
its actionable activities, its visualized roadmap 
emphasizing the need for concurrency, and 
the amount of information and considerations 
it considered. However, they also saw the need 
for further improvements before it could be ra­
ted a 5 out of 5.
10. Conclude, discuss, and reflect on the sup­
port framework, its potential drawbacks, bar­
riers, and limitations, and its application.
The support framework successfully achieved 
its goal - at least in theory, as indicated by the 
evaluation. However, due to the lengthy ap­
proval process of up to seven years for Med­
tech companies, the practical testing of the ro­
admap was not feasible. The feedback from the 
case company confirmed the potential of the 
framework to assist the industry in adopting 
more circular practices and ultimately reducing 
the production of waste. Nevertheless, barriers 
to the transition persist, including current legi­
slation, the lack of value recovery companies 
to manage medical devices after EoU and EoL, 
and the need for MedTech companies to be in­
centivized financially through tenders in order 
to drive the transition.
Furthermore, the main drawback of the fra­
mework is its extensive scope, which may lead 
to a lack of in-depth coverage of the included 
topics as it aims to encompass everything. 
However, this is also the distinguishing feature 
that sets it apart from existing tools in the li­
terature. While other tools may only highlight 
the importance of certain factors or focus on 
one aspect while neglecting its connections 
to others, this framework encompasses all es­
sential factors, offers practical guidance, and 
enables independent completion for MedTech 
companies interested in transitioning to a 
circular model. 
Currently, the framework is tailored for trans­
forming SUDs into reusable medical devices. 
However, with further refinement, it has the 
potential to be adapted for medical devices 
that already incorporate circular strategies, 
thereby focusing on enhancement. Additio­
nally, through further iterations, it could also 
be customized to support the development of 
circular devices from the outset, underscoring 
the broad applicability of the framework within 
the industry.
==PAGE:134==
116
Epilogue
This thesis aimed to address the following 
overarching goal:
Develop a support framework that will faci­
litate the development of a circular economy 
roadmap for the Danish MedTech industry 
with the goal of reducing the amount of wa­
ste produced by SUDs through circular pro­
duct innovation.
This was done through the utilization of the 
Design Research Methodology and its four 
phases and deliverables. In the Research Cla­
rification phase, the context and the barriers 
for the Danish MedTech industry in the circular 
transition process were investigated, and it was 
found that the regulatory framework was the 
greatest barrier as patient safety is the most 
important factor within the industry. However, 
through a systematic literature review it was 
found that another large barrier was the lack 
of actionable tools in how to assist MedTech 
in the transition process, but that a roadmap 
could function as an enabler for this process. 
Which led to the above thesis goal. 
In Descriptive Study I, the focus shifted. Th­
rough the ready2LOOP assessment, it was 
found that the areas in which the MedTech in­
dustry was facing the greatest challenges had 
to do with circular product innovation, which, 
therefore, became the focus of the thesis. In 
Descriptive Study I, the factors relating to this 
topic were investigated, and what companies 
need to include in terms of necessary conside­
rations and supporting systems. Through this 
it was found that there was a need for circular 
product design, which employs both eco-effi­
ciency and eco-effectiveness principles, inclu­
ding postponing, resisting, and reversing obso­
lescence strategies - all with the overarching 
aim of slowing and closing the loop to reduce 
resource consumption and waste generati­
on, which had been found through Research 
Clarification to be a great problem within the 
MedTech industry concerning SUDs. Moreover, 
it was found that supporting systems were also 
required for the success of the circular tran­
sition, which included circular business model 
development, a reverse logistics system, and a 
collaborative stakeholder network to support 
the circular initiatives. The collaborative sta­
Conclusion
keholder network should include both value 
network actors, including new value recovery 
companies, as well as ecosystem stakehol­
ders, including the financial network and po­
licymakers. This was crucial to ensure the wil­
lingness, support, and feasibility of the circular 
initiatives and help drive the circular transition 
across the industry. The reverse logistics sy­
stem was also necessary to ensure the viabili­
ty of the new circular business model and the 
feasibility and viability of the new circular stra­
tegies relating to value recovery, either focu­
sing on life-extension or recycling processes. 
In the Prescriptive Study phase, all these iden­
tified factors and considerations were synthe­
sized into one comprehensive framework con­
sisting of a support and an actionable roadmap 
following the NPD process. This framework was 
meant to assist MedTech companies in get­
ting an overview of the necessary processes 
for the circular transition and to ensure that 
all the necessary actions and considerations 
are implemented concerning the identified 
factors. The framework took a starting point in 
an existing medical device, and by using it, it 
would assist the company in redesigning the 
device and surrounding system for circularity. 
This means that the amount of waste produ­
ced would be reduced as the device’s lifecycle 
would be longer, it would have multiple use 
cycles, and/or be a part of a closed loop cycle. 
The roadmap was developed using a MedTech 
company’s inputs in the support, and then, ba­
sed on these, the roadmap’s activities were de­
veloped, and thereby, a customized roadmap 
for that particular company was developed 
based on a particular medical device chosen 
by the company. 
Lastly, in Descriptive Study II, the developed 
framework was evaluated with the case com­
pany in terms of its usability and usefulness 
for both the company itself and the broader 
context of the MedTech industry. Overall, po­
sitive feedback was received, confirming the 
framework's relevance and usefulness, as the 
case company saw the potential for the fra­
mework and its ability to function as the first 
best step toward the circular transition. It out­
lined all the necessary steps, factors, and con­
siderations MedTech companies need to take, 
==PAGE:135==
117
Epilogue
as well as in which order they should be tar­
geted. Therefore, based on the evaluation and 
supported by literature, it can be said that the 
framework would facilitate the circular transi­
tion for the Danish MedTech industry. 
The framework's focus on redesigning SUDs 
suggests that if MedTech companies utilize it, 
they will be assisted in developing reusable 
medical devices, ultimately leading to a reduc­
tion in the amount of waste through circular 
initiatives. Although the framework, especially 
the roadmap, was not fully carried out due to 
time constraints, rendering its actual useful­
ness uncertain, the case company's reflections 
indicate that it holds promise and relevance 
for the industry. Therefore, it can be said with 
some certainty that the framework can be 
utilized by the Danish MedTech industry and 
support them in their transition towards more 
circular practices through circular product in­
novation and its supporting systems, thereby 
contributing to the overarching thesis goal of 
reducing waste in the industry.
